especially significant during stages like Disqualification. When a lead does
not align with product offerings for technical, budgetary, or strategic
reasons, transparent communication can ensure a positive experience.
Organisations can foster trust by being straightforward about mismatches
and possibly suggesting alternative solutions, which could pave the way for
future engagement. (Care 2020)

In SaaS, Sales and PreSales teams are two sides of the same coin. While the
sales team provides the initial pitch and navigates the client’s business
landscape, the PreSales team dives deeply into the technicalities, ensuring
alignment at every stage. Be it during the Product Demonstration stage,
where the products’ technical nuances are showcased, or the proposal and
value proposition stage, where feasibility is assessed, seamless
collaboration between these two teams is pivotal.

Therefore, feedback loops are invaluable. After each significant interaction,
especially during the win/loss analysis stage, gathering feedback is crucial.
Understanding the dynamics that led to successful conversion or
identifying the factors that resulted in a lost opportunity can offer
invaluable insights. These feedback loops refine sales strategy and offer
potential product enhancement ideas.

Closing a deal is just the beginning. Post-sale stages such as Onboarding and
Expansion/Upselling are pivotal in ensuring client satisfaction and
retention. Ensuring a smooth onboarding process, regular check-ins, and
updates can fortify client relationships. It is essential to view clients not just
as revenue streams but as long-term partners, ensuring that the maximum
value is derived from the SaaS solution.

Just as in team sports, where a single player’s prowess does not determine
success but rather the collective effort and harmony of the entire team, in
the SaaS landscape, multiple departments must function cohesively to
achieve a unified goal. The seamless collaboration of Sales, PreSales,
Professional Service, Client Success, Product Development, and Product
Management teams is pivotal in ensuring consistent value delivery.

In team sports, whether it is basketball, soccer, or any other game, every
player has a distinct role, but they are all working towards a single
objective: victory. Similarly, in a SaaS company, while Sales may be at the
frontline acquiring clients, and Product Development focuses on creating
the best solutions, the synergy of all these departments culminates in
success. Each team brings unique strengths, perspectives, and expertise to
the table. Just as a forward in soccer relies on the midfielder for that perfect
pass, Sales rely on Product Management to understand and communicate
the features and benefits that resonate with potential clients.

The ultimate goal for this cohesive operation is dual-fold: delivering
unparalleled value to clients, ensuring their satisfaction and loyalty, and
driving growth and profitability for the company.


**Key Takeaway:**

Adopt a Consultative Approach: Instead of just pushing for a sale,
understand your potential client’s needs and tailor your pitch accordingly.
Offer solutions that truly address their pain points. Do not be shy to
disqualify.

Regular training and upgrading: Given the rapidly changing SaaS
landscape, ensure that both Sales and PreSales teams undergo regular
training in product updates, industry trends, and sales techniques.

Leverage CRM and sales automation tools: Utilise CRM systems to track
and manage leads. Automation tools can help streamline repetitive tasks,
ensuring that no lead falls through the cracks.

Tailored product demonstrations: Customise your product demos based
on the specific needs of the potential client. One-size-fits-all demos are
often flat. For Standard demos just use Demo Automation tools.

Transparent Communication: Especially when disqualifying a lead, be
transparent about the reasons. This can foster trust and open the door for
future engagement.

Collaboration between Sales and PreSales Teams: Foster strong
communication and collaboration between these teams. Regular debriefs
can provide feedback loops to refine and improve selling strategies.

Feedback and Continuous Improvement: After each stage, especially after
a win or lose, gather feedback. Understand what worked and what did not
and refine your approach accordingly.

Long-term Relationship Building: Beyond the initial sale, focus is placed on
building a long-term relationship with the client. This includes regular
check-ins, updates, and ensuring that the maximum value is obtained from
the solution.

Engage with Value Proposition: Ensure that the value proposition is clearly
communicated, not just in terms of features but also how it aligns with the
business and technical needs of the client.

Educational Content: Share insightful content, webinars, and educational
materials, particularly during long-term development. This positions your
brand as a thought leader and keeps potential clients engaged.

Stay Adaptable: The SaaS landscape is dynamic. Prepare to pivot your
approach based on changes in the industry, client needs, or internal
product updates.

Robust Post-Sale Support: Once a deal is closed, the journey is not over.
Offers strong post-sale support, ensures smooth onboarding, and promptly
addresses any hiccups.

Clear and Concise Proposals: Ensure that the proposals are detailed yet
concise. Avoid technical jargon unless necessary and ensure that the client
understands every aspect of the offering.

Empathy and Active Listening: Understand that every client is unique.
Listen actively to their needs, concerns, and objections and tailor your
approach accordingly.

Ethical Selling: Always operates with integrity. Avoid overselling or
promising features that are not present or are still in the pipeline.

Set Clear Expectations: From the outset, ensure that both parties have a
clear understanding of what to expect in terms of deliverables, timelines,
and outcomes.


## 4. Developing a Champion

In the dynamic world of PreSales, we have strong products, skilled teams,
and a strong understanding of clients' needs. However, there is a key
element that is sometimes missed: the champion within the client’s
organisation. This person is crucial for guiding us through the client’s
landscape. Let us examine who this individual is and why they are a
cornerstone.

In Sales, a champion is more than just a fan of a product. This person is your
advocate of the client company. They strongly believe in your solution,
seeing beyond its functional and technical details. They understand how
your product perfectly fits and addresses their organisation’s needs. (Care
2020)

For PreSales, a champion is invaluable. This individual paves the way for
smoother interactions, offers insights into the organisation’s internal
dynamics, and accelerates the often-tedious approval processes. Their
advocacy bridges the gap between external sales pitches and internal
organisational buy-ins. (Kane 2020)

Identifying a champion is an art – a subtle dance of observation and
intuition. They are not always the loudest in the meeting room or the most
assertive in a discussion. Instead, their passion might manifest through
pointed questions, a keen interest in understanding the solution, or a clear
vision of how it aligns with the company’s goals. A true champion also
possesses the capability, directly or indirectly, to influence the decision-
making processes within the company.

As PreSales professionals set out to cultivate champions, their approach
needs to be multifaceted. It is not only about showcasing how remarkable
the product is. It is about enabling the champion to carry the torch forward.
Provide them with tools that arm them for advocacy, such as tailored
materials, comprehensive videos, or access to specialised portals. When a
champion steps into an internal discussion equipped with these resources,
they transform into potent allies. They do not just vouch for the software;
they become its herald.

However, while resources are important, developing champions goes
beyond that. It involves building relationships. This means creating and
nurturing trust, encouraging mutual respect, and creating a vision of
success together. By having meaningful conversations, understanding the
client's broader challenges, and showing how the solution fits into the
larger organizational picture, we can strengthen the champions' beliefs and
support.

Continuity is key. Relationships are not built overnight, and they are
certainly not sustained in silos. Regular touchpoints, be they through
updates, casual check-ins, or industry-relevant insights, can keep the bond
with the champion alive and robust. (Carnegie 2010)

The PreSales path is not always straightforward. Obstacles emerge, doubts
crop up, and reservations often loom large. As PreSales professionals work
tirelessly to address these issues externally, champions play a pivotal role
internally. Their deep-rooted belief in the solution, combined with their
innate understanding of their organisation, allows them to navigate
challenges, address concerns, and keep the momentum unabated.

While milestones in PreSales are often marked by closed deals and inked
contracts, it is essential to pause and celebrate silent victories. One such
triumph is the champions’ persistent advocacy, tireless efforts, and
unwavering support. A nod of appreciation, be it a token of gratitude, a
heartfelt note, or a simple acknowledgement, can go a long way.

The synergy between PreSales professionals and champions is a symphony
of mutual benefit. As champions navigate internal corridors, advocating for
a solution, PreSales teams reciprocate by delivering a product tailored to
add value to the organisation. When rooted in genuine commitment, this
harmonious exchange transforms into a partnership that transcends mere
business dealings.

In the grand scheme of PreSales, while presentations, demos, and metrics
are undeniably crucial, human touchpoints leave an indelible mark.
Cultivating champions embodies this sentiment. By recognising, nurturing,
and valuing this advocate, PreSales professionals not only elevate their
chances of success but also create lasting imprints where their solutions
resonate profoundly and meaningfully.

How to Develop a Champion
Build Trust and Relationships: Invest time in understanding the champion's
role, challenges, and objectives. Show genuine interest in their success and
build a relationship based on trust and mutual respect.

Provide Tailored Resources: Equip the champion with customized
materials, comprehensive demos, and access to specialized information
portals. This empowers them with the knowledge they need to advocate
effectively for your solution.

Engage in Regular Communication: Maintain consistent and meaningful
contact through updates, check-ins, and sharing industry insights. Regular
communication helps keep the relationship strong and ensures the
champion feels supported.

Empower Their Advocacy: Enable the champion to articulate the value of
your solution within their organization. Offer training sessions, success
stories, and detailed use cases that they can use to strengthen their internal
pitch.

Acknowledge and Appreciate: Recognize and appreciate the efforts of your
champion. A simple thank you note, a small token of appreciation, or public
acknowledgement of their support can go a long way in reinforcing their
commitment.

Involve Them in the Process: Include the champion in key discussions,
planning sessions, and strategy meetings. Their insights can be invaluable,
and their involvement can increase their investment in the success of the
solution.

Align with Their Goals: Understand and align with the champion’s personal
and professional goals. Show how your solution helps them achieve their
objectives, making their advocacy a mutually beneficial effort.


**Key Takeaway:**

Role of a Champion: A champion within a client’s organization bridges
external sales efforts and internal decision-making, ensuring smoother
interactions and faster approvals.

Identifying a Champion: Identifying a champion involves recognizing those
with a deep interest and influence in decision-making, even if they aren't
the most vocal.

Nurturing and Equipping Champions: Nurturing a champion requires
providing tailored resources, building trust, and maintaining regular
touchpoints to support their advocacy.


> **Story**

As a critical deal for our company, we had a powerful champion within the
client company. This champion provided essential insights into the
organisation and distinguished supporters from detractors. When faced
with opposition, the champion stood firm, ardently advocating for the
teams’ solutions and ensuring they fit the company’s needs.

His belief in the solution was so strong that they secured an exclusive
opportunity for the team to present directly to the company’s C-Level, an
opportunity not afforded to other vendors. This crucial presentation,
steered by the champions’ insights and support, effectively addressed
concerns, won over the board, and converted the opportunity into a
successful deal. The champion’s role was key to this significant victory.


## 5. Sales Discovery

The Sales Discovery phase is the first step towards building a possible sales
relationship. In this part, the sales team determines the specifics of a
possible client situation, problems, goals, and needs. It is not just a casual
chat; it is part of an investigation meant to learn more about a possible
client.

Sales discovery is crucial in PreSales, as it lays the foundation for
understanding the client-specific situation, problems, goals, and needs,
guiding the journey from a prospect to a successful client partnership. In
most cases, Sales will execute the discovery without PreSales, but PreSales
can support when new Sales are starting or when the requirements are very
complex. (Care 2020)

The BANT Framework in Sales Discovery
The better a sales professional is equipped during this phase, the higher is
the chance of turning a prospect into a paying client. This is where the BANT
framework comes into play, acting as a compass to navigate the
complexities of discovery. (Vassallo 2015)

Let us delve deeper into the BANT framework, its significance, and how it
paves the way for a more streamlined discovery process.

The Essence of the BANT Framework

BANT, an acronym for Budget, Authority, Needs, and Timeline, is an
attempted and tested methodology that provides a structured approach to
the Sales Discovery process. When you think of discovery, imagine it as a
voyage, and the BANT framework is the map that guides sales and PreSales
professionals throughout the journey, ensuring that you do not crack off
the track.

Budget: The Financial Compass

At the heart of any business transaction lies the question of money. The
'Budget' in the BANT framework refers to understanding the financial
capability and willingness of the potential client. It is about knowing how
much they are prepared to spend and understanding their comfort level
regarding financial investment. Having a clear picture of clients’ budgetary

constraints or capabilities ensures that the solution being offered aligns
with their financial reality. This eliminates the risk of proposing a solution
beyond their reach, ensuring that time and resources are optimally utilised.
Moreover, understanding a client’s budgetary outlook allows us to tailor
the solution in a manner that offers them the best value for their
investment.

Authority: The Decision-making Beacon

In the labyrinth of corporate structures, pinpointing the individual or group
with decision-making authority can be similar to finding a needle in a
haystack. The’ Authority’ aspect of the BANT framework emphasises the
importance of engaging with those who have the power to say 'yes' to a
proposal. It is not just about pitching to an interested party but to the right
party. Knowing who holds decision-making authority ensures that
conversations are productive and fruitful. It saves time and reduces the
chances of the sales process stalling due to bureaucratic hurdles.

Needs: The Core of Discovery

Every potential client is driven by unique challenges, requirements, or pain
points that they seek to address. Unearthing this is the essence of the Sales
Discovery and in more detail in the Functional & Technical Discovery phase.
The 'Needs' element of the BANT framework underscores the importance
of understanding these intricacies. When sales and PreSales professionals
truly grasp what a client requires, you are better positioned to align your
solutions effectively. This understanding is not just about what the client
says they want but also about reading between the lines to perceive the
underlying challenges. It forms the foundation upon which trust is built,
demonstrating to the client that the sales team genuinely understands their
world and offers a solution that addresses their specific concerns. (Cohan
2022)

Timeline: The Synchronisation

Every client has its own internal timeline, be it for implementing a solution
or for making a decisive purchase. The 'Timeline' facet of the BANT
framework revolves around aligning the sales process with the client’s
expectations. By establishing a clear timeline, sales professionals can

ensure that they are in sync with client’s internal processes, whether they
are preparing for a product launch or aligning with their fiscal year.
Knowing when a client intends to make a move helps prioritise efforts and
resources, ensure timely follow-ups, and keep the momentum of the sales
process alive.


**Key Takeaway:**

Budget: Understanding the financial capabilities and willingness of
potential clients. This helps tailor the solution offering without
overshooting budgetary limits.

Authority: Identifying who has decision-making authority. This ensures that
the sales and PreSales teams engage with individuals who can green-light a
project.

Needs: Unearthing potential clients’ exact requirements, challenges, and
pain points. This forms the crux of the discovery phase and dictates the
positioning of the solution.

Timeline: Establishing when the client aims to implement the solution or
when they anticipate making a decision. This helps align the sales process
with expectations.

Importance of Documentation: The OSD & CRM
Documentation represents the lighthouse in the vast ocean of PreSales and
sales processes, illuminating the path and ensuring smooth sailing. As
PreSales professionals, understanding and appreciating the importance of
documentation are paramount. Even the most insightful discovery
processes can lead to vague memories without proper documentation,
leaving teams scrambling for direction. One such critical piece of
documentation is the Opportunity Scoping Document (OSD), which
effectively captures the essence of the Sales & PreSales Discovery.

Understanding the Opportunity Scoping Document (OSD)
The Opportunity Scoping Document is more than just a piece of paper or a
digital file. It embodies all the insights, discussions, and understandings
gathered during the Sales and PreSales Discovery phases. Think of the OSD

as the bridge between the client’s needs and the vendor's offer. More
about the OSD in chapter 8.

The OSD encapsulates the key challenges and pain points at which a
potential client is grappling. By laying these out, it provides a clear map for
teams to tailor their solutions accordingly. In addition, any budgetary
discussions or constraints expressed by the client are also documented in
the OSD. This financial information acts as a boundary, ensuring that the
proposed solutions align with clients’ monetary limitations or aspirations.

Of course, this is not just about the problem or the budget. A crucial part
of any sales process is understanding the decision-makers. Who are the
individuals or groups that have the final answer? The OSD identifies these
decision-making stakeholders, allowing both PreSales and sales teams to
tailor their pitches and conversations to resonate with those who hold the
key to approval.

Finally, the OSD provides clarity on potential client implementation
timelines. This is pivotal. By understanding when the client aims to
introduce a solution or make a decisive action, teams can better align their
schedules, follow-ups, and priorities.

Integrating the OSD with Modern CRM Systems
Integrating OSD with a Client Relationship Management (CRM) system in
today’s digital age elevates its potency. A robust CRM system stores this
valuable information and allows easy retrieval, updates, and sharing among
team members. However, there are more modern CRMs than those used
for storage and retrieval.

Enter tools like Gong.io, which take documentation to the next level.
Gong.io analyses client interactions obtains summaries, and even granular
insights into these conversations. Imagine having a magnifying glass that
allows you to see the nuances of every client interaction. Such tools make
the PreSales preparation process more informed, strategic and effective.
Instead of relying solely on written notes, teams have data-driven insights
to guide subsequent steps.

The Imperative Role of Sales Discovery for PreSales Teams
Sales Discovery may first appear as just a part of the sales process, but it is
vital for the entire PreSales journey. It is like a guiding star for the PreSales
team, shaping every step that follows.

Continuity in Conversation

No client enjoys feeling like they are talking to disconnected entities within
an organisation. They want continuity. They want the next person you
speak to be aware of the previous discussion.

Before any engagement, PreSales professionals should be intimately
familiar with discussions held during the discovery phase. This knowledge
ensures that clients experience a seamless transition between Sales and
PreSales interactions. It removes the redundancy of having clients reiterate
the previously discussed points, fostering an environment of trust and
understanding.

Being Aware of Promises and Commitments

Commitments or promises can be made in the heat of a sales conversation.
The PreSales team should be aware of this promise. Without transparency,
there is a looming risk of either overcommitting or underdelivering, both of
which can damage client trust and reputation.

Addressing Client Queries Proactively

Clients were bound to answer the questions. If a client poses specific
questions during the discovery phase or expresses certain concerns, the
PreSales team should be equipped to address them and proactively bring
them up. Being prepared to handle these queries, or even just
acknowledging their awareness, showcases a company’s commitment to
clients’ needs.

The balance between PreSales and sales is delicate. It requires
coordination, trust, and most importantly, effective communication.
Proper documentation through tools such as the OSD and CRM systems
ensures that this dance is harmonious. As PreSales professionals,
understanding the gravity of the Sales Discovery phase and equipping

oneself with insights from it ensures not only individual success but also the
success of the organisation at large.

When should PreSales Step into Sales Discovery?
In an ideal scenario, the PreSales team is introduced into the client
engagement process after the Sales Discovery phase. This ensures that you
are presented with a structured understanding of the client, allowing you
to focus on the technical and solution-oriented aspects.

However, there are scenarios in which PreSales might be roped into Sales
Discovery, especially when the sales team is new or unfamiliar with certain
functional and technical nuances. Your technical expertise can help set the
right expectations and answer intricate questions upfront.

PreSales & Sales Discovery: A Symbiotic Relationship
The PreSales team ideally engages after the Sales Discovery phase, and it is
paramount to be informed of the discovery session's outcomes. The
effectiveness of PreSales engagements is significantly enhanced when you
are told of the discussions, promises, and, most importantly, the BANT
qualifications established upfront. (Cohan 2022)

However, in cases where the sales team is newer or requires technical
support during discovery, PreSales may actively participate in the Sales
Discovery phase. Your expertise can set the right tone, clarify technical
queries, and help establish a more detailed BANT qualification.

With the BANT framework at its core, Sales Discovery sets the stage for a
holistic understanding of the potential client. This understanding becomes
the guiding star for the PreSales team, enabling them to tailor their
engagement and solution presentations precisely. By ensuring that insights
from this crucial phase are documented, analysed, and shared effectively,
organisations can seamlessly transition from initial sales discussions to in-
depth functional and technical engagements, paving the way for successful
client relationships.

Demos in the Discovery Sales Discovery?
In an ideal scenario, conducting demos should be avoided during the Sales
Discovery phase. The primary objective of this phase is to gain a

comprehensive understanding of the client's needs, challenges, and
objectives. Jumping into a demo prematurely can distract from this goal
and may lead to misaligned expectations or missed insights.

Why Avoid Demos During Discovery?
Focus on Understanding: The Sales Discovery phase is about understanding
the client’s environment, pain points, and requirements. By concentrating
on asking the right questions and listening, you build a foundation of trust
and demonstrate a genuine interest in their business.

Tailored Solutions: Without fully understanding the client's needs, any
demo provided may not align well with their specific situation. This can
result in a less impactful demonstration that does not adequately address
their pain points or showcase relevant features.

Misplaced Expectations: Early demos can set the wrong expectations
about the solution’s capabilities and fit. To tailor your presentation
effectively, it’s essential to grasp the complete picture first.

Overcoming the Demo Hurdle
However, there are scenarios where clients insist on seeing something
tangible early on. In such cases, using demo automation can be a practical
solution:

Demo Videos: Pre-recorded demo videos tailored to different common
scenarios can provide clients with a visual understanding of the solution
without the need for a live demo. These videos can be curated to highlight
key features and use cases relevant to various industries or challenges.

Click-through Demos: Interactive click-through demos allow clients to
explore the solution at their own pace. These demos can be structured to
guide the user through specific functionalities, offering a hands-on
experience while ensuring that the core message remains controlled and
focused.

Using these automated demo tools not only satisfies the client's curiosity
but also reduces the pressure on the PreSales team to deliver a live demo
prematurely. This approach maintains the integrity of the discovery
process. It ensures that when a live demo is eventually conducted, it is

highly relevant and impactful, tailored to the client's specific needs. In
Chapter 11, we will discuss the demo automation in detail.

Qualify hard at the beginning
In the competitive landscape of B2B sales, the difference between the first
and second places is not just a notch on the leaderboard; it translates to a
significant loss in terms of time, resources, and opportunity costs. While a
podium finish in sports might still fetch a silver medal in sales, second place
equates to a lost opportunity.

The Extended B2B Sales Cycle
B2B sales dynamics differ from those of B2C sales. While consumers may
purchase within days or hours, B2B sales cycles can stretch over months.
The complexity is not only in terms of the product or solution being sold
but also the stakeholders involved, the financial magnitude of the decision,
and the subsequent impact on the client’s organisation.

Each stage of this elongated sales cycle – from the first contact through
sales discovery, multiple presentations, solution customisations,
negotiations, and finally, to the closing stage – demands meticulous work
hours. Collectively, hundreds of hours can easily be invested in a single sales
opportunity with multiple departments and teams involved.

The Cost of not qualifying early
To calculate the cost of a sales cycle, we would need the hourly rates (or
costs) associated with each role: Sales, PreSales, and Product Management.
Without specific hourly rates, I provide a generic framework that can be
used to plug in numbers.

Use Case: Imagine you are part of a sales team at a software company. Your
team has just embarked on an 8-month sales cycle with a potential client.
Here is how the costs break down.

Let us assume the following hourly costs for each role:

•   Sales: $50/hour

•   PreSales: $60/hour

•   Product Management: $70/hour

Calculation:

1. Sales Cost

\[Hours\] x \[Rate\] = Cost

100 hours x $50/hour = $5,000

2. PreSales Cost:

\[Hours\] x \[Rate\] = Cost

70 hours x $60/hour = $4,200

3. Product Management Cost:

\[Hours\] x \[Rate\] = Cost

10 hours x $70/hour = $700

Total Cost for the 8-month Sales Cycle: $5,000 (Sales) + $4,200 (PreSales)
+ $700 (Product Management) = $9,900

In this scenario, with the assumptions provided, the cost for an 8-month
sales cycle would be $9,900.

However, hold on for a moment.

While the direct costs associated with hours spent by various departments
are a significant factor, many more indirect and ancillary expenses amplify
the real cost of a sales cycle.

The Real Cost of Not Qualifying Early
The sales process, particularly in the B2B domain, is often complex,
multifaceted, and resource-intensive. Calculating sheer person-hours
dedicated by Sales, PreSales, and Product Management teams only
scratches the surface. When considering indirect costs, the real financial
implications become clearer.

Let us delve deeper:

Opportunity Cost: The time your Sales, PreSales, and Product Management
teams spend on one potential deal is the time not spent on other
potentially lucrative deals. Missed opportunities can be significant,
especially if the deal is not closed.

Resource Drain: Each unqualified lead progressing through the sales funnel
consumes resources. This is not only about the sales teams’ time but also
about the involvement of technical teams, product teams, and sometimes
even the executive team.

Dilution of Focus: Engaging with every lead without stringent qualifications
can spread the team thin, reducing engagement quality with genuine
prospects.

Financial Implications: There is a tangible cost associated with every sales
engagement: travel, setting up demos, preparing customised solutions, and
sometimes even offering pilot phases. Without proper qualification, these
costs can be stacked without any return on investment.

Overhead Costs: These are indirect costs that keep the team operating.
This includes rent, utilities, software licences, training, and other overheads
contributing to the cost of each sales hour.

Training and Development: Your teams are not just born experts. The
organisation invests heavily in training, workshops, certifications, and
continuous learning initiatives to ensure that you are at the top of the
game.

Tools and Software: Using advanced tools such as CRM systems, data
analytics, and communication platforms comes at a price. Their role in
facilitating the sales process is vital, and associated costs increase over
time.

Travel & Accommodation: For deals that require face-to-face meetings,
product demos, or on-site evaluations, the costs of flights,
accommodations, meals, and other travel-related expenses can be
substantial.

Collateral Development: Brochures, presentations, product demos, and
other sales materials incur design, development, and production costs.

Miscellaneous Costs: These might include costs associated with legal
consultations, contract drafting, third-party evaluations, and occasional
client entertainment or relationship-building activities.

When you factor in all of these elements, the $9,900 calculated for an 8-
month sales cycle based purely on person-hours seems conservative. The
actual cost is likely much higher, underscoring the importance of rigorously
qualifying opportunities.

If you do some simple math and assume you are a PreSales Consultant with
a quota of 1,5 Million USD per year and you are very good with a close rate
of around 35%, you close every third deal. Assuming that for a deal worth
around 300k USD, you need to spend around 80–160 hours on average.
Depending on where you are located, you have approximately 1600 hours
available per year. Assuming that you can use all of these hours, you can
actively work on around 10 – 20 opportunities. Thus, the pipeline is
approximately 3 to 6 million, depending on the abovementioned criteria.
Thus, if your closing rate is 35%, you can win approximately 1–2 million
USD. So, every deal you engage in and do not close decreases your chance
of archiving your quote.

Embarking on an extensive sales cycle without proper qualification risks
these direct and indirect financial costs and can strain team morale,
especially if efforts consistently do not yield results. The real expense goes
beyond just dollars; it is about the efficient use of time, resources, and
organisational energy. Therefore, qualifying hard in the beginning is a
strategic choice and an economic imperative.


**Key Takeaway:**

Sales Discovery is crucial as it lays the foundation for understanding a
client's specific needs, problems, and goals, which guides the entire sales
journey. It builds trust by demonstrating a deep understanding of the
client's situation, increasing the likelihood of a successful partnership.

The BANT framework (Budget, Authority, Needs, Timeline) is essential in
this phase. Understanding the client's financial capacity helps tailor
solutions within their budget. Identifying decision-makers ensures you
engage with those who can approve the deal. Uncovering specific

challenges and requirements allows for effective alignment of your
solution. Knowing the client's timeline helps synchronize the sales process
with their schedule.

Documentation plays a vital role in capturing all insights from the discovery
phase. The Opportunity Scoping Document (OSD) bridges the client's needs
with the proposed solution. Integrating this with a CRM system ensures
information is easily retrievable, updated, and shared among team
members, enhancing the efficiency and effectiveness of the sales process.


> **Story**

When I first started as a Solution Consultant, I was eager to prove my worth
and jumped at every opportunity that came my way. I found myself on
countless calls, diving into deals of all sizes and scopes. However, the reality
quickly set in: many of the opportunities I was given were either
unwinnable or small in scope. The information I received was often
incomplete or subpar, making my job even more challenging.

In those early days, I felt pressure to demonstrate my value to the team. I
understood that, as a newcomer, I was expected to take on these less-than-
ideal opportunities to learn and adapt. But it was frustrating and
exhausting, and I often wondered if I was making any real progress.

As time went on, I began to realize that success in this role was notwas
about more than just jumping into as many deals as possible. It required
patience and a strategic approach. I needed to build not only my functional
and technical skills but also my ability to sell effectively and listen actively.
This realization was a turning point for me.

Here's what I learned through my journey:

Stay Calm and Patient: I learned to remain calm and patient. Not every deal
needed to be chased, and it was okay to take a step back and assess the
situation before diving in.

Use Every Deal as a Learning Opportunity: Each deal, regardless of its size
or winnability, became a valuable learning experience. I took notes, asked
questions, and reflected on what worked and what didn't.

Build experience Gradually: Over time, as I gained more experience, I
became better at qualifying opportunities. I learned to identify which deals
were worth pursuing and which were not, allowing me to focus my efforts
more effectively.

Develop Functional and Technical Skills: While honing my technical skills
was important, I also invested time in understanding the functional aspects
of our solutions and how they aligned with customer needs.

Improve Sales Skills and Active Listening: Selling effectively requires more
than just knowledge of the product. It involved understanding customer
pain points, actively listening to their concerns, and addressing them with
tailored solutions.

Qualify Harder and Invest Time Wisely: As my experience grew, I became
more discerning in my qualification process. I learned to invest my time in
opportunities that had the highest potential for success, ensuring that my
efforts were spent where they could make the most impact.

Eventually, the really good deals started coming my way. My colleagues and
clients noticed the quality of my work, and I began to build a reputation for
delivering results. It took time and persistence, but the journey was
incredibly rewarding.

In retrospect, those early struggles were crucial to my development as a
Solution Consultant. They taught me the importance of patience, strategic
thinking, and continuous learning. By staying calm, using each opportunity
as a learning experience, and gradually building my skills and experience, I
transformed challenges into stepping stones for success.


## 6. Qualify Early, Qualify Hard,

Qualification can be a game changer, distinguishing between successful
conversions and missed opportunities, especially when performed firmly
and strategically. However, what does it truly mean to qualify hard?

Qualifying: A Strategy, Not Scepticism
The idea of qualifying hard is not about doubting or being sceptical of the
potential client. It's about making sure that both the selling organization
and the potential client are likely to succeed. The goal is to create a
situation where both parties win and to establish a long-term partnership.
The real essence of qualifying hard lies in understanding potential clients’
readiness for the product or solution and vendors’ commitment to deliver
what is expected.

The Importance of Assessing Fit
In a diverse world of business solutions, not every offering is a universal fit.
As the key is designed for a specific lock, solutions and products address
specific challenges and pain points. It is a fact of business life that not every
potential client will be the right fit for a given product or solution.

Early assessment of this fit is crucial. This helps to understand whether
what is being offered genuinely addresses the client’s challenges. Diving
deep into the sales process without this early assessment is akin to setting
sails without a compass. Sales and PreSales professionals must take the
time to discern the true nature of client’s needs and match those against
the strengths and capabilities of your product or solution. This step ensures
that energy and resources are not expended in directions unlikely to bear
fruit.

Engaging with Decision Makers
A significant aspect of the qualification process revolves around
understanding who makes decisions within a potential client organisation.
It is not just about pitching a product or solution; it is also about ensuring
that the pitch is directed at those with the authority to give it a nod.

Early engagement with these stakeholders can be illuminated. It clarifies
the decision-making process, highlighting potential bottlenecks or

challenges that might arise. Understanding this landscape is invaluable for
Sales professionals. This means that you can tailor your interactions,
presentations, and pitches to resonate with those who matter the most,
ensuring that unforeseen hierarchical complications do not derail the sales
process.

The Imperative of Setting Clear Expectations
A journey without clear expectations is fraught with potential
misunderstanding and misalignment. Clarity is paramount. Setting
expectations early can be the pillar upon which successful client
relationships are built.

Both the selling organisation and potential client should enter the
discussions with an unambiguous understanding of what to expect. This
covers everything from timelines to deliverables and from costs to support
structures. A transparent dialogue at the beginning ensures that both
parties are in sync as the relationship progresses, with no unwelcome
surprises lurking around the corner.

Qualifying hard is not an act of defiance or doubt; it is an exercise in
strategy that ensures that the path taken is the one most likely to lead to
success. For Sales and PreSales professionals, mastering this art is not just
beneficial; it is essential. This paves the way for a more efficient use of
resources, better-aligned client relationships, and, ultimately, a higher rate
of successful conversions. Through tools like BANT, practices such as early
assessment, engagement with key stakeholders, and setting clear
expectations, the journey of Sales becomes a harmonious symphony, with
each note played to perfection. Qualifying hard initially is not about being
overly sceptical; it is about being strategic.


**Key Takeaway:**

Understanding the Client Readiness: Using tools such as the BANT
framework can provide insights into the prospect's readiness to purchase.

Assessing Fit: Not every potential client correctly fits the product or
solution. Early assessment can determine whether the solution truly
addresses the clients’ pain points.

Stakeholder Engagement: Engaging with the right stakeholders early can
provide clarity on the decision-making process and avoid later-stage
bottlenecks.

Setting Clear Expectations: Both the selling organisation and potential
client should have clear expectations.


> **Story**

Some companies tend to promise more than what can be delivered. Early
in my career, I often aimed to close every deal, even to stretch the truth.
However, I learned that saying 'no' to a deal is not bad; it is essential to be
honest with yourself and your clients. Now, I make a point to be upfront
from the first meeting. If our solution is not the best fit, I will tell the client
and suggest other companies that could meet their needs. This approach is
about building trust, understanding clients’ goals, and evaluating if we can
genuinely add value. If we cannot, it is better to step back. The key is to
always add value.


## 7. Functional & Technical Discovery

Understanding the prospects’ requirements and technical details is critical
in today’s fast-paced sales environment. While initial Sales Discovery
meetings may focus on wider aims and ambitions, in-depth technical and
functional discoveries often determine the success or failure of a sale. This
chapter examines the subtle art of the Functional/Technical Discovery
phase, drawing on past insights and methods to construct a comprehensive
approach to this key point in the sales cycle. (Cohan 2022)

Peter Cohan writes in his book that discovery is the stage in the process
that will provide you with a competitive advantage (Cohan 2022):

•    Client trust grows as one learns about and explores one’s desires and
goals.
•    Demo is more personalised, result-oriented, and provides value.

After reading this book, I think you should read the Doing Discovery by
Peter Cohen. He talks in a lot more depth about how to make a proper
discovery and win the client’s heart. In addition, taking one of his courses
brings you a competitive personal advantage, which has helped me a lot in
my sales cycle.


> **Story**

Before getting into the details of Discovery, I want to share a common
experience that many of us have had. Imagine being called into a meeting
ready for a discovery session. You are prepared, have even conducted some
research, and use client data. The salesperson assures you that they know
exactly what the client wants and guides your presentation. However, just
a few minutes in, someone interrupts, saying your points are off-target and
they need something completely different. Your entire presentation goes
off-track. This explains why a thorough and in-depth discovery is important.
Now, let us examine how to do this in the right way.


### 7.1 The Dual Benefits of Discovery

For Vendors: Discovery enables vendors to fully understand the needs of a
prospect. This understanding enables them to propose an exact answer,
thus increasing the likelihood of a sale. (Cohan 2022)

For Prospect: Through the discovery process, prospects can discern which
vendors truly understand their requirements. The vendor investing time in
comprehensive discovery often stands out, as you accurately echo the
prospect environment, culture, and challenges. (Cohan 2022)

In its most basic form, discovery is a collection of knowledge. Having a
thorough understanding of the prospect issue, the suggested solution is a
perfect fit. Discovery is defined as "the process of gathering knowledge to
enable a detailed suggestion of a solution to a prospect problem" in its
simplest form.

Skipping discovery and jumping straight into a product demo is like a doctor
giving out medicine without checking what is wrong with the patient. This
is too soon and can even be risky.

In the world of PreSales, discovery is the bridge between understanding
and solutions. It is a pathway to cultivate relationships and ensure that
solutions resonate with each prospect’s unique challenges and visions.

Mastering the Discovery Process in PreSales
Discovery in PreSales is like a careful balance between learning enough
about prospects and building a good relationship with them. The main goal
is to understand the potential problems and come up with solutions that
are just right. But when do we know we have learned "enough"? This is
when the vendor has all of the information needed to recommend a perfect
solution with confidence. This detailed understanding mostly benefits
vendors.


### 7.2 The Process of Doing Discovery

The following nine steps explain how to execute a successful discovery.

1. Preparation:

Objective: To equip oneself with foundational knowledge about the
potential client and industry.

Activities: Do some preliminary research on the client business, its
competitors, the market, and how it stands in relation to those
competitors. Check any past interactions or commitments you have had

with the client. Create a list of open-ended questions that will help guide
conversations.

2. Initiate Engagement:

Objective: We set a stage for open and constructive conversation.

Activities: Schedule a dedicated discovery session with key stakeholders. I
recommend starting with 25 minutes and, in the end, asking for additional
sessions. This shows commitment but also an interest in diving deeper.
Clearly communicate the purpose of the discovery session: understanding
your needs, challenges, and objectives.

3. Information Gathering:

Objective: To extract critical information to tailor a solution effectively.

Activities: Asking open-ended questions will help you understand clients'
wants, needs, and points of pain. Active listening techniques will help you
ensure that the client feels understood and heard. Write down everything
the client says, thinks, or suggests. If a client allows a transcript or a
recording, it is always good to return it. Many AI tools can be used to
perform a summary automatically. (Rackham 1995)

4. Technical Assessment (if applicable)

Objective: To understand the current technical landscape and client
constraints.

Activities: Take a look at the tools, platforms, and technologies they already
have. Find any technical restrictions, limits, or rules that need to be
followed. Find out how to integrate your new systems with the ones they
already have and what problems might arise.

5. Stakeholder Interviews:

Objective: To gain diverse perspectives from various individuals within a
client’s organisation.

Activities: Engage stakeholders from different departments to gather
various insights. Adjust your line of questioning based on each

stakeholder’s role and expertise. Ensure confidentiality and create a
conducive environment for open dialogue.

6. Gap Analysis:

Objective: To identify discrepancies between clients’ current state and
their desired outcomes.

Activities: Analyse the information gathered to pinpoint gaps in their
processes, tools, or strategies. Prioritise these gaps based on their potential
impacts and urgency.

7. Feedback and Validation

Objective: To confirm your understanding and ensure alignment with
clients’ expectations.

Activities: Present a summary of your findings to the client, validating your
understanding. Seek feedback on additional areas of focus or correction.
Refine your understanding based on the feedback received.

8. Documentation and Sharing

Objective: To consolidate information and share it with relevant internal
teams or even with the client.

Activities: Organise and document all findings, insights, and feedback in a
structured manner. Share documentation with sales, technical, and
product teams to ensure alignment. Use the Opportunity Scoping
Document (OSD), which is explained in more detail in Chapter 8.

9. Plan Next Steps

Objective: The subsequent steps in the PreSales process are defined on the
basis of the insights gathered.

Activities: Based on the discovery findings, tailor your solution or proposal
to address clients’ specific needs. Schedule follow-up sessions or demos to
showcase how your solution addresses their challenges. (Cohan 2022)


### 7.3 Structured Approach to Discovery

In the context of PreSales, discovery often feels like unravelling a tightly
wound yarn ball. Each layer, each strand, reveals more about a potential
client and their needs. For PreSales professionals, the key lies in having a
structured approach to this unravelling process. With a step-by-step
methodology, not only does this task become more manageable, but it also
ensures that no critical detail is overlooked.

The steps are the following (Cohan 2022):

•   Preperation
•   Opening: Setting the Stage
•   Demographics: Getting to the Core
•   Supply Chain & Business Operations: The Flow of Value
•   Workflows/Processes Environment: Assessing the Tech Landscape
•   Major Pain: Identifying Pain Points and Opportunities
•   Extended Environment: Broadening the Scope
•   Culture: Navigating Company Dynamics
•   Vision Wrap-up: Clarifying the Way Forward

Preparation
Thorough preparation, which includes understanding the client's context,
is the cornerstone of a successful discovery process. Before engaging with
a potential client, it is crucial to gather as much information as possible.
This not only helps you understand the client's context but also
demonstrates your commitment and professionalism, making the client
feel valued and understood.

Start with researching the company. Use Google to get an overview of the
company, recent news, and any notable events. LinkedIn is a valuable
resource for reviewing the company's page, including recent posts,
company updates, and key personnel profiles. Look for mutual connections
or shared interests. If available, read the company's annual reports, as
these documents provide insights into the company's financial health,
strategic priorities, and plans. Additionally, explore the company's official
website to understand its products, services, and value propositions,
paying special attention to the "About Us" and "News" sections.

Understanding the industry is equally important. Look for recent industry
reports to grasp market dynamics, trends, and challenges that the company
might be facing. Identifying the company's main competitors and
understanding their positioning in the market can also provide insights into
potential areas of differentiation.

Gather insights on key personnel by reviewing their LinkedIn profiles to
understand their backgrounds, interests, and professional journeys. Check
other social media platforms for additional insights into their interests and
activities.

Review relevant case studies and references within your company. If your
company has worked with similar clients or within the same industry,
reviewing these case studies can help you understand what solutions were
effective. Look for testimonials or references that might be relevant to the
potential client, as these can help build credibility and trust.

Based on your research, prepare a list of questions that you can ask during
the discovery process. These should be open-ended questions designed to
uncover the client's needs, pain points, and goals. Formulate hypotheses
about the client's challenges and potential solutions to guide your
discovery conversation and help you validate or refine your understanding.
This approach makes the client feel that their needs and goals are being
addressed and considered in the process.

By leveraging these resources and taking the time to prepare, you position
yourself as a knowledgeable and credible partner who understands the
client's world and is ready to help them succeed. Preparation is the key to
unlocking a deeper understanding during the discovery phase and
ultimately crafting tailored solutions that meet the client's specific needs.

Opening the Conversation
Warm and welcoming initiation benefits every conversation, particularly in
a professional setting. When reaching out to prospects, starting with
rapport-building questions can lay the foundation for meaningful
exchange. Such a friendly beginning often eases any initial apprehension
that the prospect might have. By showcasing genuine interest in their
professional journey or even their day, you signal that you are not just there

to make a sale but to listen, understand, and assist. This approach is more
than just a strategic manoeuvre; it is about building a relationship based on
trust and mutual respect rights from the outset. One important remark
here is to Be yourself. Do not try to play a role or state interest in something
you are not interested in. For example, if one likes football and you do not.
It is not a big deal to find something else. If you check their LinkedIn profile,
you can find many points to discuss. (Cohan 2022; Belfort 2020)

Decoding Demographics
Once the conversation is in full swing, gaining insight into the client’s
organisational structure is essential. Understanding a company’s
demographics is not just about numbers; it is also about grasping the
dynamics of its operations. By enquiring about team sizes, specific roles,
and routine workflows, PreSales professionals can better understand how
an organisation operates. This knowledge becomes the bedrock upon
which solutions are crafted, ensuring that they are custom-fitted to the
company’s unique structure and requirements. (Cohan 2022)

Mapping the Supply Chain & Business Operations
In an increasingly interconnected world, the supply chain and business map
of an organisation reveal more than just operational details, you uncover
dependencies, bottlenecks, and potential areas of vulnerability. For
PreSales professionals, understanding this map is crucial for several
reasons.

Identifying dependencies: Recognising which parts of the business depend
on others can help pinpoint areas where disruption can cause significant
downstream effects. By identifying these, solutions can be crafted to
strengthen these weak links or to offer contingencies.

Uncovering Bottlenecks: Every supply chain or business operation might
have its chokepoints. Recognising these issues can guide the proposal of
solutions that alleviate these bottlenecks and ensure smoother operations.

Recognising opportunities for optimisation: Beyond just vulnerabilities, a
detailed understanding of a company’s supply chain or business map can
highlight areas ripe for optimisation. Perhaps there is a process that can be

streamlined or an intermediary step that can be bypassed with the correct
solution.

Gaining a Global Perspective: Especially for organisations with a global
footprint, understanding the supply chain map can reveal insights into
geopolitical risks, logistical challenges, or even socioeconomic factors that
might impact operations.

Building Holistic Solutions: With a clear picture of the supply chain or
business map, you can ensure that the solutions you propose do not
address only one part of the business in isolation. Instead, holistic solutions
that consider the entire operation can be offered.

Understanding Workflows & Tech Infrastructure
In our digital world, it is vital for you, as a PreSales professional, to obtain
the hang of a company’s tech setup. The solutions you suggest need to
tackle company issues and blend smoothly with the technology they
already use. It involves obtaining a deep understanding of the company’s
software, tools, and platforms. In this way, any solution offered will
improve, not interrupt, the existing processes. This stage of discovery is
similar to determining the inner workings of the clocks. Once you know how
every part ticks, you can add something new without causing any hiccups.

Pinpointing Pain Points
Every company, regardless of its success, faces challenges. Identifying these
pain points is similar to identifying weak spots in a structure. This is done
by identifying the weak links in a chain. This is essential for two reasons.

Understanding the Struggle: It is not just about knowing that a company
faces challenges but also about understanding the nature of these
challenges. Like a doctor diagnosing a patient, a PreSales professional must
pinpoint exactly where and why a client is facing difficulties. This deep
understanding is what separates a good solution from a great one.

Crafting Tailored Solutions: Once you have identified these pain points,
you can move beyond generic solutions. This is where your expertise really
shines. You are not just offering a one-size-fits-all fix; you are creating a
solution that speaks directly to clients’ unique issues. It is like crafting a key

that fits perfectly into their locks and addressing their specific needs and
concerns.

Let us now discuss the value realisation event (VRE). This is a crucial part of
the journey. A VRE is a specific, measurable milestone that occurs after a
solution is implemented. This is the moment when the client first sees
tangible benefits from a product or service. This could be an increase in
efficiency, reduction in costs, or any other significant improvement. (Cohan
2022)

The beauty of a VRE is that it is not just theoretical; it provides concrete
proof of the value that your solution brings. It is the "aha" moment for your
client, where the benefits of addressing their pain points become clear. It
is like turning on a light in a dark room; the improvements are visible and
undeniable. (Cohan 2022)

Expanding the Horizons
While the primary challenges a company faces are crucial, it is also
beneficial to explore areas that may be indirectly affected. Often, a
problem in an organisation can have a domino effect, causing issues in
related areas. By widening the scope of the enquiry, PreSales professionals
can uncover these secondary challenges and highlight additional ways that
the product or solution can be of value. This approach involves seeing the
bigger picture and understanding how various parts of an organisation
interconnect and impact each other.

Culture & Integration
Every organisation, be it a growing startup or a global conglomerate, has a
unique ethos. Recognising and understanding this culture is vital. It is not
sufficient for a solution to be technically sound; it needs to resonate with
the company’s intrinsic nature. By gauging how a company typically
approaches changes, particularly when adopting new solutions, PreSales
professionals can ensure that their offerings are in tune with the company’s
cultural dynamics. Even the most groundbreaking solution can falter if it
clashes with a company’s ethos.

Concluding with Vision
As the discovery phase approaches its conclusion, it is beneficial to bring
the conversation full circle. This involves a discussion of the prospects for
future aspirations and how the proposed solutions align with this vision. By
charting a clear roadmap for subsequent interactions, both parties can
move forward with a shared understanding and objectives. This clarity not
only streamlines future engagements but also solidifies the relationship
between the vendor and prospect. (Cohan 2022)

As this discovery journey concludes, an essential step is to connect the dots.
The identified problems, be they primary or secondary, need to be viewed
in the broader context of the company’s overarching business implications.
By linking granular issues with critical business concerns, PreSales
professionals offer a holistic view, showcasing not just the immediate
benefits of your solution but its long-term value.

In essence, the structured approach to discovery is more than just
gathering information; it is about gaining an understanding. It is a dance of
enquiry and empathy between offering and listening. For PreSales
professionals, mastering this dance ensures that you not only present
solutions but also build relationships, fostering trust and collaboration at
every step.


**Key Takeaway:**

Preparation: Effective preparation for discovery involves thorough
research on the company, industry, and key personnel using resources like
Google, LinkedIn, annual reports, and internal case studies to build
credibility and tailor solutions to the client’s specific needs.

Opening the Conversation: Initiating rapport-building questions sets a
warm, congenial tone. This helps foster bonds, making the prospect more
open and receptive. A question reflecting a genuine interest in their
professional journey can be a great start. (Rackham 1995; Greene 2018)

Deciphering Demographics: The goal is to map out the organisational
structure, delving into team sizes, roles, and workflows. This structural
understanding serves as the foundation upon which solutions can be built.

Understanding Workflows and Tech Infrastructure: This step focuses on
assimilating information about the technical landscape, ensuring that your
solutions integrate seamlessly.

Understanding the Supply Chain & Business Operations: Uncovering
dependencies, bottlenecks, and potential areas of vulnerability in the
Supply Chain.

Pinpointing Pain Points: Identifying core challenges is crucial. It involves
gauging where the shoe pinches the most and offers a cushioned sole in
response.

Expanding the Horizons: Beyond immediate challenges, related areas may
be affected. This exploration can reveal additional layers of potential value
for a product.

Culture & Integration: Recognising the company’s ethos and method of
adopting new solutions ensures that the offering is in harmony with the
company’s intrinsic nature.

Concluding with Vision: A wrap-up centred around the prospect's forward-
looking vision ensures alignment and charts out a roadmap for subsequent
interactions.


### 7.4 Detailed Stages and Questions


#### 7.4.1 Opening: Setting the Stage

The first few minutes of the talk are very important. They set the direction
for the entire chat and swayed the final result. Therefore, starting the
conversation correctly is key.

Imagine walking into a room filled with strangers. The first person you
decide to speak to might be hesitant, wary, or simply curious. Your opening
sentence determines whether you remain distant or become an engaged
conversational partner. The same principle applies when entering a
PreSales conversation. The goal is to foster a bond that goes beyond mere
transactional discussions and to create a foundation of trust and mutual
respect.

Initiating your conversation with a rapport-building question is not just a
technique; it is an art. It is akin to the first brushstroke on a blank canvas,
setting the mood and tone for the masterpiece to come. These questions
serve two purposes. First, you break the ice, serving as a bridge between
two individuals who might meet for the first time. You cut through any
initial awkwardness or hesitation, paving the way for more open dialogue.
Second, and perhaps more importantly, you establish an immediate
connection with your prospects. By expressing genuine interest in the
prospect's background or journey, you are subtly communicating that this
conversation is not just about making a sale but about understanding and
valuing the person in front of you. (Cohan 2022)

The importance of building rapport in the early stages cannot be
overstated. As humans, we are inherently more inclined to open up, share
information, and collaborate with those we trust. Furthermore, as many of
us know, trust does not come easily. Therefore, it needs to be nurtured.
When a PreSales professional takes time to cultivate this trust from the
outset, they are setting the stage for a productive conversation and laying
the foundation for a potentially long-term relationship.

A genuine conversation is smooth and naturally flows from one topic to
another. Building rapport ensures that our arising conversations have
smooth and genuine quality. Prospects will feel more at ease, making them
more likely to share critical information, express their concerns openly, and
engage in meaningful dialogue. This genuine connection can differentiate
between a conversation that feels forced and transactional and one that is
insightful and transformative.

What kind of questions best serve this purpose?

Imagine being asked, "Tell me – before we dive in – how did you ascend to
this position of power and authority at [prospect company name]?" This
question is not just about understanding the prospective professional
journey. It acknowledges their achievements and expresses admiration for
their journeys. This question encourages reflection and sharing, making the
prospect feel valued and respected. (Rackham 1995; Cohan 2022)

Similarly, inquiring, "What drove you to your current role, and how has your
journey been so far?" delves deeper into the understanding of individuals’

motivations, aspirations, and experiences. It is not a superficial query; it
seeks to understand the essence of a person’s professional life.


**Key Takeaway:**

Importance: Initiating your conversation with a rapport-building question
helps break the ice and establish immediate connectivity with your
prospects.

Reason Behind: Building rapport establishes trust, making the ensuing
conversation smoother and more genuine.

Typical Questions:

“Tell me – before we dive in – how did you ascend to this position of power
and authority at [prospect company name]?”

“What drove you to your current role, and how has your journey been so
far?”

"Leaders always inspire me in your position. Could you share a defining
moment or experience that influenced your professional path?"

"Every organisation has its unique culture and story. What about [prospect
company name] drew you in and motivated you to participate?"

"I have come across many professionals with diverse backgrounds. What
unique experiences or challenges do you believe have shaped your
perspective in this role?"

"Considering the rapid evolution of industries today, what keeps you
passionate and motivated in your current role?"

"From your experience, how has the landscape of [specific industry or field]
transformed, and how have you adapted to these changes?"

"Is there a particular mentor or influencer who has played a significant role
in guiding your professional decisions or shaping your leadership style?"

"Navigating to a role like yours must have come with a fair share of the
learning curves. Are there any insights or anecdotes you would be willing
to share that were particularly enlightening?"

"Before we delve deeper, I would love to understand what a typical day is
like for you at [prospect company name]? What energizes you the most
about it?"

"In the context of your industry and your role, what milestones or
achievements are you particularly proud of?"

"Given the rich tapestry of experiences one accumulates in a role like yours,
are there any values or philosophies you have found consistently guiding?"


#### 7.4.2 Demographics: Getting to the Core

There is an inherent need to understand the essence, uncover hidden
dynamics, and truly get to an organisation’s heart. Understanding a
company’s demographics is one of the most critical areas of this
investigative journey. This is not merely related to number or location. It is
a window into the structural core and rhythm of an enterprise. (Cohan
2022)

At the foundational level, company demographics provide crucial insights
into the organisation’s structural backbone and its inherent workflow
dynamics. When we talk about the structural backbone, we refer to the
matrix of teams, the hierarchy of roles, the distributed versus centralised
character of operations, and the sheer size and expanse of the organisation.
These elements are much like the framework of a building. Just as the
architecture of a building determines how spaces interact, the structural
elements of a company dictate how different units communicate, how
decisions flow, and where power centres reside.

On the other hand, workflow dynamics are the heartbeat of this structural
body. You signify the tasks that keep the organisation alive and thriving.
Understanding these dynamics is essential, whether it is a technology firm
where daily workflows revolve around software development cycles or a
manufacturing entity focused on production lines. This knowledge reveals
how the day unfolds for most employees, what challenges arise, and where
efficiencies and inefficiencies lie.

Why is this understanding pivotal in PreSales? The reason is twofold. First,
a clear grasp of demographic makeup can guide product customisation.
Imagine pitching a software solution for a multinational corporation with

globally distributed teams. The challenges they face, driven by time zones,
cultural nuances, and communication lags, will vastly differ from a local
startup with a team of ten. Customising a product or solution pitch to cater
to these unique challenges can be the difference between a sale and a
missed opportunity.

Second, demographics offer an invaluable context for pain points. To
elucidate this, consider an organisation that has grown exponentially over
the years. As they have expanded, they might have adopted various tools
and solutions in an ad hoc manner. Over time, this could lead to a
fragmented technological landscape, causing integration nightmares.
Knowing this history, a PreSales professional can position the product as a
solution that addresses the current needs and alleviates legacy challenges.

Now, the question arises: How does one go about gathering this
demographic information? The key lies in asking pointed and thoughtful
questions. A simple query like, "Can you provide a breakdown of your team
sizes and locations?" can reveal a company’s scale and geographic
distribution. This could be essential when considering factors such as
product training, support availability, and integration complexity. (Cohan
2022)

Digging deeper into the day-to-day with a question such as, "What are the
primary workflows your team deals with daily?" can reveal the company’s
pulse. It sheds light on crucial repetitive tasks and is potentially ripe for
optimisation.

Lastly, understanding the evolution with "How has the company’s structure
evolved over the years?" can provide insights into the company’s growth
trajectory, past challenges, pivots, etc. Such insights can be instrumental in
tailoring a solution that respects a company’s past while guiding it into the
future.


**Key Takeaway:**

Importance: Understanding company demographics offers insights into the
structural backbone and workflow dynamics.

Reason Behind: Grasping the demographic makeup can guide product
customisation and offer context for pain points.

Typical Questions:

“Can you provide a breakdown of your team sizes and locations?”

“What are the primary workflows your team deals with daily?”

“How has the company’s structure evolved over the years?”

“Who are the primary stakeholders in various departments, and how do
you typically collaborate?”

“How would you describe the decision-making process within your
organisation?”

“Are specific teams or units pivotal to your company’s daily operations?”

“How has your employee count changed over the past few years, and what
factors contributed to that growth or reduction?”

“Do you have remote or hybrid teams, and how do you manage
communication and workflows across different setups?”

“Are certain times of the year or quarters particularly busy or crucial for
your company?”

“How do you foresee the company’s demographic structure changing in the
near future, and what are the driving factors behind that vision?”

“Do different teams use different tools, or is there a centralized system in
place for most workflows?”

“How do internal team transitions or role shifts typically occur within the
organisation?”

“Could you share any challenges you have faced in terms of scaling,
especially as your team sizes and locations have expanded?”


#### 7.4.3 Supply Chain & Business Operations: The Flow of Value

Venturing the realm of PreSales often feels like navigating the intricate
circuitry of a complex machine. Every gear, wire, and node plays a critical
role. Within this machinery, the supply chain and business operations stand
out as vital pathways – they are the channels through which value flows,

where products become more than just items, and services transform into
solutions.

An organisation’s supply chain and business operations tell a story of
creation, delivery, and consumption. This narrative is not just about raw
materials becoming end-products. It captures the lifecycle of value, from
the initial spark of an idea to the ultimate satisfaction of a client’s needs.

When we discuss the 'flow of value', we delve into the entire journey of a
product or service. It encompasses the sourcing of raw materials, the
nuances of manufacturing or development, the logistics of distribution, and
the strategies behind sales and client engagement. Just as rivers have
tributaries, these operations have multiple streams feeding into the main
channel, each with its own challenges and opportunities.

Business operations, on the other hand, speak of day-to-day. They unravel
the processes, routines, and systems that keep the organisation ticking.
Understanding these operations is akin to understanding the rhythm of an
organisation’s heartbeat, from internal communications to decision-
making protocols and from resource allocation to performance metrics.

Why is this insight pivotal in Presales? First, understanding the supply chain
allows product positioning that aligns with a company’s operational flow.
For example, a solution that optimises inventory management could be a
goldmine for a company grappling with warehousing inefficiencies. Second,
grasping business operations allows you to pitch solutions that seamlessly
integrate with the client’s daily routine, enhancing productivity rather than
disrupting it.


**Key Takeaway:**

Importance: Grasping the Supply Chain & Business Operations is key to
understanding how an organisation creates and delivers value.

Reason Behind: This insight allows for tailored solutions that integrate
seamlessly into clients’ operational flow and daily routine.

Typical Questions:

“How does your product journey begin, from the idea to client delivery?”

“Can you describe the primary challenges faced in your manufacturing or
development phase?”

“How do you manage inventory and warehousing? Are there any known
inefficiencies?”

“What are the major distribution channels for your products or services?”

“How do you handle returns, defects, or client feedback within your supply
chain?”

“What systems or software are currently in place for managing your
business operations?”

“How do teams communicate and collaborate across different phases of
your supply chain?”

“Are there seasonal variations or peaks in your supply chain or business
operations?”

“How do you foresee your supply chain evolving in the future? Any major
changes on the horizon?”

“How do external factors, such as geopolitical changes or environmental
concerns, impact your operations?”

“What metrics or KPIs are central to gauging the efficiency and
effectiveness of your operations?”

“How do you handle disruptions or bottlenecks in your supply chain? Any
contingency plans in place?”

“How are innovations or new methodologies introduced into your supply
chain or business operations?”


#### 7.4.4 Workflows/Processes Environment: Assessing the Tech

Landscape
In the intricate maze of PreSales, one of the paramount areas that requires
deep attention and understanding is the prospective company technology
landscape. For a PreSales professional, this is not just about understanding
which tools and systems a company uses; it is also about the broader

implications of those tools in the business’s day-to-day operations and
long-term strategy.

Understanding a company’s technology infrastructure is like understanding
its nervous system. Whether small or massive, every organisation relies
heavily on its technology infrastructure to operate, grow, and thrive. This
reliance makes it imperative for PreSales professionals to recognise and
grasp this infrastructure. Doing so provides a clear snapshot of the potential
compatibility issues or challenges that might arise when integrating a new
product or service. This is akin to understanding the layout of a house
before renovating it.

Now, why is this so crucial? The answer lies in the age-old age – time is
money. When a new solution is introduced into an existing technology
environment, if it is not aligned well with existing tools, systems, and
processes, it can cause significant disruptions. These disruptions not only
result in operational hiccups but can also translate to financial losses and,
more importantly, a loss in the client’s trust in a vendor. If a client spends
more time grappling with integration issues, they spend less time realising
the actual value of the product or solution. In a world where rapid
deployment and quick wins are cherished, ensuring minimal disruption is
not just a nicety but a necessity.

Moreover, diving into the technological landscape of a company offers an
insightful look into its past, present, and future. How? A company’s
technology infrastructure can often be a testament to its past decisions,
current needs, and future aspirations. Did you invest in state-of-the-art
systems five years ago and are now looking for upgrades? Are you still using
legacy systems, signalling an imminent, comprehensive tech overhaul? Or
perhaps you mix old and new, indicating a phased approach to technology
adoption? These nuances become evident when we unravel the tapestry of
their technological environment.

Hence, when engaging with prospects, it is essential to pose questions that
provide insights into their systems and offer a narrative of their journey and
vision.

A foundational question such as, “Can you walk me through your current
