Consider the universe of potential business solutions as a vast ocean
brimming with opportunities. In this vast expanse, FTQ acts as a lighthouse,
illuminating the path for PreSales professionals, guiding them to the shores
of viable and impactful solutions. (Keenan 2018; Kane 2020)


### 9.1 Using the OSD to Qualify from a functional &

technical Perspective
Beyond using the Opportunity Scoping Document (OSD) as a repository of
information, it plays a crucial role in the qualification process from both
functional and technical perspectives.

Envision the qualification process as a distillation of what is feasible,
valuable, and impactful. From a multitude of options, you are honing in on
those solutions and approaches that truly resonate with prospects’ needs
and constraints. In this context, the OSD acts as a filter, ensuring that what
emerges from the qualification process is a concentrated solution custom-
tailored for the prospect. (Cohan 2022)

Diving Deep into Functional Qualification with the OSD
Functionally, you are looking to ensure that the proposed solutions serve
the core business needs. It addresses pain points, optimises processes, and
introduces features that bring tangible value.

The OSD provides a window for a prospective functional landscape. By
delving into the described challenges and major pain points in the OSD, you
can gain a nuanced understanding of their current operational
inefficiencies. It is not just about knowing what problems exist but

understanding their implications, their ripple effects through the
organisation, and the root causes behind them.

Armed with this knowledge, the qualification process then seeks to map
each of these functional challenges to specific features or modules of the
proposed solution. It is a dance of alignment, where the rhythm is set by
the OSDs’ detailed account of the prospective functional world. Every
feature proposed and every module introduced must echo the insights
captured in the OSD.

Now, we compare this to the desired outcomes and vision section of the
OSD. Here, you have the brighter side of the picture - the prospect's
aspirations. In functional qualification, you are also ensuring that the
proposed solutions not only solve existing problems but also pave the way
for realising these aspirations. It is a balancing act, addressing the present
and laying the foundation for a brighter and more efficient future.

Navigating Technical Qualification through the OSDs Lens
While functional qualification is about the 'what,' technical qualification
delves into the 'how.' It is about ensuring that the proposed solution not
only fits functional needs but is also technically feasible, sustainable, and
efficient.

Here is where the OSD, with its detailed account of the prospects of IT
overview and architecture, becomes indispensable. By understanding the
prospect's current technical landscape, you can gain insights into the
systems they employ, their interconnectivity, and potential bottlenecks or
legacy constraints.

Thus, technical qualification becomes an exercise that ensures
compatibility. You are looking to validate that the proposed solution can be
seamlessly integrated with existing systems. It ensures that the solution
does not disrupt but enhances the current architecture. In cases where
disruptions are inevitable, they are strategic, leading to long-term
efficiency and scalability.

Another crucial component to consider is the assumptions and gap section
of the OSD. Qualifications serve as a touchstone. It offers clarity on the
areas of uncertainty, potential points of friction, and areas that might

require deeper exploration or validation. Being aware of these assumptions
safeguards the process against overlooked nuances and ensures that the
proposed solutions are grounded in reality.

A Confluence of Functionality and Technicality
The true magic of the OSD in the qualification process arises from the
synergy between functional and technical qualifications. Both dimensions
are intrinsically linked, and the OSD ensures that this linkage is robust and
value-driven.

Consider the project prioritisation and business release sections of the OSD.
Here, you understand the sequence in which the prospect wishes to roll out
solutions. This information is pivotal for both functional alignment
(ensuring that the right features are introduced at the right time) and
technical feasibility (guaranteeing that the infrastructure is prepared and
optimised for each rollout).

Additionally, as you move forward on the sales journey, referencing the
OSD ensures continuity and coherence. Whether demonstrating a feature,
outlining a technical integration strategy, or negotiating terms, the OSD
remains the beacon, guiding each step to ensure alignment with the
insights and aspirations captured during the discovery phase.


### 9.2 Key Components of Functional & Technical

Qualification
Our goal is straightforward: to ensure that the solutions we propose are
not only an ideal fit for the prospect's needs but also technically feasible
within our capabilities. This essential process is split into two closely linked
parts: functional and technical qualifications.

Imagine approaching a grand tapestry, beautifully detailed and vast. From
a distance, you appreciate its overall design — the functional aspect. As you
step closer, you begin to discern the individual threads and intricate
patterns woven into the fabric — the technical dimension. Both views are
essential to understanding the entire masterpiece.

Embarking on the Functional Qualification Journey
At its core, functional qualification revolves around a central question: "Will
our solution effectively address the prospect's business needs and pain
points?" It is the realm of understanding ‘what’ the solution does in the
broader context of business goals and challenges.

Understanding Business Needs: The foundation of functional qualification
begins by diving deeply into the business needs of the prospect. This is not
just a superficial acknowledgement of challenges but a profound
comprehension of the operational, strategic, and industry-specific issues
they face. It requires us to put ourselves in the shoes of the prospect, to
feel the pinch points, and to visualise the roadblocks we encounter. (Hess
2020)

Mapping Solutions to Needs: Once a clear picture of business needs
emerges, the next step is aligning those needs with the specific features
and capabilities of your solution. This mapping is at the heart of functional
qualifications. It is about ensuring that for every pain point identified, there
is a feature or module in the solution that addresses it.

Assessing Value Proposition: Beyond aligning features with needs, it is vital
to understand the real value of the solution. How does it optimise the
current processes? How might it introduce efficiency or open new avenues
of revenue? Functional qualification seeks to create a compelling narrative
of transformative values.

Futureproofing: The business world is dynamic, with priority shifting and
market evolution. Hence, functional qualification is not just about
addressing current needs but also anticipating future requirements. It
ensures that the solution is scalable, adaptable, resilient, and ready to
evolve with the business landscape. (Kane 2020; Care 2020)

Navigating the Depths of Technical Qualification
Technical qualifications, on the other hand, dive into the intricate 'how' of
the solution. It seeks to answer the question: "Can our solution be
seamlessly implemented in the prospective technical environment?" This is
a thorough assessment of the compatibility, integration capabilities, and
technical robustness.

Understanding Technical Infrastructure: Just as a builder needs to
understand the nature of soils before construction, a PreSales professional
must grasp the technical architecture of a prospect. This includes the
software and hardware employed, data workflows, integration
touchpoints, and any legacy systems that may be in play.

Compatibility Assessment: With a firm understanding of the prospective
technical landscape, the next step is to ensure that your solution fits like a
glove. This is not merely about aligning technical specifications but ensuring
that the implementation will not introduce unforeseen disruptions or
challenges.

Integration Capabilities: In today’s interconnected digital ecosystems, no
solution stands alone. Thus, assessing how easily and effectively a solution
integrates with existing systems is paramount. Whether through APIs,
middleware, or standard connectors, the goal is seamless data flow and
functionality interplay.

Technical Constraints and Assumptions: Every technical environment has
constraints, be it due to legacy systems, stringent security policies, or
unique data storage solutions. Recognising these and identifying
assumptions ensures that the proposed solutions are realistic and
grounded in implementable reality.

The Interplay between Functional and Technical Qualification
While we have discussed these qualifications as two separate streams, they
are in essence two sides of the same coin. A feature, regardless of how
functionally sound and aligned with business needs, loses its value if it
cannot be implemented technically. Conversely, a solution that is
technically perfect but does not address core business needs is a square
peg in a round hole.

A crucial aspect is the iterative nature of the qualifications. As you delve
deeper into the technical infrastructure, you might uncover nuances that
impact functional alignment and vice versa. For instance, discovering a
legacy system might introduce technical challenges but might also shed
light on some long-standing functional workarounds that the prospect has

been using. These insights can further refine the process of functional
qualification.

Example Elements and Structure of the FTQ
Summary from the BANT: Budget (B), Authority (A), Need (N), Timing (T),
Overall Average

Summary from the ORC: Assessment of Deal Worth, Comment/Evidence,
Value, Weight, Score

Strategic Alignment: Market Strategy Fit, Ecosystem Fit, Industry Fit,
Innovation Potential, Referenceability, Strategic Partnerships

Company and Prospect Information (GTM Specific): Global Presence, Local
Sales Presence, Language Application / Support / Sales / PS / Contract,
Volumes (Users, Compliance)

Technical Requirements and Compatibility: Current Software
Infrastructure, Future Tech Plans, Integration Capabilities, Customization
Requirements, Integration Complexity, Data Migration and Integrity

Workflow and Process Analysis: Workflow Complexity, Process
Digitalization

Pain Points and Needs: Major Pain Points, Impact Quantification, Strategic
Importance to Prospect, Operational Efficiency Gains, Compliance and Risk
Management, Solution Maturity Level, Functional Fit

Decision-Making Process: Key Stakeholders, Budget Approval, Decision
Timeline, Budget and Approval Process, Cost-Benefit Analysis

Competition and Differentiation: Competitive Landscape, Competitor
Solutions Considered, Unique Selling Proposition (USP), Reference/Case
Study/Testimonial, Price Sensitivity

Legal and Compliance: Compliance Requirements, Data Privacy and
Security, Contractual Requirements

Implementation and Adoption: Implementation Timeline, Adoption and
Change Management, Project Complexity, Support and Training Needs,
Long-term Support and Evolution

Value and ROI: Value Proposition Alignment, ROI Calculation

Future Growth and Scalability: Scalability, Future Needs and Roadmap
Alignment

Cultural and Organizational Fit: Organizational Culture, Change
Management Alignment

Solution Specific Question: Volumes, Modules etc.


### 9.3 How to create and implement an FTQ

Ensuring that a solution meets both the functional needs and technical
requirements of a prospect is paramount. This process, known as
Functional & Technical Qualification (FTQ), serves as a critical step in
aligning solutions with client expectations and technical landscapes. FTQ
acts as a guiding framework, much like a compass in a vast ocean of
potential solutions, helping PreSales professionals navigate towards the
right fit for their clients. This chapter will explore the steps to create and
implement an effective FTQ framework, ensuring your solutions are both
practically viable and technically robust.

Creating the FTQ Framework
1. Define Objectives and Scope

Objective: Clearly outline what you intend to achieve with the FTQ process.
The main goal is to ensure that the proposed solutions are both functionally
appropriate and technically feasible.

Scope: Define the scope by determining the aspects of the business and
technical environment that need evaluation. This includes understanding
the client's business processes, pain points, technical infrastructure, and
future needs.

2. Gather Comprehensive Information

Client's Business Needs: Dive deeply into the business needs of the
prospect. Understand their operational, strategic, and industry-specific
challenges.

Technical Landscape: Assess the current technical infrastructure, including
software and hardware in use, data workflows, integration touchpoints,
and any legacy systems.

3. Develop Evaluation Criteria

Functional Criteria: Identify key functional requirements that the solution
must meet. This includes specific features, process optimizations, and value
propositions that align with the client's business needs.

Technical Criteria: Develop criteria to assess the solution's technical
compatibility. This includes integration capabilities, scalability,
sustainability, and potential technical constraints.

4. Create a Scoring System

Scoring Metrics: Establish a scoring system to evaluate how well the
proposed solution meets each criterion. This can be a simple scale (e.g., 1-
5) or a weighted system where critical criteria have more influence on the
overall score.

Documentation: Ensure that each aspect of the evaluation is well-
documented to provide transparency and facilitate decision-making.

5. Develop an Evaluation Process

Step-by-Step Procedure: Outline a clear, step-by-step process for
conducting the FTQ. This should include gathering initial data, conducting
interviews with stakeholders, reviewing technical documentation, and
scoring the solution against the defined criteria.

Collaboration: Involve cross-functional teams, including sales, PreSales,
product management, and development, to ensure a holistic evaluation.

Implementing the FTQ Framework
1. Train the Team

Workshops and Training Sessions: Conduct training sessions to ensure all
team members understand the FTQ framework, its importance, and how
to use it effectively.

Documentation: Provide detailed documentation and examples to help
team members grasp the nuances of the evaluation process.

2. Pilot the Framework

Select Initial Projects: Start with a few pilot projects to test the FTQ
framework. This allows for adjustments and fine-tuning based on real-
world applications.

Gather Feedback: Collect feedback from all stakeholders involved in the
pilot to identify areas for improvement.

3. Refine and Optimize

Continuous Improvement: Use insights from the pilot projects to refine the
framework. This might include adjusting scoring metrics, adding new
evaluation criteria, or improving the process flow.

Regular Updates: Ensure the FTQ framework remains relevant by regularly
updating it to reflect changes in technology, market conditions, and
business needs.

4. Integrate into the Sales Process

Standard Operating Procedure: Make the FTQ framework a standard part
of the sales process. Ensure it is used consistently for all prospective
solutions.

Alignment with Sales Goals: Align the FTQ process with overall sales goals
and strategies to ensure it supports the organization's broader objectives.

5. Monitor and Review

Performance Metrics: Establish metrics to monitor the effectiveness of the
FTQ framework. This includes tracking the success rate of qualified
opportunities, client satisfaction, and time efficiency.

Regular Reviews: Conduct regular reviews of the FTQ process to ensure it
continues to meet its objectives and remains aligned with business goals.

Creating and implementing an FTQ framework is crucial for ensuring that
proposed solutions not only meet prospects' functional needs but are also
technically feasible and sustainable. By following a structured approach,

PreSales professionals can significantly enhance their ability to deliver
tailored, impactful solutions that drive business success.


### 9.4 Opportunity Review Call (ORC)

The Opportunity Review Call (ORC) embodies this collaborative spirit,
designed to collectively assess, strategize, and, most crucially, qualify
opportunities. This chapter explains the purpose, structure, and profound
value of ORCs in the Sales journey. Please note that not every opportunity
must be presented in the ORC. This is more where the fit from a technical
or functional perspective needs to be discussed or where 90% of the
requests are fulfilled, but the missing 10 % need to have commitments from
other departments.

Picture the Opportunity Review Call (ORC) as a gathering of masterminds,
each contributing a distinct voice, perspective, and expertise. It is a
rendezvous where different facets of an opportunity are laid bare,
scrutinised, and polished, ensuring that the sales approach is robust,
comprehensive, and primed for success.

Purpose of the ORC: Beyond Simple Review
While the primary aim of ORCs is to review potential business
opportunities, this goes beyond mere discussion. At its heart, the ORC
serves as a nexus for qualification. By pooling collective insights from
different departments, the ORC evaluates an opportunity worth pursuing,
identifies potential bottlenecks, and identifies a coherent strategy. It is not
just a touchpoint; it is a pivotal juncture in which decisions are made about
the viability of an approach towards an opportunity.

Setting the ORC Rhythm and Drawing the Right Minds
Two key elements need to be emphasised to harness the potential of ORCs:
consistent cadence and diverse expertise. A weekly rhythm is
recommended, ensuring that opportunities remain at the front and centre
and that evolving challenges or shifts are addressed in a timely manner.

The diversity in participation fortifies the ORC. Drawing representatives
from Sales, PreSales, Professional Service, Product Management, and
Development ensures a holistic understanding of the opportunity. Sales

offer insights into client dynamics; PreSales blends technical understanding
with solution positioning; Professional Service shares post-sale deployment
experiences; Product Management provides a lens into product capabilities
and future directions; and Development sheds light on technical feasibility
and possible customisations.

ORC Preparation: Setting the Stage
Efficiency in the Opportunity Review Call (ORC) is a function of preparation.
Before diving into the discussion, participants should have a clear agenda
and the Opportunity Solution Document (OSD). By sharing these a few days
in advance, attendees can reflect on the opportunity nuances, readying
themselves with insights, concerns, or potential strategies. Preparation
signals respect for participants’ time, fostering an environment of mutual
value and trust.

Tracking and Documenting: The ORC Backbone
While discussions fuel the ORC, tracking and documentation provide
structure and continuity. As diverse viewpoints converge, chronicle
discussions, decisions, and action steps are paramount. This written record
serves as a reference point for current opportunities and as a knowledge
reservoir for future opportunities.

Platforms such as Slack or Microsoft Teams can be potent allies. By
fashioning a dedicated ORC channel or group, dialogues, anchor action
points, relevant documents are circulated, and archives of previous
sessions are recorded. This evolving digital compendium aids information
continuity, knowledge sharing, and collaborative synergy.

The ORC Aftermath: Keeping the Momentum
The ORC conclusion is not the end but the commencement of a series of
action steps. It is essential to ensure that insights from the ORC translate
into tangible actions. This might range from deep diving into technical
complexities to aligning with Product Management on upcoming features
that could influence the opportunities trajectory.

The digital ORC channel, whether on Slack or Microsoft Teams, was pivotal
in this phase. Continuous collaboration, real-time updates, and

asynchronous discussions fostered by these platforms ensure that the
ORCs momentum is carried forward, keeping the opportunities pulse alive
and vibrant.

Structure of the ORC Call
Pre-Call Preparation
Opportunity Gathering: Collect all important opportunities.

Submission Requests: Allow team members to submit requests to the
moderation team to secure a spot on the call.

Prerequisites:
Opportunity Scoping Document (OSD): Ensure a filled-out OSD is shared
upfront.

Quick Overview: Include a brief overview of what will be discussed and who
needs to be present.

Call Structure
Action Items and To-Dos: Review the action items and tasks from the last
session.

Open Points Discussion: Address any unresolved points from the previous
meeting.

Opportunity Discussion: Typically, 2-3 opportunities will be discussed in
detail. Each opportunity is presented by a Solution Consultant using the
OSD. The presentation should include a summary of the opportunity, key
issues, and proposed solutions.

Moderation: A designated moderator leads the discussion. The moderator
is responsible for keeping the discussion on track, taking notes, and
capturing action items.

Action Items: Each opportunity should have clearly defined action items.
Assign ownership of each action item to a specific person. Ensure the next
steps and timelines are clearly outlined.

Post-Call Follow-Up: Send a summary of the meeting notes, including all
action items and their owners. Set deadlines for the next steps and ensure
they are followed up before the next call. By following this structured
approach, the opportunity review call can be effective and efficient,
ensuring that all opportunities are thoroughly discussed and actionable
steps are clearly defined.


### 9.5 After Functional & Technical Qualification – What

Next?
The Functional & Technical Qualification (FTQ) stage is undeniably vital,
laying the groundwork for future engagement. However, once that is
complete, the journey is not complete; in fact, it is just beginning. A
successful PreSales journey is akin to crafting a masterpiece, and
qualification is the initial sketch. The intricate details, nuances, and final
touches are yet to be added.

The Art of Demo Preparation
After qualification, the next natural step is to prepare for a demo. This is
not just about showcasing the features of the solution but also tailoring it
to resonate with client-specific challenges and aspirations. In this stage,
every slide, every click, and every example chosen should mirror the client’s
environment, reinforcing the idea that the proposed solution is not only
viable but also optimal. It is not just about the 'what' of the product but the
'why' of its relevance to the client. (Riefstahl 2011; Cohan 2023)

The Power of Demonstrations and Workshops
Then comes the moment of truth - the actual demo. Nevertheless, a demo
is not merely a passive presentation; it is also an interactive workshop. As
the solution unfolds on the screen, engaging the client, eliciting feedback,
answering queries, and addressing concerns in real-time is essential. These
sessions become collaborative think tanks, where the client and PreSales
professional come together to co-create a vision of what is possible. (Care
2020)

Crafting the Proposal
With the demo behind it, it is time to consolidate the understanding and
discussions into a tangible proposal. This is not just a document; it is a
testament to potential partnerships. The proposal intricately weaves
together the technical specifications of the solution, the nuances of
integration within the clients’ existing landscape, potential customisations,
and any foreseen challenges. The narrative tells the client, "We understand
your world, and here is how our solution fits seamlessly within it."

Resource Estimation: Setting Expectations Right
Any solution's success is not just about its technical prowess but also the
efficiency of its implementation. A significant aspect of this approach is
resource estimation. It is about painting a realistic picture of what it would
take to bring the proposed solution to life within the client’s ecosystem.
Whether it is the time required, the workforce involved, or the hardware
prerequisites, a clear estimate sets the right expectations, ensuring that
there are no unpleasant surprises.

Designing the Implementation Roadmap
Then comes the task of crafting a roadmap. It is one thing to promise a
solution and entirely another to showcase a clear, step-by-step plan to
make it a reality. An effective roadmap demystifies the journey ahead by
marking milestones, timelines, and deliverables. It becomes a reference
point, ensuring that the client and solution provider are in synchrony at
every step.

Sealing the Deal: The Closing
The culmination of the PreSales journey is the closing. However, closing is
not just about signatures on a contract; it is about reinforcing the
commitment to a shared vision. It is the moment when trust,
understanding, and collaboration promise to translate into tangible results.
(Raz 2017)

The Proof is in the Concept
While not always mandatory, proof of concept (PoC) becomes instrumental
in some scenarios. It is like a trailer for a movie, giving the client a glimpse

of what they expect. An effective PoC can allay concerns, reinforce the
efficacy of the solution, and set the stage for a full-fledged implementation.

Initiating the RFX Process
The RFX (Request for Information, Proposal, or Quotation) process is often
the next logical progression. It is a formalised approach to understanding
solution specifics, costs, and other relevant details. While it can seem
procedural, navigating the RFX process effectively can solidify the client’s
confidence in the solution and provider.

The Dance of Negotiation
Last but not least comes negotiations. In the world of B2B sales,
negotiations are less about compromise and more about collaboration. It
is about finding a sweet spot where the solution value and the client’s
investment align. A successful negotiation ensures that both parties see the
engagement as a win-win, setting the stage for a fruitful partnership. (Raz
2017)


> **Story**

Before we formalized our Functional Technical Qualification (FTQ) process
and instituted the Opportunity Review Calls (ORC), our sales pipeline was a
chaotic landscape filled with unqualified opportunities. Sales and their
managers were constantly trying to convince everyone that each new deal
was the golden ticket we needed to chase. They often bypassed the
necessary qualification steps and went directly to Product Management,
who was too accommodating to say no. This left us trapped between Sales,
who always said yes, and Product Management, who did the same.

As a result, we wasted thousands of hours each year conducting discovery
sessions, executing demos, and developing solutions in collaboration with
Product, only to discover that we didn’t have the capacity or tools to bring
these ideas to life. Our resources were stretched thin, and morale was low
because we were constantly chasing deals that never panned out.

Then, we introduced the FTQ process and ORC, and everything began to
change. The FTQ process brought structure and rigour to our qualification
steps. We could now objectively assess whether an opportunity fit within

our sweet spot and if we had the resources to deliver it. We stopped
pursuing deals that didn’t align with our strengths or strategic goals.

The ORCs added another layer of alignment. These regular calls became a
platform for Sales, Product Management, and Development to come
together and discuss opportunities based on facts, not just enthusiasm.
Decisions became more objective, and our messaging became consistent
and fact-based. This new alignment ensured that we only committed to
opportunities that we could realistically develop and deliver.

With these processes in place, our focus sharpened. We zeroed in on
opportunities that truly fit our capabilities, significantly reducing wasted
time and resources. The alignment between Sales, Product Management,
and Development became seamless, fostering a collaborative environment
where everyone was on the same page, strengthening our unity and shared
purpose.

Now, our sales process is much smoother. We have clear guidelines on
what opportunities to pursue and a structured approach to evaluate them.
This transformation has not only improved our efficiency but also built trust
within our teams and with our clients. We moved from a chaotic and
subjective approach to a structured and objective one, driving better
results and higher satisfaction for everyone involved.


**Key Takeaway:**

Qualification is the Foundation: The Technical & Functional Qualification
stage is crucial, but it's just the beginning. It sets the stage for the detailed
work that follows.

Demo Preparation as an Art: Preparing for a demo involves tailoring the
presentation to address client-specific challenges and aspirations,
highlighting not just the product features but also its relevance and value
to the client.

Interactive Demonstrations and Workshops: Effective demos are
interactive, engaging clients in real-time discussions and feedback,
transforming the session into a collaborative workshop.

Crafting Comprehensive Proposals: Proposals should reflect a deep
understanding of the client's environment, integrating technical
specifications, customizations, and addressing potential challenges to
showcase a seamless fit of the solution.

Resource Estimation: Clearly estimating the resources needed for
implementation ensures clients have realistic expectations, minimizing
surprises and fostering trust.

Implementation Roadmap: A well-designed roadmap with clear
milestones, timelines, and deliverables ensures both the client and
provider are aligned and can track progress effectively.

Closing the Deal with Commitment: Closing is about more than contracts;
it’s about reaffirming the shared vision and commitment to achieving
tangible results together.

Proof of Concept (PoC): PoCs can provide a preview of the solution’s
capabilities, addressing concerns and reinforcing confidence in the
proposed solution.

Navigating the RFX Process: Effectively managing the RFX process can
solidify client confidence, detailing solution specifics, costs, and other
essential information.

Collaborative Negotiations: Successful negotiations focus on
collaboration, ensuring both parties find value and view the engagement
as a win-win, paving the way for a strong partnership.


## 10. The Demo (Tell the Story)

The product demo is one of the most influential phases in the PreSales
process. It is the moment when the abstract becomes tangible, and
prospects see firsthand how your solution addresses their needs. However,
a demo is only as effective as the preparation behind it. This chapter details
the art and science behind crafting a compelling, targeted, and effective
product demo. (Cohan 2023)

When we talk about the product demo, we delve into a realm where
abstract concepts transform into practical applications right before the
eyes of the prospect. Consider the product demo as a bridge; on the one
side, you have the theories, features, and functionalities of a product. On
the other hand, you have real-world challenges, needs, and objectives for
potential clients. The demo serves as a pathway that connects these two
sides, ensuring that clients can precisely visualise how the product or
solution fits into their unique landscape.

Showing product features is not the only goal of demos. A truly effective
demo goes beyond just presenting it; it turns into an engaging
conversation. Unlike one-way lectures, a significant demonstration is
interactive. The audience is not just watching; they are involved in asking
questions, engaging, and imagining how the product can transform
operations or solve their key problems. This level of engagement is crucial.
This ensures that potential clients are not just passive viewers but also
active participants in discovering how the product can be a game changer.
(Cohan 2023; Riefstahl 2011)

Nevertheless, perhaps the most profound impact of a demo lies in its ability
to foster trust. In today’s digital era, where flashy marketing campaigns and
polished advertisements are omnipresent, scepticism has naturally found
its way into the decision-making process. Prospects often approach
solutions with a mixture of hopes and doubts. They hope the product can
address their challenges but harbour doubts based on past experiences,
competitor offerings, or internal constraints. (Hess 2020)

In this landscape, truly seeing is believing.

When a prospect sees a product working flawlessly in real-time, directly
addressing the specific challenges they have talked about, and
demonstrating its robust capabilities, they begin to see its true potential.
Each feature you show, every solution to a known problem, gradually
reduces any scepticism that they might have. It is like watching a master
magician unveiling the secrets behind the complex tricks. As the process
unfolds and product functionality becomes clear, any doubts turn into
comprehension. From there, comprehension develops into trust. (Care
2020; Carnegie 2010)

Moreover, the undercurrent of empathy runs through successful
demonstrations. It is not just about showcasing a product’s features but
also demonstrating an understanding of the prospective challenges. This
signifies that the PreSales professional is not merely selling but offers a
genuine solution to a problem. This subtle yet powerful message amplifies
the trust-building process. (Cohan 2023)

Furthermore, the modern product demo capitalises on the human desire
for stories. Instead of a disjoint display of features, the demo becomes a
narrative. It starts with a depiction of common challenges, progresses into
the introduction of a hero (the product), showcases the journey of
overcoming obstacles, and culminates in a successful resolution. Moreover,
like any captivating story, it resonates, remains in memory, and compels
action. (Brian 2006)

The product demo in PreSales is not just a simple step; it is a major
milestone. It is where visualising the product, engaging the client, and
building trust all come together. This process is key to shaping perceptions,
swaying decisions, and laying the groundwork for successful partnerships.
For PreSales professionals, becoming adept at both the art and science of
the Demo is essential. In this brief demo period, you are doing more than
just showing off a product; you are actively shaping the future of a business
relationship. (Hess 2020; Greene 2018)


**Key Takeaway:**

Visualisation: This converts concepts and features into real-world
applications.

Engagement: A well-conducted demo draws on the audience, creating an
interactive session rather than a monologue.

Trust-building: Seeing a product in action can assuage doubts and build
confidence in its capabilities.


### 10.1 The Science behind the Demo


#### 10.1.1 Limbic Persona-Based Selling: Navigating the Emotional

Labyrinths of the Client Brain
One method that has been shown to be incredibly effective in connecting
with clients is limbic persona-based selling. However, before diving deeply,
let us demystify what it means.

The limbic system, an ancient part of our brain, is responsible for many of
our emotions, motivations, and decision-making processes. Historically,
this has allowed us to quickly assess survival situations. Fast forward to
today, it influences our purchasing decisions, often without us realising it.
Even in today’s data-driven environment, emotions remain at the core of
every client decision. (Conn 2003)

Now, picture being able to directly connect with that system and resonate
with the deeply rooted emotions of your potential clients. This is where the
limbic persona-based approach shines. It combines a deep understanding
of the target audience's personas with emotionally charged visuals and
narratives, aiming to target the limbic system. (Carnegie 2010; Greene
2018)

The first step in this approach is to understand and define the personas.
Think of personas as semifictional representations of ideal clients. The
personas are based on both market research and real data about existing
clients, encompassing demographics, behaviours, motivations, and goals.
Crafting these personas requires a mixture of research, interviews, and
sometimes intuition. (Keenan 2018)

Having a persona is only the starting point. True magic lies in
communicating with these personas in a way that strikes an emotional
chord. Moreover, our focus shifts to creating compelling and emotionally
charged visuals.

Visual content has the power to communicate complex ideas quickly and
efficiently. For instance, the image of a calm sea can immediately bring
about feelings of peace and calm. However, a storm can evoke a sense of
urgency or danger. Using visuals that resonate with your personas'
challenges or goals directly speaks to their emotions. Employing metaphors
strengthened this effect. For example, if the software provides top-notch
security, using an image of a fortress or shield could be highly effective.
(Conn 2003)

However, visuals alone are not enough. One must be woven into a
compelling narrative or story around each persona. Storytelling is an age-
old art form, and its power in sales and marketing is unmatched. It is one
thing to tell your prospective client that your product will help their
business grow; it is entirely another to narrate a story of how a similar
company faced challenges and, with the help of your product, overcome
them to achieve remarkable success. Complemented by powerful visuals,
such stories make a product or service more relatable, memorable, and
engaging. (Keenan 2018; Hess 2020)

Another subtle yet potent aspect to consider is the use of limbic triggers,
such as colours and patterns. The brain, especially the limbic system, reacts
differently to various colours. Blue, often associated with trust and
calmness, can be used to explain features that ensure data safety. Red,
which signifies urgency, might be appropriate when discussing a problem
that requires an immediate solution.

A practical application of Limbic Persona-Based Selling can be seen in the
interactive demos. Tailor the flow and content of the demo based on the
persona you are addressing. Engage the audience by making them step into
the persona’s shoes. Let them 'feel' the challenges and visualise the
solutions through curated visuals. (Rosenberg 2015; Roam 2016)

Of course, the effectiveness of this approach needs to be validated and
refined. It is crucial to gather feedback after a sales pitch or presentation.
Did the visuals resonate? Was the story compelling? Did it evoke the
desired emotions? Such insights can help to continually improve your
approach, ensuring that it remains impactful.

Training becomes the linchpin for the widespread and consistent
application of this method. Your sales and marketing teams need to
internalise the principles behind Limbic Persona-Based Selling. Only with a
deep understanding can one adapt and utilise it effectively in various real-
time situations.

Implementing Limbic Persona-Based Selling: A Step-by-Step Guide
The most successful PreSales professionals realise that it is not just about
showing a product or service but truly connecting with the prospect. This is
where the revolutionary approach to Limbic Persona-Based Selling comes
into play. By diving deep into the emotional labyrinths of the client’s brain,
this technique offers a refreshing perspective on the sales strategy.

How do we traverse this emotional labyrinth? Let us embark on a journey
to understand and implement this strategy step by step.

Deep down, many of our decisions, particularly complex ones, originate
from an emotional and instinctual part of our brain known as the limbic
system. This system, which is tied to our emotions and memories, has
evolved over time but continues to play a subtle yet significant role in how
we make choices today. Limbic Persona-Based Selling recognises this fact.
It targets this fundamental decision-making centre by combining scientific
insights with the art of selling. By addressing this core emotional part of our
brains, we can connect more effectively with clients on a deeper and more
intuitive level. (Greene 2018)

The Genesis: Crafting the Personas
Before we aim for the limbic system, we must ask, "Whose limbic system
are we targeting?" This brings us to the cornerstone of our strategy, the
personas. Personas are nuanced, detailed representations of segments of
your target audience. However, these are not merely demographic cutouts.
They are living and breathing profiles imbued with motivations, pain points,
goals, and behaviours. Every successful strategy begins with understanding
its audience and in this case, by crafting detailed personas.

Creating a persona is similar to piecing a jigsaw puzzle together. It begins
with market research and layering of demographic data with behavioural
insights. Client interviews, surveys, and feedback sessions were invaluable.

Interactions with client-facing teams, such as sales, support, and services,
can provide golden nuggets of information. Over time, these fragments
merge, revealing a detailed picture of a typical client or user. (Hess 2020)

Speaking the Limbic Language: The Power of Visuals
With personas in hand, the next challenge is to communicate with them in
a language that resonates deeply. The universal language of emotions is
often visual. Images, graphics, and animations: They have a supernatural
ability to bypass layers of cognitive processing and strike directly at the
heart of emotion.

Consider this: a long textual description of a serene beach might tell you
about its beauty, but a single image can transport you there, making you
almost hear the waves and feel the breeze. In other words, the power
visuals command. (Conn 2003; Keenan 2018)

In the world of Limbic Persona-Based Selling, visuals are not merely
decorative elements. These are strategic tools that are carefully chosen to
evoke specific emotions in alignment with the personas’ pain points or
aspirations. For example, for a persona struggling with data management,
an image of tangled threads unravelling into a straight line could symbolise
the chaos-to-clarity transformation offered by the solution.

Narrating the Tale: Storytelling with Precision
While visuals strike an emotional chord, stories give context and direction.
Storytelling is not a new sales tool. However, in the context of Limbic
Persona-Based Selling, it plays a specialised role.

Every story is tailored to a specific person. It unfolds in a narrative arc that
starts with the personas’ challenges, explores their aspirations, and
concludes with the resolution offered by the product. The storytelling
journey comprises distinct phases.

Introduction to the Challenge: This is where the story begins by presenting
the personas’ current struggles or pain points.

Development of Aspirations: In this phase, the story delves into what the
persona aspires to achieve, showcasing their hopes and dreams.

Climax with Product Resolution: The story reaches its peak by introducing
your product as a solution that bridges the gap between personas’
challenges and their aspirations.

Recall that a good story is not just a collection of facts; it is an emotional
journey. It taps into the feelings associated with each phase: the anxiety of
facing a challenge, hope ignited by the potential for change, and joy of
finding a solution. Through these stories, you are not merely selling a
product; you are guiding your clients on a transformative journey. (Greene
2018)

Subtleties and Nuances: Limbic Triggers
Beyond the obvious tools of visuals and stories lie subtle cues and limbic
triggers. For instance, colours have specific emotional resonances. Blue
may evoke feelings of trust and calmness, perfect for a solution that offers
security. By contrast, red could signal urgency or importance, apt to
highlight critical challenges or standout features. Patterns, design
structures, and even sounds (if permitted by the medium) can also play
pivotal roles in moulding the emotional landscape of the pitch. (Greene
2018)

Into the Field: Practical Implementation
Theory and strategy, when not well implemented, remain just that theory.
The real magic of Limbic Persona-Based Selling unfolds in real-time
interactions. Tailored demos become playgrounds where personas, visuals,
stories, and limbic triggers come together. The aim? To offer a prospect not
just a look at a product but also an experience.

It is essential to be adaptable during these demos. While you enter armed
with persona-specific content, real-time feedback, verbal cues, or even
body language might necessitate on-the-fly adjustments. It is a dance, after
all, and sometimes the rhythm changes unexpectedly.

Potential Hiccups: Treading with Caution
Human decision-making is not driven only by emotions and the limbic
system; it is a mix of both emotion and logical thinking. Imagine this as a
play-on stage. The limbic system, or our emotional side, might play a major

role in receiving most of our attention. But other characteristics are just as
important. These represent logic, past experiences, what society expects,
etc. Each plays a part of the entire story. As in well-balanced theatre
productions, focusing too much on one character can throw off the entire
play.

In the world of sales, this translates into an over-reliance on the limbic
persona to the exclusion of logical considerations. For instance, if a client
primarily identifies as an “Explorer” driven by novelty and unique
experiences, focusing solely on these emotional drivers might be tempting.
However, in your role as a business executive, the same client may also
have budget constraints, logistical concerns, or corporate guidelines to
consider. These elements, governed more by rational thought processes,
cannot be ignored. Addressing only the emotional and ignoring the rational
might resonate on a personal level but may fail to tick the necessary
checkboxes on the professional front.

Another pitfall that PreSales professionals might stumble upon is the trap
of over-generalisation. While limbic personas provide a structured
framework for understanding and categorising clients, humans are
nuanced, multifaceted creatures. A single client could exhibit traits from
multiple personas, depending on the context, mood, or even time of day.
Grouping them strictly into one category can lead to oversimplification and
may even come across as tone-deaf. (Hess 2020)

The effectiveness of limbic persona-based selling depends largely on
authenticity. In an era where information is everywhere, clients are more
perceptive and can quickly differentiate real engagement from
manipulative strategies. If a product is forced into a client’s emotional story
without a true fit, it can become fake. Even worse, any hint of manipulation
or dishonesty can instantly destroy the trust that you have worked hard to
build. In sales, trust is like gold. Once lost, it is extremely difficult, and
sometimes even impossible, to get it back.

Understanding the emotional underpinnings of a client's decision-making
process is a powerful asset. However, like any tool, wielders’ skill,
discretion, and integrity determine the outcome. Knowing when to play up
emotional chords, when to strike a balance with logic, and when to step
back and re-evaluate is vital.


#### 10.1.2 Visual Picture-based Selling

“A picture is worth 1000 words” Fred R. Barnard

As a PreSales consultant, understanding the nuances of client interaction is
crucial. More than just being a mediator between the product and the
potential client, the PreSales role is concerned with effectively
communicating value. Furthermore, the central aspect of this
communication is visual picture-based selling. Dive into this chapter to
comprehend the significance of visual storytelling and how it can amplify
sales strategy. (Greene 2018; Brian 2006)

Leveraging Visuals and Limbic Selling
From the vivid tapestries of ancient caves to the digital billboards of today’s
metropolises, conveying complex ideas through visuals remains
unparalleled. This predilection is not just cultural; it is wired into our very
neurobiology. Our brain, a marvel of evolution, has developed specific
pathways that prioritise and fast-track the processing of visual information.

Picture: On your busy day, walking down a crowded street, you notice a
bright billboard. It could be advertising the newest phone or trendy
clothing. Even if you are too far from reading the words, the picture grabs
your attention. This is how strong visuals are. Research in areas such as
brain science and advertising shows that we prefer pictures over text. A
complex paragraph might take minutes to understand, but a single image
or chart can provide the same information instantly.

What makes these visuals impactful? Why does the brain latch onto them
with passion? This is where the limbic system enters the spotlight. (Conn
2003)

Deep within the recesses of our brain lies in the limbic system, often hailed
as the emotional epicentre. It is a complex network that governs feelings,
moods, and emotions. Whether it is the flutter of joy from a compliment or
the pang of sadness from a memory, the limbic system is orchestrating
these responses. While its role in our daily lives is vast and multifaceted, its
significance in the context of sales is monumental. (Conn 2003)

Limbic selling focuses on engaging the limbic system that governs
emotions. It involves creating messages that appeal to both the logical and
emotional parts of the brain. When we talk about a product, service, or
solution in PreSales, it is more than just listing the features or data. We are
telling a story, triggering emotions, and building connections. As the limbic
system is closely linked to emotional responses, it plays a key role in this
process.

How does one marry the world of visuals with limbic sales? The answer lies
in understanding the essence of both concepts. Because our brain
processes visual information at breakneck speed, we must ensure that the
visuals we present in our sales pitches or presentations strike a chord with
the audience’s emotional centres. This does not mean manipulating
emotions but engaging with them genuinely and authentically. (Greene
2018)

For instance, consider presenting a cybersecurity solution. Instead of
simply showcasing graphs about its efficiency or listing its features, imagine
using visuals that depict a shield or fortress. These images, while simple,
evoke feelings of safety, security, and protection. You resonate with the
limbic system’s innate desire for safety. Such a visual narrative does not
simply share product details; it tells a story, connects on an emotional level,
and leaves a lasting impression.

Therefore, the art of PreSales has become a delicate balancing act. On the
one hand, you have a compelling world of visuals with the ability to simplify,
engage, and convey. However, there is a limbic system, with its vast
emotional landscape waiting to be explored. By intertwining these two and
by understanding the profound interplay between sight and emotion,
PreSales professionals can elevate their selling strategies.

Power of Visuals: Tapping into the Brains Preference
A Natural Bias: The age-old adage, "A picture is worth a thousand words,"
has profound roots in our neurobiology. Studies indicate that the human
brain can process images almost 60,000 times faster than text. As PreSales
consultants, leveraging this natural bias can be a game-changer.

Retain and Recall: Not only do we process visuals faster, but we also retain
them better. Think back to the recent advertisement or presentation that
you saw. Chances are that you remember the visuals more than the words.
Utilising this for product or solution presentations can significantly enhance
the recall during the decision-making process.


#### 10.1.3 Storytelling: Narrating a Relatable Journey

The real power of storytelling is its ability to capture and engage objects. It
is more than a series of events or a collection of facts. It connects emotions,
experiences, and aspirations. Reflecting on this – when did a mere data
point last excite you, or a basic product feature inspire you to dream?
Stories weave characters, challenges, and resolutions in a way that can truly
enhance. They have the ability to make abstract concepts real, bring distant
ideas close, and turn ordinary things into extraordinary things.

However, as PreSales professionals, the task is not merely to tell a story but
also to tell the right story. It begins with an intimate understanding of the
audience. Every potential client or client has a backdrop – a context that
shapes their perceptions, decisions, and reactions. This is where the craft
of creating detailed personas comes into play. (Rosenberg 2015; Riefstahl
2011)

Imagine you are in a theatre, and before the play begins, you can know
every audience member – their likes, dislikes, past experiences, aspirations,
and fears. Armed with this knowledge, would not the play be crafted
differently, resonating deeply with each individual? This is precisely the
advantage that personas offer.

Creating personas is similar to sketching characters in a novel. These are
not generic profiles but intricate delineations of potential clients. It is about
understanding the daily challenges, the aspirations that keep them awake
at night, the fears that occasionally shadow their decisions, and the desires
that drive their actions. Such profound insights make the subsequent story
relatable and grounded.

Now, the stage is set with these personas sketched, and the challenges are
outlined. Enter the protagonist: Your product or solution. In the grand
narrative, your product is not just an inanimate object or a list of features;

it is also the hero. It is the beacon of hope in the client’s journey, the
solution to their pressing challenges, and the bridge to their aspirations.

For instance, if you are selling a cloud-based data storage solution, a
straightforward approach might be to list its features– storage capacity,
security protocols, and accessibility. However, the narrative is different in
the world of storytelling. You would narrate the tale of a company, perhaps
one very similar to your potential client. This company, overwhelmed by
growing data and struggling with outdated storage systems, is facing a
challenge. Their operations are hampered, their efficiency is dwindling, and
they are searching for a saviour. Enter your product, address each
challenge, streamline operations, and catalyse growth. The value
proposition is clear at the storage end, and the products placed in the
potential client ecosystem are evident. If you ask yourself now, how can I
know this? Execute the Discovery.

This storytelling approach involves multiple aspects. First, it humanises the
product, making it more than just a tool; it becomes an ally. Second, it
contextualises a product, showcasing its value in real-world scenarios.
Finally, this fosters a connection. When potential clients see their
reflections in the story when they can relate to the challenges and envisage
the resolutions, the engagement is no longer transactional; it is emotional.


**Key Takeaway:**

The Magic of Stories: From ancient campfires to modern marketing
campaigns, storytelling remains a potent tool. Stories are naturally
engaging, weaving together sequences in memorable patterns. When a
potential client hears a story, they are more likely to empathise, relate, and
remember.

Crafting Personas and Challenges: Before building a story, understanding
the audience is vital. Create detailed personas for potential clients.
Understanding their challenges, desires, fears, and aspirations. This made
the story more relatable and grounded.

Your Product as the Hero: With challenges outlined for every persona,
introduce your product or solution as the hero of the story. This positioning

not only makes your product memorable but also showcases its value
proposition in a real-world context.

Why Storytelling Matters in Demos
However, amidst this forest of data points, one ancient art form has stood
the test of time as a powerful instrument of persuasion: storytelling. At first
glance, the idea of weaving tales into product demos may seem antiquated,
even out of place. However, when you delve deeper, you find that stories
have always been our primary mode of communication, our bridge to
understanding complex ideas, and our torchbearers of memory.

Crafting an Emotional Connection: The Heartbeat of Relatability
Long before our ancestors painted on cave walls or wrote on papyrus, they
told them stories. Around campfires, beneath the canopy of stars, they
shared tales of hunts, gods, dreams, and despair. Why? Stories are their
way of making sense of the world, sharing experiences, and passing down
knowledge. At its core, storytelling is an endeavour of the heart, reaching
out to touch another, saying, "I see you. I understand." (Greene 2018)

Fast forward to a modern-day product demo, and its essence remains
unchanged. When you, as a PreSales professional, craft a narrative around
a demo, you are not just talking about a product; you are painting a picture
of the challenges, solutions, aspirations, and achievements. It is not about
the latest software features; it is about Alex, the middle manager drowning
in paperwork, suddenly finding a lifeline in an automation tool. It is about
Clara, the young entrepreneur, seeing her dreams take flight with the aid
of cutting-edge analytics.

By embedding the product within these stories, the demo transforms from
a mere presentation to a shared journey. The audience does not just see a
tool; they see themselves, their challenges, and their potential triumph.
This emotional connection, the heartbeat of relatability, elevates the demo
from informative to impactful. (Hess 2020)

The Thread of Memory: Stories as Beacons of Retention
We have all experienced this: a presentation overloaded with facts, charts,
and graphs. Often, all the data blend together, leaving us with only bits and

pieces to remember. Now, consider a story – a narrative of triumph,
overcoming challenges, or a straightforward solution to a long-standing
issue. Which of these is more memorable?

Scientific research has consistently shown that our brains are wired to
remember stories better than to remember isolated facts. A well-told
narrative engages multiple parts of the brain, from regions responsible for
processing language to evoking emotions. This multifaceted engagement
creates a richer and deeper memory imprint. (Greene 2018)

In the context of a demo, this means that while your audience might not
remember every feature or statistic, they will remember the story in which
these elements are embedded. You will recall Alex’s relief or Claras’
excitement and, by extension, the product that made those emotions
possible. In a sea of data, stories are the lighthouses guiding ships of
memory.

The Magnetic Pull: Stories as Catalysts of Engagement
In an age when attention is a fleeting resource, grabbing and holding an
audience’s interest is invaluable. Stories act like alchemists in this context,
transforming ordinary presentations into fascinating experiences.
Remember the enchantment of childhood bedtime stories? How do you get
lost in tales of distant lands, heroes, and villains, with the rest of the world
just fading into the background? This level of engagement and absorption
shows the true essence and power of storytelling. (Riefstahl 2011)

PreSales professionals can tap this primal force by infusing demos with
narratives. The audience is no longer a passive recipient of information;
they are active participants in the story. Their curiosity is stimulated: How
will the tale unfold? Will the protagonist overcome these challenges? What
role does the product play in this journey? This engagement ensures that
not only is the message heard but also absorbed and reflected upon.
(Cohan 2023)


> **Story**

We have encountered a prospect in the aerospace industry. Our discovery
process revealed a significant match between their needs and our
solutions. However, the challenge was the competitors’ strong connections

with the company. We had a Solution Consultant on our team who had
successfully sold to a similar client in the past and had extensive industry
knowledge. Recognising the importance of this opportunity, we decided to
bring our 'A-Team' to the forefront. Midway through the sales cycle, we
introduced this seasoned Solution Consultant. His impact was immediate
and profound. He not only knew the industry but also connected our
prospect's needs to the success story of the other client. He even facilitated
the calls between them. Despite our competitors’ better connections and
access to higher management, it was our ability to tell a compelling story
and demonstrate proven success that ultimately led the prospect to choose
us.


**Key Takeaway:**

Creating Relatability: Humans are hardwired to resonate with stories. You
craft an emotional connection by weaving a narrative around the demo.

Enhancing Retention: People remember stories better than isolated facts
or figures. A compelling narrative can enhance the recall.

Driving Engagement: Stories can capture the audience, ensuring attention
throughout the demo.


#### 10.1.4 Incorporating Emotion into the Demo

At the heart of every business decision is a human element – an emotional
core that, when tapped into, can significantly amplify the impact of a
demonstration. (Rosenberg 2015)

The Inherent Human Aspect
Imagine that you are watching a film. Two versions of the same storyline
were presented to you. The first is a list of events, and the second is
brimming with characters, challenges, aspirations, defeats, and triumphs.
Which version resonates more profoundly? Naturally, the latter. It is not
the events in isolation that captivate us but the human narratives that
thread through them.

Likewise, when discussing a product or service, understanding its technical
specifications is essential, but the human element truly resonates. Sharing
stories of individuals or organisations that have tangibly benefited from the

product can be instrumental. These tales are not just endorsements; they
enable potential clients to envision themselves in those success stories. The
gap between a product’s features and its real-world impact is bridged. You
answer every prospect's fundamental question: "What can this do for me?"

The Power of Empathy
Understanding is not only about recognising clients’ needs. This is about
resonating deeply with their challenges, frustrations, and aspirations.
When you start a demo, you are not merely introducing a product but
stepping into the clients’ world, fraught with specific challenges and pain
points.

Empathy is the compass that guides you through this terrain. By showcasing
a genuine understanding of the potential client hurdles, you are not just
building trust; you are setting the stage for the solution that your product
offers. This empathetic approach does not reinforce pain points but
highlights the relief, efficiency, or growth your product can usher in. When
potential clients feel understood, they are more receptive to the solutions
that you present. (Rosenberg 2015; Greene 2018)

The Ripple Effect of Enthusiasm
There is a magnetic pull to real passion. When someone demonstrating a
product is truly enthusiastic, it is not just contagious; it also sends a
powerful message. It subtly suggests, 'If they are this excited about the
product, maybe it is worth my full attention.' Your belief in a product subtly
but effectively highlights its value. (Raz 2017)

True beliefs about a product cannot be faked. It comes from a deep
understanding of the product and a sincere conviction of its benefits. This
genuine enthusiasm, combined with our natural attraction for passionate
displays, creates an irresistible environment. It is a subtle encouragement
for the potential client to engage more deeply, listen more closely, and start
imagining what could be possible with the product. (Greene 2018)

Crafting an Emotional Arc
Integrating emotion into a demo does not imply transforming it into a
theatrical display. It is about constructing an emotional arc — starting with

understanding the clients’ world, introducing the product as a beacon of
change, and talking with genuine enthusiasm about its potential impact.
This arc ensures that, while the client learns about the technicalities of the
products, they simultaneously experience emotional resonance.


**Key Takeaway:**

Human Element: Share stories of individuals or organisations that have
benefited from the product.

Empathy: Show a genuine understanding of the challenges faced by the
potential client and highlight how your product addresses these challenges.

Enthusiasm: The enthusiasm of a person to demonstrate a product can be
catching. If you genuinely believe in the value of the product, your audience
is more likely to believe in it, too.


#### 10.1.5 Engagement Strategies

Engagement is not just a buzzword but a foundation for successful client
interactions. Even if one has the most revolutionary product or service,
one’s message can be lost or forgotten without effective engagement. This
chapter provides PreSales consultants with comprehensive strategies to
enhance engagement during demos and presentations.

Interaction: Nurturing a Two-Way Conversation
A monologue, regardless of how informative, can become monotonous.
Interaction breaks this monotony, creating a collaborative space where
both you and the audience actively participate. (Riefstahl 2011)

In the ever-evolving world of PreSales, professionals are often armed with
a plethora of information ready to present to potential clients. However,
the art of engagement goes far beyond its capacity to disseminate data.
True engagement lies in the dance of interaction, where both parties are
not just present but are actively involved in the discourse. When we
nurture this dynamic, we unlock the realm of mutual understanding,
deeper insights, and more authentic connections.

Imagine attending a theatrical play. If the actor on stage, despite delivering
a stellar performance, never acknowledges the audience’s presence, the

overall experience may feel somewhat lacking. Now, juxtapose it with a
play where the performer occasionally breaks the fourth wall, making
direct eye contact or even engaging in a brief banter with someone in the
first row. Although unconventional, the latter scenario fosters a unique
bond between the performer and the audience. Similarly, in the arena of
PreSales, shifting from a monologue to dialogue can have transformative
effects.

At the heart of this interaction lies the power of the questions. As
consultants, it is tempting to stay in the driver’s seat, steering the
conversation direction from start to end. However, a new dynamic is
created by periodically pausing to pose questions to the audience. It is
essential to clarify that the objective is not to interrogate or test the
audience’s knowledge. Instead, it is an invitation that encourages them to
pause, reflect, and dive deeper into the topic at hand. Such questions can
range from seeking personal experiences related to the topic to asking their
opinions or even prompting them to envision potential scenarios. These
questions serve as gentle nudges, ensuring that the audience remains
physically present, mentally, and emotionally engaged.

While proactively posing questions is invaluable, seeking feedback is
equally crucial. Often, there is a tendency to reserve this segment for the
end of a presentation, treating it almost as an afterthought. However, there
is immense merit in interspersing feedback sessions throughout. This can
be as simple as asking, "I pause here for a second. Do you have any
questions?" or "Would you like me to delve deeper into this area?" Such
check-ins serve dual purposes. For the audience, it is an assurance that their
understanding and perspective are valued. For PreSales professionals, it is
a goldmine of insights, providing real-time feedback about presentation
pacing, depth, and relevance. More importantly, it offers the opportunity
to pivot or adjust the approach, ensuring that the content remains resonant
and engaging for the audience. (Riefstahl 2011; Cohan 2023)

The interaction is a two-way process. While it is important to ask questions
and seek feedback, the key is active listening. In our rush to share
information or answer questions, we sometimes forget to really listen.
Active listening involves more than just hearing words; it is about
understanding the emotions and meanings behind them. When a client

asks a question or gives feedback, genuinely listening to them can change
the entire dynamic. This leads to better responses and shows the clients
that their views are respected and valued. Also, check your pace and adjust
it as needed.


**Key Takeaway:**

The Power of Questions: Proactively ask your audience questions. It is not
about testing their knowledge but encouraging them to think and reflect.
It is a subtle nudge reminding them to remain present and engaged.

Seek Feedback Regularly: Instead of waiting until the end of your
presentation, ask for feedback intermittently. This provides insights into
the audience’s perspective and allows for real-time adjustments to the
approach.

Active Listening: Interaction is not just about speaking; it is equally about
listening. Listen attentively when a client or potential client provides input
or asks questions. This not only helps in addressing the queries effectively
but also demonstrates respect and value for their perspective.

Pacing: The Rhythmic Dance of Engagement
One aspect that is often overlooked despite its profound impact is pacing.
The speed at which we relay information, the rhythm we follow, and the
cadence we adopt can transform an ordinary presentation into an
extraordinary one. Let us deeply understand this dance of engagement and
explore how pacing can make or break the effectiveness of our demos and
presentations.

Imagine, for a moment, attending a concert. The lights dim, and the music
begins. Surprisingly, however, the entire performance adheres to a singular
tempo. Be it crescendos or subtle lulls, there is no variation. Every note,
chord, and beat maintains the same speed. Such an experience, despite the
brilliance of the musicians, can quickly become monotonous, causing your
mind to wander and your interest to wane. Now, contrast this with the
symphony that ebbs and flows, rising to exhilarating highs and then softly
whispering, inviting listeners to lean in. This dynamic journey captivates the
audience, ensuring that they remain hooked from start to finish.

Our demos and presentations can be visualised as compositions by
translating this musical analogy into the context of PreSales. To sustain
engagement and ensure information retention, it is imperative to master
the art of pacing.

Let us begin with the fast-paced segments. These are akin to the upbeat,
energetic tracks that make listeners sit up and take notice. In a
presentation, such segments are invaluable, particularly when introducing
a new section or showcasing the key highlights of a product or solution.
Imagine that you are unveiling the groundbreaking features of your
software. Launching this section briskly can serve as a drumroll of sorts,
signalling that the audience that is about to be shared is paramount. This
lively tempo captures their attention, ensuring they are all ears and eagerly
anticipating the information you are about to relay. (Greene 2018)

Similar to a music concert that balances high-energy songs with slower
melodies, our presentations also require moments of calm. This is where
detailed and slower explanations have been proposed. After the
excitement of a fast-paced section, shifting to a slower and more
thoughtful pace provides a chance to breathe. During these moments, we
invite the audience to dive deeper to grasp the complexities and subtleties
of the topic. This approach is particularly useful for explaining intricate and
technical information. By breaking it down and giving examples, you can
help the audience not only understand but also remember the information.
This balance ensures that the audience is excited by the highlights and fully
comprehends the complete picture of what you are presenting.

However, while the interplay between the fast and slow segments is
essential, the transitions truly orchestrate this rhythmic dance. Think of
these as bridges in a song that seamlessly connects one verse to the next.
In the world of PreSales, ensuring smooth transitions between different
pacing segments can create a world of difference. A sudden change in
tempo can be jarring, potentially disrupting the flow and disorienting the
audience. To mitigate this problem, transitional statements or visuals can
be used as game changers. They act as signposts, guide the audience,
prepare them for what is to come, and ensure that they remain on the
journey with you.


**Key Takeaway:**

Fast-Paced Segments: These segments are perfect for introducing new
sections or showcasing highlights. Brisk pace captures attention and
signifies the importance of the points being discussed.

Slower, Detailed Explanations: After a fast-paced segment, slowing down
to delve into details can provide depth. This change in pace allows the
audience to digest complex information, ensuring that they understand
and retain it.

Mindful Transitions: Ensure smooth transitions between different pacing
segments. A sudden change can be jarring; therefore, transitional
statements or visuals can be used to guide the audience through pace
changes.

Anchoring: Creating Memorable Moments
To understand anchoring, imagine attending seminars. Among the myriad
slides and the influx of data, a singular moment remains with you. It can be
an image, phrase, or even a gesture by the presenter. Days or even weeks
later, when you recollect the workshop, that particular moment stands out,
acting as a touchstone for recalling the broader message of the event. That
is, in essence, the power of anchoring: the ability to create memorable
moments that serve as reference points, enabling easier recall and deeper
understanding of complex topics. (Keenan 2018)

The world that we navigate today is visual-centric. Images, graphics, videos,
and animations surround us, shaping our perceptions and influencing our
decisions. In the PreSales domain, leveraging strong visuals can create a
world of differences. Consider the following example. Suppose you are
presenting a software solution that addresses the challenges of online data
breaches. While you could delve into statistics and technicalities, an
evocative image of a padlock unlocked with digital streams flowing out can
serve as a powerful anchor. It not only conveys the gravity of the problem
but does so in a manner that is instantly relatable and memorable. Such
visuals, when aligned with the core message, act as anchors, ensuring that
the idea you represent remains etched in the audience’s minds.

However, anchoring does not rely solely on visuals. When harnessed
effectively, the power of words can be equally impactful. Crafting emotive
statements is the art itself. These phrases encapsulate the essence of an
idea, delivering it in a succinct yet powerful manner. Consider the phrase,
"A stitch in time saves nine." This age-old adage, while simple, conveys the
importance of timely actions. Similarly, in PreSales, statements that
resonate with the product or solution being presented can serve as
anchors. For instance, if presenting a time-management tool, the
statement "Time saved today is productivity earned tomorrow" can be an
anchor that underscores the value proposition of the tools.

Introducing a visual anchor or key phrase is insufficient. Our memory can
be selective; therefore, to solidify a point, it is vital to revisit it regularly.
Think of it like nurturing a young plant. For it to grow, consistent care is
required. Similarly, to ensure that an anchor firmly sets in the audience’s
memory, it needs to be revisited and reiterated. Strategically looping back
to these key points during your presentation does more than highlight their
importance; it builds a mental map for the audience. Each return to these
points acts as reinforcement, ensuring that by the end of your talk, these
key ideas are not just understood but also remembered.


**Key Takeaway:**

Strong Visuals: Use impactful visuals that resonate with your message. For
instance, if you are discussing a problem, your product solves it, and an
evocative image illustrating the problem can make it more memorable.
(Greene 2018)

Emotive Statements: Sometimes, a well-phrased statement can leave a
lasting impression. Craft statements encapsulate the essence of your
points, making it easier for the audience to recall. (Rosenberg 2015)

Reiteration: Reinforce an anchored point and circle it back during your
presentation. By revisiting important points, you emphasise their
significance, ensuring that they remain in the minds of the audience.


#### 10.1.6 Understanding the Psychology of Attention

Handling the complexities of human attention is a key challenge in PreSales,
in which the success of each interaction can shape the final result. In

presentations or demos, 'attention' means more than just having your
audience watch and listen. It is about making sure that your message is not
only heard but also remembered and influences action.

Today, our modern digital environment is filled with distractions towards
the brim. From incessant notifications on our devices to a constant influx
of information from all sides, attention span has declined. PreSales
consultants need to be concise and impactful. Every second of the
presentation is precious. One cannot afford to have a sluggish start.
Starting strong is paramount, whether it is an eye-catching statistic, a
thought-provoking question, or an anecdote to which the audience can
relate. (Greene 2018)

While capturing attention at the beginning is vital, maintaining it
throughout is an art. This requires an understanding of the human nature.
We are naturally drawn to new things, a trait embedded in our brains from
ancient times, to alert us to changes in our environment. In PreSales, this
tendency can be used for our advantage. If a presentation is too
predictable, the audience’s mind may start wandering. To keep them
engaged, we introduced unexpected elements. This could be an interactive
part, surprising fact, or relevant video. These novel components act like
anchors, pulling the attention of anyone who might be starting to lose
focus. (Brian 2006)

Nevertheless, beyond novelty, personal gain is an even more potent driver
