of attention. Each individual in your audience is subconsciously asking,
"What is in it for me?" Tapping into this can work wonders. Instead of
making your audience wait, lay out the value proposition immediately. This
approach primes them to pay attention, eager to see how the product or
solution you are showcasing fulfils the promised benefits. Moreover, this
proposition should not be a one-size-fits-all statement. Personalising it to
resonate with your specific audience’s needs and desires will make it much
more effective. (Keenan 2018; Cohan 2023)

While the content and structure of your presentation play vital roles, we
must not overlook the external factors. The environment in which the
presentation or demo is conducted can create or break the audience’s
attention span. Think about it — a room that is too cold or too hot,
uncomfortable seating arrangements, poor lighting, or even the wrong

time of day can adversely affect attentiveness. Ensuring that the audience
is comfortable is the first step towards ensuring engagement.

The role of emotions in holding attention is equally significant. By evoking
feelings such as curiosity or joy, you can make your content resonate more
deeply with your audience. It is not about manipulating emotions but
forming genuine connections by conveying the message.

The concept of the attention span is intrinsically linked to cognitive load, or
the amount of information that our brains can process at any given
moment. Studies have shown that the average adult-focused attention
span is about 45 minutes, after which performance and attentiveness start
to wane. This is not just about the limit of our concentration but also about
the need for our brains to process and assimilate the information received.
Hence, introducing short breaks every 45–60 min in presentations or
workshops can be highly beneficial. These interludes allow attendees to
refresh, stretch, check emails, and grab coffee. Such pauses not only
mitigate the effects of cognitive fatigue, but also allow for better retention
and processing of information. By respecting the natural ebb and flow of
human attention, PreSales professionals can enhance the overall
effectiveness of their sessions, ensuring that the audience remains
engaged, refreshed, and more receptive to the content being delivered.
Incorporating interactive elements such as real-time polls or Q&A sessions
can further enhance engagement. Such components foster a sense of
participation, making the audience feel involved in the conversation, rather
than just being passive listeners. However, to be aware of communicating
these breaks upfront, everybody knows to concentrate now on your stuff,
not the mail. (Cohan 2023; Riefstahl 2011; Roam 2016)

On a more scientific note, delving slightly into the world of neuroscience
can offer PreSales professionals insights into the mechanics of attention.
For instance, understanding how neurotransmitters such as dopamine,
associated with rewards and attention, function, can help craft more
effective presentations.

In an era where multitasking is the norm, engaging multiple senses can be
a game changer. Most presentations primarily engage the auditory and
visual senses. Nevertheless, imagine the impact of a tactile or olfactory
component where appropriate. This is bound to make the presentation

stand out in the audience’s memory. It is important to remember that
multitasking is often simply a rapid switch between tasks, which can lead
to fragmented attention and retention. (Belfort 2020)

Finally, recognising the cultural and demographic diversity of audiences is
crucial. Attention dynamics can differ significantly across cultures and age
groups. A strategy that works for one demographic may not necessarily
resonate with another. This allows us to understand and customise our
approach based on audience dynamics.


**Key Takeaway:**

Engaging Start: Begin presentations with strong content, such as a notable
statistic or relatable anecdote, to capture attention instantly.

Novelty: Introduce unexpected elements, such as interactive segments or
videos to maintain interest.

Value Proposition: Address the subconscious query of "What is in it for
me?" by presenting the benefits early on. Tailor these propositions for your
specific audience.

Comfortable Environment: The setting of room temperature, seating, and
lighting play a pivotal role in maintaining audience attention. Ensure
comfort.

Breaks and Attention Span: Incorporate short breaks every 45-60 minutes
to help attendees refresh, leading to better content retention.

Interactive Elements: Foster active participation using tools, such as real-
time polls or Q&As.

Multi-sensory Engagement: In a multitasking era, engage beyond auditory
and visual senses. Consider tactile or olfactory components where
appropriate.

Cultural Considerations: Adapt strategies based on cultural and
demographic differences, ensuring resonance across diverse audiences.


### 10.2 Demo Process for PreSales Professionals

1. Pre-Demo Research:

Objective: To understand clients’ context, needs, and challenges in
tailoring the demo accordingly. Use the information from the discovery and
the OSD.

Activities:

•   Review notes from the discovery phase to understand clients’ pain
points and objectives.

•   Conduct additional research on client industry, competitors, and recent
news or events.

•   Identify key stakeholders who will attend the demo and understand
their roles and priorities.

2. Demo Planning:

Objective: To structure a demo to align with clients’ needs and ensure
logical flow.

Activities:

•   Outline the main sections or features of the showcase.

•   Determine the duration and allocate time for each section, ensuring
time for Q&A.

•   Prepare backup plans for potential technical issues such as connectivity
problems or software glitches.

3. Tailoring Content:

Objective: To customise demo content to resonate with client-specific
challenges and objectives.

Activities:

•   Highlight features or functionalities that directly address clients’ pain
points.

•   Use industry-specific examples or scenarios.

•   Avoid generic pitches; personalise the content based on prior
interactions and research.

5. Dry Run or Rehearsals:

Objective: To practice the demo presentation and ensure preparedness.

Activities:

•   Conduct a full rehearsal of the demo, following the planned structure
and timing.
•   Simulate potential questions and technical issues to test responses and
solutions.
•   Gather feedback from colleagues or team members who participate in
the dry run.
•   Make necessary adjustments based on rehearsal feedback to improve
the demo flow and content.

4. Technical Setup:

Objective: To ensure a smooth demo experience without technical hiccups.

Activities:

•   Test all equipment and software beforehand, including screen-sharing
tools, microphones, and video.

•   Ensure a stable internet connection.

•   Have backup devices or alternative platforms ready in case of
unforeseen issues.

5. Execution:

Objective: Deliver a compelling and engaging demo.

Activities:

•   Begin with an introduction, setting the context, and agenda for the
demo.

•   Showcase the product or solution, emphasising its benefits and value
proposition.

•   Engage the audience with questions, interactive elements, or real-time
feedback.

•   Address questions or concerns promptly and clearly.

•   Conclude by summarising key points and outlining next steps.

6. Addressing Questions:

Objective: Engage with the audience and address their concerns or queries.

Activities:

•   Encourage questions throughout the demo to ensure clarity.

•   Listen actively to the questions and provide concise, clear answers.

•   If unsure about a particular query, commit to returning an answer post-
demo.

7. Feedback Collection:

Objective: Gather insights on the demo’s effectiveness and areas of
improvement.

Activities:

•   Distribute post-demo surveys or feedback forms to attendees.

•   Conduct one-on-one feedback sessions if feasible.

•   Analyse feedback to identify trends, positive takeaways, and areas
requiring refinement.

8. Follow-Up:

Objective: Maintain momentum and nurture client relationships.

Activities:

•   Send a thank-you note or email to attendees expressing gratitude for
your time.

•   Share additional resources, documentation, or answers to questions
raised during the demo.

•   Schedule follow-up meetings or discussions to delve deeper into
specific topics or move forward in the sales process.

In the next chapters, we dive deeper into each of the areas.


### 10.3 The Structure of a Successful Demo

Different demos serve distinct purposes, and understanding their unique
characteristics is the first step towards a successful presentation.

Inspiration Look & Feel Session: This is akin to a teaser. It generates
interest and excitement regarding a product or solution, offering glimpses
into its potential.

Deep Dive Demo: As the name suggests, this is a detailed product
exploration, delving deeply into its features, benefits, and potential use
cases. (Cohan 2023)

Q&A Session: This is an interactive format in which the audience poses
questions, allowing for clarity and a deeper understanding.

RFX Demo: Tailored to respond to a Request for Proposal (RFP) or similar
queries; this demo is structured around specific requirements and
questions the request poses.

Quick overview of the different demo types:

Demo         Goals            Objective        Length     When to
Type                                                      Use
Inspiration Create            Showcase the     45 – 60 Early in the
Look &      excitement        high-level       minutes sales cycle to
Feel Demo and vision          value and                generate
aesthetic                interest
appeal of the
solution

Deep Dive     Provide         Demonstrate       1-4        Mid to late
Demo          detailed        specific          hours      sales cycle
insights and    features and                 when
functionality   capabilities in              specifics are
detail                       required

Q&A           Address         Clarify doubts    30-60   Anytime
Demo          specific        and answer        minutes during the
queries and     questions                 sales cycle
concerns        about the                 when
solution                  questions
arise

RFX Demo      Respond to      Tailored      2-4            During the
formal          demonstration hours          formal
requests and    addressing                   procurement
requirements    specific                     process
RFP/RFI
requirements

Experienced professionals understand that demos are more than just a
display of a product or solution. Essentially, they weave a narrative that
highlights the value, potential, and practicality of what is being presented.
Over time, the sales and PreSales teams have developed various types of
demos, each crafted for a specific purpose. Let us delve into these distinct
presentations to discover the art and science that underpin each one.

Imagine, for a moment, walking into a movie theatre. The lights dim, and
the trailer begins. It is short, gripping, and leaves you yearning for more.
This cinematic teaser is quite similar to the Inspiration Look & Feel Session

in the world of demos. These sessions are crafted to kindle interest, almost
teasing the audience with the allure of what the product or solution
promises. Your approach does not overwhelm the viewer with excessive
information. Rather, it is about providing just enough attractive glimpses to
ignite a spark of excitement and curiosity. It is about painting a vision, a
possibility, and a hint of what will come. This kind of demo can be especially
powerful during initial engagements, when the goal is to catch the eye and
pique interest.

However, not all situations require a teaser. Sometimes, what is needed is
an in-depth and comprehensive exposition. Enter the Deep Dive Demo.
Unlike the Look & Feel Session, the Deep Dive does not skirt around the
edges. It dives headfirst into the heart of the product by exploring its
myriad features, functionalities, benefits, and potential applications.
However, it is not just a lengthy exposition. It is methodical and structured,
ensuring that every facet is logically and cohesively illuminated. The
objective is clear: to leave no stones unturned, ensuring that the audience
thoroughly understands the products’ capabilities. For those who are
already familiar with the product or those seeking detailed insights, the
Deep Dive is the go-to format.

Engagement is key in demos, and the essence of engagement is a Q&A
Session. This is where the dynamic shifts from one-way presentation to
two-way interaction. Audiences equipped with questions play a more
active role. The sessions nature is fluid, adapting to the queries raised.
However, this apparent spontaneity is underpinned by thorough
preparation. A skilled PreSales professional anticipates possible questions
and prepares clear and concise answers. However, you should not just
anticipate questions; ask the prospect if they can send questions upfront
so you can prepare materials for it. This interactive style promotes
transparency, allowing immediate clarification and deeper understanding.
It is a format that thrives on dialogue, effectively resolving any
uncertainties on the spot.

Finally, in the business world, specific tailored requirements often arise.
Companies frequently issue Requests for Proposals (RFPs) that detail
specific needs, questions, or challenges. Responding to these requests
necessitates a specialised demo, aptly named the RFX Demo. RFX Demo is

a presentation carefully crafted to meet specific details of the Request for
Proposal (RFP). Instead of using a one-size-fits-all approach, this type of
demo is designed based on the unique needs and questions outlined in the
RFP. It is similar to a bespoke suit, tailored exactly to the specifications
provided. The presenter’s task is to align the product features perfectly
with the requirements of the RFP, ensuring an exact match.

Demos are much more than straightforward presentations; they are
narratives and journeys that lead the audience from initial curiosity to a
complete understanding. Each type of demo offers a unique appeal and
purpose. From the enticing Inspiration Look & Feel Session to the detailed
Deep Dive Demo, the interactive Q&A Session, and the precisely tailored
RFX Demo, each plays a distinct role. For PreSales professionals, excelling
in these formats is not just about deep product knowledge; it is also about
connecting with the audience, addressing their specific needs, and crafting
a story that truly resonates with them. Demo remains a powerful tool in
the constantly changing sales world. When executed with the right
strategy, it can be the key to a successful customer engagement. (Cohan
2023; Riefstahl 2011)

Now, let us look at the Demo structure, but this can vary depending on the
clients’ use cases, challenges, and needs.


#### 10.3.1 The Look & Feel Demo

The Look & Feel Demo focuses on showing off the overall feel and design
of a product or solution, rather than going into detailed technical features.
The main goal is to leave a lasting, positive impression and grab the
audience’s attention by showcasing how easy and pleasant the product is
to use and how it looks. This demo is all about highlighting the user
experience and the product's interface. Here, is the detailed breakdown of
the structure.

1. Introduction

Set the Stage: Start by framing the context of the demo. Mention what the
audience will be seeing and the objectives of the Look & Feel session.

Picture Pitch: A Picture pitch is a very powerful way of framing solution
capabilities at an early stage.

Brief Overview: Provide a snapshot of the product’s primary purpose and
its significance in its target domain or industry, including the value the
solution adds.

2. Interface and Design

User Interface (UI) Highlights: Showcase the visual aspects of the product,
emphasising its design principles, colour schemes, layout, and other visual
elements that make the product unique and user-friendly.

Navigation Overview: Walk the audience through primary navigation
paths, showing them how users can move from one section or feature to
another.

3. Key Features Spotlight

Feature Highlighting: Instead of deep-diving into every feature, select a
few standout features and demonstrate them briefly in the context of what
the value is for the client. The goal is to demonstrate product capabilities
without overwhelming the audience.

Real-world Application: Give a quick scenario or use case where each
highlighted feature is beneficial. This helps the audience relate the product
to real-world applications.

4. User Experience (UX)

Ease of Use: Emphasis on the products’ user-friendliness and intuitive
design. If possible, demonstrate a common task that users can easily
accomplish.

Responsiveness: Highlight how the product performs under different
conditions, such as on various devices or screen sizes, ensuring it offers a
seamless experience.

5. Customisation and Personalisation

Themes and Skins: If the product offers configuration options like themes,
skins, or personal dashboards, briefly showcase them. This adds a layer of
adaptability and personal touch to the product.

Settings Overview: Quickly touch upon configuration settings, allowing
users to tailor the product to their preferences.

6. Feedback and Engagement Tools

User Feedback Mechanisms: If the product has in-built tools for users to
give feedback, rate, or review, showcase them. This demonstrates that the
product values user input and is geared towards continuous improvement.

Engagement Features: Highlight any built-in tools or features designed to
boost user engagement, such as notifications, gamification elements, or
community features.

7. Conclusion and Next Steps

Reiterate the Value Proposition: Summarise the key points, emphasising
the products’ unique selling points and how they stand out in the market.

Transition to the Next Phase: If the Look & Feel Demo is a precursor to a
more detailed demonstration or other stages in the sales process, guide the
audience on what to expect next.

Open the Floor: Although the Look & Feel is not as in-depth as other demos,
it is always a good idea to open the floor for a few questions or to gather
initial impressions from the audience.

Remember, the Look & Feel Demo is about creating a memorable first
impression. It is the equivalent of a movie trailer, capturing the essence,
generating excitement, and leaving the audience eager for more. Every
showcased element should be carefully curated to represent the products’
best attributes while ensuring that the demonstration remains concise and
engaging. But I also recommend here to execute a Discovery before even if
only for 15 min. You can also combine the Discovery and the Look & Feel
Demo ,but you should be experienced in both before even trying it.


#### 10.3.2 Deep Dive Demo (The Standard Demo)

As PreSales professionals, it is essential to provide clients with a thorough
understanding of the product. This is where the Deep Dive Demo,
sometimes known as the Standard Demo, comes in. Let us walk through

the detailed structure of this type of demo, step by step, to see how it
effectively communicates the full capabilities of your product.

Setting the stage begins with an Introduction. Picture this: a room filled
with anticipation. On the one hand, the vendor, and on the other, the client
representatives. This is the moment for the introductions, ensuring that
each side knows the faces and roles of the other. However, beyond
pleasantries, laying down ground rules for meetings is paramount.
Discussing points like when to ask questions ensures the session remains
productive. Believe it or not, the human attention span has its limitations.
A break every 40-60 minutes can indeed seem unconventional, but think
about it: how long can one truly focus without drifting off? By showcasing
the roadmap of the discussion points for the day, attendees know what to
expect and when. (Greene 2018; Cohan 2023; Brian 2006)

With the stage set, move onto the Picture Pitch. This is not just about
talking, but captivating. Start off on a high note, offering a vivid overview
that gets your audience’s attention locked in for at least the first ten
minutes. Moreover, given our proclivity towards shifting attention,
especially in the digital age, keeping them with you, not on your gadgets, is
crucial. Discussing the overall challenges, potential solutions, and the
benefits of your offering during this segment sets the tone. There are
instances where clients are eager to see the actual application in action.
Seize this momentum after the Picture Pitch to give them a brief look,
showcasing enticing features such as UI support, dashboards, and analytics.
If you have been speaking for close to 40 minutes, consider a 5-10 minute
break. It allows the audience to process the information, engage in
clarifications, and refresh their focus.

Now, Explaining the Scenes becomes vital if you have multiple scenarios or
modules to discuss. For instance, if more than one Module or Application
is showcased, it is essential to provide a cohesive narrative that connects
these modules and the personas involved. (Rosenberg 2015; Riefstahl
2011)

Speaking of personas, it is time for Setting the Scene 1. By painting a vivid
picture of the 2-4 personas in the play, you provide a relatable context for
your audience. Narrate their challenges, how they feel about the pinch in

the absence of an efficient solution, and highlight how their tasks weave
into the overall business process.

A method that stands the test of time is Tell-Show-Tell. Initially, we
describe the impending demonstration, focusing on the personas’
challenges and how your solution can be a game changer. Subsequently,
display the solution in action, but remember that brevity is key.
Overshooting can be overwhelming for clients unfamiliar with an
application. Wrapping up and reiterating what was demonstrated and
linking it back to real-world advantages. (Roam 2016; Riefstahl 2011)

After painting this comprehensive picture, it is time to Close the Scene 1.
Lay emphasis on the larger organisational advantages of adopting your
solution. Whether monetary savings or process optimisations provide
compelling reasons backed by real-life instances. (Riefstahl 2011)

If a second scene or scenario exists, the cycle is repeated. Setting Scene 2
involves introducing new personas or contexts and delving deeply into their
challenges and solutions. Once again, the Tell-Show-Tell method is
employed for each persona, followed by a closing segment highlighting
broader organisational benefits.

But it does not end with showcasing the solutions features. The ultimate
deal-clincher is establishing the real-world efficacy of your offering. This
involves emphasising the Overall Benefits. Enrich your narrative with
powerful testimonials, preferably in video format, for that personal touch.
Incorporate case studies of clients who have reaped tangible benefits from
your solution.

Finally, as the curtains draw on the demo, outline the Closing & Next Steps.
Recall that a heterogeneous audience might be present, spanning finance,
IT, security, and more. Early on, determine which parts of the demo are
relevant to whom. If possible, these sessions were split to cater to specific
interests and queries. Above all, steer clear of beginning with boring
company overviews. The first impression counts. Ensure you capture
attention from the get-go.

1. Introduction:

Team Presentations: Start by introducing everyone involved from your
team. Seeing and recognising each other’s faces make the interaction more
personal and engaging. When introducing yourself, use your own words
instead of relying on a Sales Director, script, or standard introduction. Begin
with your name, title, and a brief overview of your professional
background, highlighting why you are an expert in this field and what
aspects of your work are most important to you. Adding personal details
about oneself and your family can also help in building rapport and
establishing a more relaxed and friendly atmosphere.

Guidelines for Engagement: Set the ground rules for the session
immediately at the start. Clarify when it is appropriate for attendees to ask
questions – should they interject during the presentation or hold questions
until the end? Also, inform everyone about the schedule for breaks. This
could include when they occur and how long they last. Establishing these
guidelines early helps to ensure that the session runs smoothly, and
everyone is on the same page regarding how to engage and interact.


*Figure 3: Rules for the Meeting*


Pacing the Session: Recommend taking a break every 40–60 min. It is
important to remember that our ability to focus on and absorb information
is limited. Regular breaks are essential for regeneration and help maintain
the audience’s attention and engagement throughout the session.


*Figure 4:Breaktime to use for breaks in Powerpoint*


Charting the Day: A cursory glance at the roadmap for the day, spotlighting
the core discussion points, prepares the audience for the journey ahead.


*Figure 5: Agenda/ Roadmap Slide*


2. Picture Pitch:

Engaging Imagery: Starting with a captivating visual story. Aim for
approximately ten minutes of focused attention anchored by a sequence of
engaging images. This opening segment is designed to capture the

audience’s interests immediately, drawing them in and away from any
outside distractions.


*Figure 6: Slides of a Picture Pitch*


A Glimpse of the Application: If you notice that the audience is eager to
see the application itself rather than just presentations, take advantage of
this interest. After your initial visual introduction, give them a sneak peek
of the applications’ features. This could include showcasing its excellent
user interface, powerful data analytics capabilities, smooth navigation, and
interactive dashboard. This teaser can further pique their curiosity and
engagement.

3. Explaining the Scenes:

Scene Segregation: If your demo includes several different aspects, such as
showcasing for example both Global Trade Management and Transport
Management, it is important to lay them out in a clear sequence. Outline
the order in which you present these segments. Clearly define the timeline,
the different personas you will address, and how these processes are
interrelated. This structured approach helps the audience to follow along
more easily and understand how each part fits into the bigger picture.

4. Setting the Scene 1 (Introduction of the Personas):

Persona Portraits: Introduce the 2-4 central characters. Sketch their
challenges and underline the necessity for solutions in their narratives.


*Figure 7: Slide Introduction to the team*


Process Preview: Before diving into the details, provide a broad overview
of the end-to-end process relevant to the section you are presenting.
Providing a comprehensive understanding upfront helps the audience see
how everything is connected. They will be able to better understand the
interplay between different personas and the overall business context.


*Figure 8: Slide Example Workflow*


5. Tell-Show-Tell for each Persona:

Tell/Narrative: Set the stage for the next part of your presentation by
outlining what is to come. Describe the challenges and struggles faced by
the persona you are focusing on. Then, introduce how your solution acts as
a 'magic wand,' effectively addressing and resolving these issues. This
preview helps to prepare the audience for the story you are about to tell
and highlight the transformative impact of your solution. (Roam 2016)


*Figure 9: Slide explaining what you show: Tell*


You can also add some nice graphics of what you will show or the example
to make it more insightful.


*Figure 10: Slide Explain the use case: Tell*


Show/Visualization: Now, it is time to present a solution. Be mindful of
how you do this; it is a delicate balance between providing sufficient
information and overwhelming your audience. Aim for clarity and
simplicity. A straightforward explanation is often more effective than
delving into the complex details.

Tell/reiteration: Circle back to what was showcased, crystallising its real-
world implications and value propositions.


*Figure 11: Slide Key Points: Tell*


6. Closing Scene 1 (Benefits for the Organisation)

Valued Outcomes: Explain both the tangible and intangible benefits of your
solution. This could include cost savings, operational improvements, or
examples from case studies in which a similar solution led to significant
changes. Highlighting these benefits helps the audience understand the
real-world impact of your solution. I recommend making a break after each
scene to give some breathing room time for conversations and preparation
of the next scene. (Cohan 2023)


*Figure 12: Slide Business Outcomes for client*


4. Setting the Scene 2 (Introduction of the Personas)

Sequel to the First: The narrative continuity ensures that the audience
remains aligned and engaged. This methodology mirrored the first
scenario.

5. Tell-Show-Tell for each Persona: Replicate the approach from Scene 1,
adapting to the unique nuances of the current persona and scenario.

6. Closing the Scene 2 (Benefits for the Organisation): Reiterate the
advantages, drawing a clear correlation between the solutions capabilities
and organisational upliftment.

Start with the next Scene or transition to overall Benefits

7. Overall Benefits of the Application

The Grand Narrative: Culminate the demonstration by weaving a
compelling narrative. Why should you invest? What are the tangible
takeaways?

Voice of Validation: Testimonials, particularly in video formats, infuse
authenticity. Couple this with case studies, painting a picture of real-world
successes. (Kane 2020)


*Figure 13: Slide Testimonial with video*


8. Closing & Next Steps

Outline the Closing & Next Steps: As you conclude, it’s crucial to clarify
what comes after the demo. This involves summarizing the key points
discussed, reaffirming the benefits, and discussing the specific next steps,
which might include scheduling further meetings, discussing contract
terms, or outlining the implementation phases.


*Figure 14: Slide showing next steps overview*


Address the Audience Needs: Acknowledge that your audience might have
varied interests based on their roles, such as finance, IT, or security
concerns. Tailor this part of the presentation to address these varied
interests directly, potentially splitting sessions if necessary to cater to
specific needs.

Avoid Starting with Overviews: To maintain engagement until the end of
your presentation, start your closing with a dynamic summary rather than
a dry overview.

Key takeaway
1. Introduction and Setting the Stage:

Purposeful Introductions: Ensure both sides are familiar with each other's
roles and faces.

Ground Rules: Establish clear guidelines on when to ask questions and the
schedule for breaks to maintain focus and productivity.

2. Picture Pitch:

Captivate Your Audience: Begin with an engaging and visually appealing
overview to capture attention.

Initial Display: Use this opportunity to briefly showcase the application's
key features like UI, dashboards, and analytics to spur interest.

3. Explaining the Scenes:

Narrative Connection: If demonstrating multiple modules or scenarios,
provide a cohesive narrative that links them together and relates to the
personas involved.

4. Setting the Scene and Persona Introduction:

Persona Context: Introduce 2-4 personas, detail their challenges, and show
how your solution addresses these in the context of their daily operations.

5. Tell-Show-Tell Method:

Describe, Display, Reiterate: Begin by describing what will be shown,
demonstrate the solution in action, and conclude by linking the
demonstration back to the benefits for a tangible business impact.

6. Close the Scene:

Highlight Organizational Benefits: Emphasize how your solution can lead
to cost savings or process optimizations, providing compelling reasons
backed by real-life instances.

7. Overall Benefits:

Real-World Efficacy: Stress the practical benefits of the solution with
testimonials and case studies to underline its success and impact.

8. Closing & Next Steps:

Engagement and Action Plan: Clearly outline the next steps following the
demo, ensuring the audience knows the immediate actions to take toward
adoption or further engagement.


#### 10.3.3 The Q&A Demo

The primary objective of the Q&A Demo is to address specific queries and
concerns of prospective clients. This is not an open field for broad
presentations or extensive pitch narratives, like you would find in the Deep
Dive. Instead, they are reserved for precision, clarity, and immediacy.
However, while you may not always find the need to employ full demo
personas or picture pitches as you would in a deep dive, your inclusion is
not entirely off the table either. Such decisions hinge largely on the context.
If, for instance, these elements have not been showcased in prior
interactions, bringing them into the Q&A Demo might be beneficial.
However, this is conditional and contingent upon the unique situation and
requirements of the discussion at hand.

A key element of a successful Q&A Demo is its thorough preparation. Going
into this type of session without knowing which questions to ask is like
trying to find your way through a maze without a map. Therefore, it is
crucial to gather these questions beforehand. This proactive strategy offers
two main benefits: it keeps the conversation focused on what the
prospective client is most concerned about and it allows the PreSales

professional to prepare thoroughly and thoughtfully for the session. (Cohan
2023)

To gather questions for a Q&A Demo, you can use a discovery session to
determine what concerns your audience might have. If this is not possible,
you can ask questions through email or set up a specific call to prepare. It
is important not to start the Q&A Demo without knowing these questions
beforehand. This knowledge helps you customise the demo, ensure the
right people are there, and adjust the logistics and content to fit the
audience’s needs. (Cohan 2022)

Another valuable tip for steering a Q&A Demo to success is prioritisation.
When presented with a slew of questions, it is natural to feel overwhelmed.
Here, the clients’ insights can be invaluable. Engage with them to discern
the most pressing concerns from the list. What topics are most eager to
delve into? By identifying the top three or four questions, a PreSales
professional can begin a session on a strong note, addressing these high-
priority queries first. This aligns with clients’ immediate interests and sets
a positive and engaging tone for the remainder of the session.


*Figure 15: Example of a Q&A Slide for a Session*


Post this initial segment, a brief intermission can be introduced. It serves as
a momentary pause, allowing the prospective client to reflect, discuss
internally, and come forth with spontaneous questions or thoughts. Having

addressed the priority questions with complete focus and commitment,
this break can be a perfect segment into subsequent topics. The essence of
a Q&A Demo is to foster an environment of dialogue and understanding,
and this structured yet flexible approach ensures just that.

In a Q&A Demo, it is important to remember that while this format is more
direct, it is not separate from other types of demos. You can include parts
of a Deep Dive Demo or aspects of a Look & Feel presentation. The key is
to perform this carefully. Whether these elements are included depends on
how much the audience already knows about the product and what they
want to achieve in the demo. Your main aim should be to find a good
balance: to answer the audience questions while giving them a complete
view of the product. If you do not get any questions upfront, try to call each
participant individual. In direct calls, the person you speak to is more open.


**Key Takeaway:**

Focused Objectives: The Q&A Demo is designed specifically to address
direct queries from prospective clients about the product or service. It is
not for broad presentations but for specific, targeted discussions.

Preparation is Key: Adequately preparing for the Q&A Demo is crucial. This
involves collecting questions in advance either through discovery sessions,
emails, or direct calls to ensure that all concerns are ready to be addressed
during the demo.

Customization and Relevance: Tailor the demo to fit the specific questions
and needs of the audience. This includes ensuring the right subject matter
experts are present and adjusting the content and logistics accordingly.

Prioritization of Questions: Work with clients to identify and prioritize their
most pressing questions. Start the session by addressing these key
concerns to immediately engage the audience and set a constructive tone
for the rest of the demo.

Flexibility in Format: While primarily focused on Q&A, the session can
incorporate elements from other types of demos like the Deep Dive or Look
& Feel presentations, depending on previous interactions with the client
and their familiarity with the product.

Interactive Engagement: Encourage an interactive dialogue. After
addressing the prioritized questions, take a brief intermission to allow the
client to reflect and possibly come up with more spontaneous questions,
enhancing the interactive nature of the session.

Balanced Delivery: While the focus is on addressing specific questions,
ensure to provide a holistic view of the product when appropriate,
integrating demonstrations or features not initially questioned but relevant
to the client’s needs and interests.

Open and Direct Communication: If prior collection of questions is
challenging, consider direct calls with participants. People tend to be more
open and detailed in one-on-one conversations, which can lead to more
insightful and productive demos.


#### 10.3.4 RFX Demo

This particular demo, grounded in the specificities of an RFX (Request for
Proposal, Quotation, Information), requires a heightened sense of
preparation and alignment. However, what distinguishes it from other
demos? How can Presales professionals maximise their efficacy?

When we delve into the core of the RFX Demo, we are immediately
confronted with a pivotal advantage: a document that theoretically
outlines the prospective clients’ needs and requirements. By its nature, RFX
is a beacon of clarity. It maps out the territory and sets expectations.
However, it is crucial to note that RFX is comprehensive, although it may
not seem to be an exhaustive representation of a client’s vision or
challenges. It is a starting point, a compass directing PreSales professionals
towards the potential alignment of product offerings and client needs.

The RFX gives you a basic understanding, but to really nail an RFX Demo,
you need to focus on the discovery session. This first meeting is very
important but is often overlooked. It is your chance to get what the client
wants and needs. Instead of guessing or making assumptions, use these
discovery sessions to get the real scoop. They help confirm what is in the
RFX and also dig up deeper issues, hopes, and goals that the client might
not have written down. Think of it like peeling an onion – you are getting
to the heart of what the client really wants. (Cohan 2022)

Having acquired this enriched understanding, the next step in the RFX
Demo journey aligns closely with the Deep Dive approach. It is crucial to
understand the subtle differences in how we present our products and
services. Think of the Deep Dive as a comprehensive exploration. It is like
taking a broad view of what our product or service can do, covering various
aspects and potential. On the other hand, the RFX Demo is more focused
and specific. It is like zeroing in on what the client specifically needs, making
sure every feature or capability we showcase is directly answering the client
requirements, as stated in the RFX. Imagine it as a dance where precision
meets relevance. We align our product strengths exactly with the client’s
desires. It is about hitting the correct notes at the right time. Remember
that the key is to start with the most important points that resonate with
clients’ needs. Even if the client sets an agenda, do not hesitate to reshape
it, if necessary. Standing out of the competition sometimes means doing
things differently. It is not just about following a set pattern but about
tailoring your approach to create a unique and compelling case for your
product. (Keenan 2018; Brian 2006)

For example, you can create overview slides showing what is available out
of the box, what needs configuration, and what may not be available.


*Figure 16: RFX Capability Overview*


Or you use a slide with use cases explaining their questions in a kind of flow
through a day-life example:


*Figure 17: Day in a Life flow*


However, the RFX Demo does not stop at showcasing. Drawing inspiration
from the Q&A Demo format, it weaves into a dialogic element, ensuring an
interactive and dynamic session. After presenting a solution or feature,
space is carved out for questions, feedback, and clarification. By oscillating
between presentation and interaction, the demo transforms from a
monologue into a conversation, fostering mutual understanding and
bridging gaps in real-time.

Another distinguishing feature of the RFX Demo is its collaborative nature.
Given the depth and specificity of RFX-based interactions, it is not just the
product or solution on trial; it is the entire ecosystem that supports it. This
calls for an 'A-Team' approach involving stakeholders beyond the PreSales
domain. Professional Services can provide insight into the implementation
and integration of nuances. Product Management can shed light on the
product roadmap, potential upgrades, and how they align with the
industry’s path. Meanwhile, Client Success can offer real-world examples
of how existing clients have leveraged the product to overcome challenges
similar to those outlined in the RFX. Collectively, this multidimensional
perspective provides a comprehensive picture, reinforcing the product
value proposition.

The RFX Demo is about being thorough and closely matching clients’ needs.
But what makes it really effective is not just covering what the client has

clearly asked for. It is also about understanding their hidden needs, which
they might not have directly mentioned. This means carefully analysing the
RFX, using discovery sessions to obtain a better understanding, presenting
our solutions accurately, and encouraging open, two-way conversations.

To master the RFX Demo is truly understanding the harmony between
client expectations and product capabilities. It is about showcasing what is
available and how it aligns with what is required.


**Key Takeaway:**

Preparation and Alignment: Successful RFX Demos require meticulous
preparation and an alignment with the client’s documented needs and
requirements. This ensures that the demo is targeted and relevant.

Understanding Client Needs: The RFX document serves as a foundational
tool that outlines the client's needs, offering a clear starting point.
However, it's crucial to acknowledge that while RFX provides clarity, it may
not fully capture the client's vision or concerns.

Importance of Discovery Sessions: Before the actual demo, engaging in
discovery sessions is critical. These sessions help verify the needs listed in
the RFX and uncover deeper client expectations, issues, and goals, thus
refining the demo's focus.

Deep Dive vs. Focused Approach: Unlike general demos that broadly
explore product capabilities, an RFX Demo is more concentrated,
emphasizing specific features and capabilities that align directly with the
client's stated needs. It's a strategic approach that ensures every part of
the demo addresses relevant client requirements.

Interactive Format: Incorporating an interactive component, similar to a
Q&A session, transforms the demo from a monologue to a dialogue. This
fosters a two-way conversation, allowing for immediate feedback,
clarification, and adjustment, thus enhancing understanding and
engagement.

Collaborative Nature: The RFX Demo isn't just about the product but
involves a team approach. Integration of insights from Professional
Services, Product Management, and Client Success teams provides a

comprehensive view of how the product can meet the client's needs,
thereby strengthening the value proposition.

Understanding and Addressing Unspoken Needs: Beyond what is explicitly
requested in the RFX, successful demos also strive to identify and respond
to the client's unarticulated needs. This may involve probing during
discovery sessions and intuitively aligning product strengths with potential
client desires not mentioned in the RFX.

Customization and Client Engagement: Effective RFX Demos are tailored
not only to meet but exceed client expectations, possibly redefining the
initial agenda to better suit uncovered needs. This proactive adaptation can
distinguish your demo from competitors.


> **Story**

We once faced a tough client during a sales process. Initially, we only had
limited interaction through a Request for Information (RFI) and then moved
to a Request for Proposal (RFP) phase. The client was quite reserved and
did not engage much with us, which led us to consider dropping out of the
process. Surprisingly, the decision to step back made the client more open
to communication. We then had multiple productive calls and discussions,
allowing us to conduct thorough discoveries and gather additional
information. This led to a successful demo, and ultimately, the client chose
our solution.


### 10.4 The Demo Preparation

The world of PreSales thrives on effective demonstrations. However, the
resounding success of any demo does not merely depend on the product’s
prowess, but on the meticulous preparation that precedes the
presentation. The adage, "Success is where preparation and opportunity
meet”, rings especially true in this scenario. This chapter will delve deeply
into the steps that serve as the foundation for an impactful product demo.

Deep Diving into the Cl       ’ Needs
Every effective demo does not start with the product but with the client. A
thorough understanding of client requirements is the cornerstone of

successful demonstration. This step is not just about knowing what the
client wants, but truly immerses oneself in the clients’ world. (Cohan 2022)

Begin revisiting notes from discovery sessions. These sessions, often
overlooked, are goldmines of information, revealing not only clients’
explicit needs but also their latent expectations. The voice of the client,
encapsulated in these notes, gives direction to the demo, highlighting
which aspects of the product resonate most profoundly with the client.

Make sure you really understand the specific problems your client faces in
their industry. For example, if they are in healthcare, they may be
concerned about keeping patient data safe. Alternatively, if they are
retailers, they may have trouble managing their inventory. Knowing these
details will help you to show how your product can solve particular issues.
Remember, always keep learning about your client; every conversation
gives you more insight into their needs. (Care 2020; Cohan 2023)

In the ocean of challenges the client might face, throwing the anchor at the
right spot is essential. Therefore, prioritising the pain points you aim to
address in the demo, ensuring that the product is showcased as the ideal
solution for the most pressing challenges. Remember, less is more.

Storyboarding the Demo
Once armed with a clear understanding of clients’ needs, the next step is
to weave a narrative around the product - this is where storyboarding
comes into play. A storyboard is essentially a demo script detailing the flow
and highlighting key touchpoints.

However, do not create this storyboard in isolation. Reflect upon the
structure of the previous chapters in this PreSales book to ensure
consistency and a coherent narrative. A familiar structure fosters comfort
and reinforces key points.

While drafting the storyboard, bring to life various scenarios and personas
that mirror the clients’ daily operations. For instance, if presenting to a
retail giant, scenarios could range from a store manager trying to restock
inventory to a client browsing items online. By embedding these relatable
scenarios, you transport the client from the demo room right into the heart
of their operations, making the products relevance undeniable.

Configuring and Customising the Product Environment
No one likes to wear ill-fitted clothing. Similarly, regardless of quality, a
generic product setup will never sit right with clients looking for solutions
tailored to their needs. Personalising the product environment to reflect
the clients branding, data, or specific configurations can bridge the gap
between the product and the client. (Hess 2020)

Apart from showcasing the products flexibility, such customisation also
paints a picture of what life could be like with the product integrated into
the client’s ecosystem. The familiarity of seeing their brand colours, logos,
or even dummy data can make the product experience more relatable,
driving home its potential impact.

Anticipating Questions and Addressing Concerns
Even with the most comprehensive demo, questions and concerns remain
inevitable. However, rather than viewing these as obstacles, consider them
as opportunities to deepen the clients understanding and address any
lingering doubts.

Being proactive in this regard is crucial. Think ahead and prepare responses
to potential questions or objections the client might raise. Not only does
this showcase your preparedness, but it also illustrates a deep
understanding of the clients concerns, further fostering trust. Moreover, by
addressing these concerns, you can expedite clients’ decision-making
process, keeping the momentum of the sale alive. It is completely normal
not to have all the answers immediately. Admitting that you do not know
something, but are willing to find it, can actually build trust with your
clients. In addition, it gives you a reason to schedule another meeting to
further discuss these points. Remember, the more you interact with your
clients, the better it is for building a strong relationship. (Cohan 2023)

The Imperative of a Dry Run
A chef does not serve a dish without tasting it. Similarly, going into a demo
without a dry run is a recipe for unforeseen challenges. This step is crucial
to ensure that all pieces of the demo puzzle fit seamlessly.

Start by ensuring that the demo environment, be it a software platform or
physical product, is stable and ready for presentation. This is also an
opportune moment to run through the demo with the Sales Director,
ensuring alignment in the narrative and approach. Their insights, combined
with the preparation, can fine-tune the demo and enhance its impact.

Technical glitches can become unwanted guests, especially in the era of
virtual meetings and demonstrations. Therefore, anticipate potential
hiccups, from internet connectivity issues to software crashes, and have
backup plans. For instance, having prerecorded segments can salvage a
demo if live demonstrations face challenges.

Rehearsing for Perfection
Practice, as they say, makes perfect. Running through the demo multiple
times refines the narrative, highlights potential pitfalls, and boosts
confidence. However, do not restrict these rehearsals to solo sessions.
Conducting a mock demo in front of colleagues unfamiliar with the client’s
specifics can provide fresh, unbiased perspectives. Their feedback, devoid
of any prior context, can pinpoint improvement areas and help sharpen the
demos focus. (Clear 2018)

Amassing Supplementary Materials
While comprehensive, a demo cannot cover every facet of a product or
address all the potential queries. This is where supplementary materials,
such as case studies, whitepapers, or user testimonials, come into play.
Having these ready not only provides additional context post-demo, but
also serves as a resource for clients to delve deeper into specific areas of
interest. In addition, consider adding prerecorded demos or even click-
through demos to the mix. The results are insane.

In essence, the path to a successful demo is paved with rigorous
preparation. By understanding the client’s world, crafting a compelling
narrative, personalizing the product environment, anticipating concerns


**Key Takeaway:**

Deep Dive into Client Needs

Revisit discovery session notes.

Understand the industry-specific challenges the client faces.

Prioritise the pain points you aim to address.

Storyboard the Demo

Plan the flow of the demo.

Use the structure of the chapter before and adjust it.

Select the scenarios and personas you want to show.

Configure /Customise the Product Environment

Set up the product or platform to reflect the client’s environment, whether
it is branding, data, or specific configuration.

Avoid generic setups. Personalisation makes a demo more relatable.

Anticipate Questions and Concerns

Prepare for potential questions, objections, or concerns.

This proactive approach not only shows preparedness but can also speed
up the decision-making process.

Dry Run

Ensure that the demo environment is stable.

Test the presentation.

Do it together with the Sales Director

Test for any potential glitches, especially if the demo is virtual.

Have backup plans. For instance, have pre-recorded segments in the case
of live-demo failures.

Rehearse

Run through the demo multiple times.

It is beneficial to do a mock demo in front of colleagues who are not familiar
with clients’ specifics – they can provide fresh perspectives.

Gather Supplementary Materials

Have case studies, whitepapers, or other relevant materials available to
share.

This can provide additional context or deeper dives post-demo.


### 10.5 The Demonstration

This chapter will guide you through creating influential demos,
underscoring the importance of storytelling as a tool to evoke emotions,
connect with the audience, and create a memorable experience.

Setup with sales
Imagine this scenario: You have been discussing a product for a while, and
now, it is time to show it in action. However, as you gear up to demonstrate,
it is essential to remember that it is not just about you. You are part of a
team and teamwork is crucial. Especially in scenarios where there might be
long stretches of talking, there is a real risk of losing energy, and
consequently, the audience’s attention. Speaking continuously for hours
can be draining, and it is easy to get lost in the details, causing the spark –
that fire that ignites interest – to disappear. (Brian 2006)

This is precisely why the Sales team plays an indispensable role. Thinking of
them as the backbone of a successful demo. They can function as
moderators while maintaining the presentation on the track. They can
effectively manage time, ensuring that all critical points are covered
without dragging the session. They field questions, ensuring that queries
are addressed in real time and weave in compelling client success stories at
strategic points. These stories can reignite interest, especially if the energy
levels begin to decrease. (Hess 2020)

Moreover, the Sales team provides a unique perspective. They can shift the
tone of the presentation effortlessly by adding variety and depth. Their
expertise in reading the room is invaluable. Not all attendees of a product
demo have the same interests or concerns. Some are champions who
already see the products value, some are decision-makers who need to be
convinced of its utility, and yet some might be distractors, sceptical of its

benefits. The Sales teams ability to gauge these dynamics can shape the
direction and tone of the demo.


#### 10.5.1 The Picture pitch

In the constantly evolving landscape of PreSales, professionals are
continually searching for innovative ways to communicate, captivate, and
convince. In this quest for connection, the "Picture Pitch" emerges as a
trailblazing technique that deviates from traditional presentation norms,
offering a dynamic, memorable, and emotionally resonant alternative.

The Essence of the Picture Pitch
The Picture Pitch approach challenges traditional presentation styles by
rapidly cycling through images, each displayed for just 5-10 seconds, in sync
with the presenter’s speech. This method is not about hurrying through the
content. Instead, it is designed to capture and maintain the audience’s
focus. By quickly moving from one image to the next, the Picture Pitch
keeps the audience engaged and connected to what the speaker is saying,
rather than allowing their attention to wander into the intricacies of each
slide. This dynamic technique ensures that the audience remains locked
onto the speaker’s message, creating a more memorable and impactful
presentation. (Keenan 2018; Brian 2006; Belfort 2020)

Some examples to understand the concept.


*Figure 18: Picture Pitch Example*


This picture is about the champion, and a picture of Rocky Balboa is added.
Most of us know of this movie and have some mental connections to it.
However, why does this approach matter and how does it fundamentally
differ from standard presentation methods?

Crafting Narratives Through Imagery
The power of the Picture Pitch lies in its ability to effectively engage the
human brain through visual storytelling. By presenting images rapidly, each
slide becomes more than just a visual aid; it transforms into a symbolic
representation of the key themes of the narrative. This technique leverages
the natural affinity of the brain for visual information, thereby making the
presentation more memorable and impactful.

In a Picture Pitch, images are carefully chosen to resonate with the
narratives’ central ideas. As these images flash briefly, they do not serve as
a focal point but rather as a catalyst for deeper understanding and
emotional connection. This approach ensures that the audience absorbs
the essence of the images while simultaneously aligning them with the
speakers’ words.

This strategy is particularly effective in PreSales presentations for SaaS
businesses, where complex ideas and solutions must be conveyed
succinctly and engagingly. By intertwining evocative visuals with a
compelling narrative, a Picture Pitch can make a powerful impression,
ensuring that the key messages are not only understood, but also
remembered long after the presentation ends. It is a harmonious blend of
visual and verbal communication that enhances the overall impact of the
presentation and leaves a lasting impression on the audience.

Strategic Image Selection: Evoking the Right Emotions
The key to an impactful Picture Pitch lies not only in pacing, but also in the
strategic selection of imagery. For instance, when discussing the robust
security of an IT system, what can be more illustrative than the imposing
image of a hefty, unyielding locker? This does not just signify security; it
embodies security, projecting an aura of impenetrability. Similarly, when
clarifying the concept of connectivity, the image of a globe crisscrossed
with lines beautifully encapsulates the essence of interconnectedness and
unity. (Greene 2018)

Drawing from popular culture can amplify the resonance of your pitch.
Consider the iconic "A-Team" on television. Using them as symbolic
representations for a team of experts infuses nostalgia and conveys the
idea of a versatile, resourceful, and unbeatable squad.


*Figure 19: Picture Pitch Example*


The Benefits: Beyond the Visual
What makes the Picture Pitch invaluable in the realm of PreSales?

Engagement: The rapid progression of images coupled with narration
ensures that audience attention remains undivided. There is little room for
distraction, requiring every second count.

Memory Imprint: Given the brain’s predisposition to visual memory, the
Picture Pitch ensures that the core messages are understood and
remembered. Days after the presentation, while the exact words might
fade, the images linger, reinforcing key-takeaways.

Emotional Connection: A well-curated image can evoke a spectrum of
emotions, from nostalgia to aspiration and from concern to comfort.
PreSales professionals can forge a deeper bond with the audience by
tapping into these emotions.

Narrative Flexibility: The Picture Pitch is malleable. Depending on the
audience’s reactions, a skilled presenter can adjust the narrative on the fly,
ensuring that the message always hits the correct chords.

The Journey to Mastery
Like all skills, mastering the Picture Pitch demands practice. It is a delicate
balancing act—ensuring that the images align perfectly with the narrative,
maintaining rhythm, and gauging audience reactions. However, the effort
invested pays rich dividends. Over time, as you refine this technique, you
will discover its profound impact, not just in terms of information delivery
but in the very essence of communication. (Clear 2018)


> **Story**

Remembering back to the day I first ventured into creating what I now
fondly refer to as the 'Picture Pitch', it feels just like yesterday. This
innovative approach was inspired by a training session; although the exact
individual who sparked this idea escapes my memory, he certainly deserves
much credit.

Initially, the concept of the Picture Pitch was met with scepticism, even
though I had doubts about its effectiveness. However, the perfect
opportunity to test its potential was presented itself during a significant
demonstration involving a diverse group of departments and personas.

One particular individual during the Discovery phase proved to be quite a
challenge. Their focus was solely on seeing the product demo, paying little
attention to the underlying concept. In that moment, I decided to take a
leap of faith and try out the Picture Pitch.

This impact was immediate and profound. After the initial segment of the
workshop where the Picture Pitch was unveiled, the same individual who
had been challenging earlier approached me with remarkable feedback. He
expressed that, despite having 30 years of experience in the business world,
my presentation was the best he had ever seen.


#### 10.5.2 Tell-Show-Tell with personas

The "Tell, Show, Tell" (TST) approach is a widely accepted teaching and
demonstration model. When it comes to sales and product demos, it
provides a structured method to communicate value to prospects. Coupling
this with personas can make demos even more tailored and engaging for
the target audience. (Riefstahl 2011; Roam 2016)

Introduction: Contextualising with Personas
Let us break down the TST approach within the realm of Limbic Persona-
Based Selling.

Tell (Set the Stage) introduces the persona. Describe who they are, their
needs, their primary emotional triggers, and what they seek in a solution.
This sets the stage for the audience to empathise with and relate to the
persona.

Example: "Meet Alex. Alex is an Achiever. He is always looking for tools that
will help him stay ahead of his competitors, which showcase his
commitment to excellence and innovation."


*Figure 20: Persona Overview*


Core: Demonstrating Value through Personas
Show (Demonstration) Now, demonstrate the features or benefits of your
product that align with your personas’ needs and desires. Make sure this
demonstration is specifically tailored to highlight solutions to the
challenges faced by the persona.

Example: "Let us examine how our software helps me stay ahead.
Advanced analytics and predictive trends This gives me an edge, allowing
me to anticipate market shifts before they happen."

3. Conclusion: Reinforce and Validate

Tell (Reiteration) Conclude by recapping what was shown. Validate the
solution in the context of the personas’ needs and desires, reinforcing the
value proposition.

Example: "So, with our software, I´m not just reacting to market changes;
I´m anticipating them. I´m not just maintaining my status; I´m elevating it."
You may want to add: “Are there any questions to this use case?”


*Figure 21: Persona Value*


Applying "Tell, Show, Tell" with Multiple Personas
If you have multiple personas, you can combine them in a summary slide
or even in a process starting with person one, transitioning to person two,
and so on. One Tell, Show, Tell should not be longer than 10 minutes. You
can find a template and a Video on www.presales-handbook.com.


*Figure 22: Multiple Personas*



#### 10.5.3 Engaging the Audience

How does one keep an audience that might span a diverse range of
stakeholders engaged through the technical intricacies and data-driven
depths of a product demo?

The Power of Interaction
The heart of any memorable conversation or presentation is two-way
communication. The era of one-sided pitches, where a salesperson drones
with a monologue, hoping to impress a passive audience, has been a long
past. Today, a sophisticated clientele demands and deserves interaction.
(Riefstahl 2011)

When interactive elements are included in a presentation, the audience is
invited to participate. It could be a segment where attendees give live
feedback, a moment when they have a hands-on experience with the
product, or even a simple query about their expectations. This active
participation does more than just break monotony; it fosters a sense of
ownership. The demo is no longer something being presented to the
audience; it is something you are co-creating with them. (Riefstahl 2011)

Moreover, periodic checkpoints serve a dual purpose. First, you allow
yourself to evaluate the audience’s understanding, ensuring that no critical
point is lost in the translation. Second, you offer attendees a breather a
moment to assimilate information and formulate questions. (Cohan 2023)

The Art of Visual Engagement
Imagine explaining the vastness of the universe without showing a single
image of a galaxy or discussing the complexities of a software system
without a schematic diagram. Difficult, is not it? This is because humans are
inherently visual creatures. Our brains process images 60,000 times faster
than text. Thus, visual aids are not just add-ons; they are powerful tools for
comprehension and retention. (Greene 2018)

You should try to make the messaging simple and easy, so the client feels
working with your system makes complex tasks easy. For example, split
complex tasks into simple, smaller tasks and explain them one by one. In

Powerpoint, you can use animations to build up the slide click by click. One
example:


*Figure 23: Animated Flow Part 1*



*Figure 24: Animated Flow Part 2*


Charts, graphs, animations, and even simple infographics can provide
clarity by condensing vast amounts of data into easily digestible formats.
However, more than that, you can break the monotony by adding colour
and dynamism to a presentation. An animation can illustrate a process, a

chart can highlight growth, and a well-placed infographic can encapsulate
the essence of a solution. (Brian 2006)

The Pulse of the Room: Polls and Questions
Few tools offer real-time insights into an audience mindset, such as polls.
Using a simple click, attendees can share their opinions, preferences, or
concerns, giving the presenter valuable feedback. Beyond gathering data,
polls serve as quick engagement boosters, rekindling interest and ensuring
the audience’s focus. (Riefstahl 2011; Cohan 2023)

Equally important are the art of pausing and inviting questions. This simple
act achieves multiple objectives. It emphasises the presenters’ confidence
in the product, showcases transparency, and allows for course correction
based on real-time feedback.

Painting with Real-World Scenarios
A product demo might be a tool or solution, but its success hinges on its
relevance to real-world challenges. A PreSales professional bridges the gap
between the product features and its practical benefits by incorporating
realistic scenarios that resonate with clients’ day-to-day issues. It is one
thing to state that software can automate processes; it is infinitely more
impactful to show how it can streamline a specific workflow with which the
client struggles.

A Symphony of Stakeholders
Every audience member is a mosaic of stakeholders, each with unique
concerns, interests, and aspirations. A CTO might be keen on technical
specifications, whereas a CEO might focus on ROI and scalability.
Addressing each stakeholder ensures that the demo resonates with the
collective and the individual. By tailoring portions of the presentation to
different stakeholders, a PreSales professional can ensure holistic
engagement. (Brian 2006)


**Key Takeaway:**

Interactive Elements: Include portions where attendees can give feedback,
ask questions, or even try out the product.

Checkpoints: Pause at intervals to gauge understanding and address
queries.

Visual Aids: Use charts, graphs, and animations to break monotony and
visually represent benefits.

Polls: Introduce live polls during the demo to gather instant feedback on
the attendees' understanding and opinions. This could relate to their
interest in specific features or their agreement with proposed solutions.

Breaks: Schedule short breaks in the demo to allow for reflection and
informal discussion. This can help maintain energy levels and give
attendees a moment to formulate questions or comments.

Interactive Segments: Introduce moments when you ask the audience for
input or feedback. This keeps them engaged and guides the direction of the
demos.

Real-World Scenarios: Use realistic scenarios that resonate with clients’
day-to-day challenges.

Addressing Key Stakeholders: Different stakeholders have different
interests. Ensure that the demo speaks to each of them.


#### 10.5.4 Handling Objections During the Demo

Picture this: You are about to start a product demo. You have got your slides
set, the product features are all lined up to wow your audience, and you
have practiced your pitch over and over. It is showtime!

However, let us be real; being a PreSales pro means knowing that demos
do not always go as planned. You might face tough questions, concerns, or
even objections from your audience. These moments are crucial. How can
you handle them really change the game? It is all about staying on your
toes, thinking quickly, and turning those challenges into opportunities to
shine.

The Psychology of Objections
First, we demystify objections. Objections are not necessarily roadblocks
but can be signposts pointing to areas needing clarity or reinforcement.
These are the feedback mechanisms and indicators of engagement. An

audience voicing objections is one that is involved, thinking critically, and
investing in understanding your product. This is a positive sign. (Rosenberg
2015; Raz 2017)

The Power of Detachment
It is crucial to see objections for what they are: opportunities to provide
more clarity, not personal criticism. Recall that when a client raises an
objection, they are not criticising you. They usually express the need for
more information or clarity regarding a product or its features. Sometimes,
an objection might stem from a misunderstanding or gap in the client’s
knowledge. It is your opportunity to step in and fill this gap. Think of it as a
moment when the client is indirectly asking for your expertise to help them
see the full picture. (Rosenberg 2015)

There is a simple way to handle it:

Listening Carefully: Understand the objection fully before responding.
Sometimes, what seems like a challenge is actually a request for more
information.

Stay Calm and Professional: Remember, it is about the product, not you.
Keep your responses focused and factual.

Clarify and Educate: Often, objections arise from a lack of understanding.
Use this as an opportunity to educate clients on the product.

Provide Evidence: Where possible, back your explanations with data or
examples. This can help to address concerns more effectively.

Follow-up: After addressing the objection, check back to ensure that the
client is satisfied with the response and understands the information.

Remember that every objection is a stepping stone towards a better
understanding of the clients’ needs and how your product can meet them.
(Raz 2017)

The Gift of Active Listening
When confronted with a difficult objection, especially in the heat of the
moment, there is an inherent tendency to swiftly respond with a
counterargument or clarify immediately. However, pausing to listen truly

can be invaluable. By giving the client the space to articulate their concerns
fully, you gain time to formulate your response and a clearer understanding
of the root issue. Often, by simply listening, one might find that the
objection is not as significant as it first seemed. (Rosenberg 2015)

The Wisdom of Parking
Not all objections require immediate response. Some concerns may arise
from reflection or additional data. In such instances, it is entirely acceptable
to park a question, promising to revisit it after a break or even post the
demo. This approach serves a dual purpose: it provides you with time to
consider the best response, and it conveys to the client that their concern
is taken seriously and merits thoughtful consideration. (Riefstahl 2011)

Embracing the Objection
When you face objections, it is easy to become defensive, especially when
you really believe in your product. However, being defensive can worsen
the situation. Instead, think of objections as opportunities to learn more.
Stay relaxed and use these moments to explain the benefits and features
of the products. (Brian 2006)

Once an objection is voiced, acknowledge it. This acknowledgement is not
about agreeing with the concern but validating the clients’ right to voice it.
After acknowledging, swing to addressing the issue. This might involve
showcasing a feature in greater detail, providing data that counters the
concern, or illustrating how the product compares favourably to
alternatives. (Raz 2017)

The Art of Clarification
Sometimes, the objections voiced may be ambiguous or rooted in
misconceptions. In such scenarios, further clarity is required rather than
offering immediate solutions. Probing the questions may be helpful. Asking
the client to elaborate on or provide examples can shed light on real
concerns, allowing you to address them effectively. It also signals to the
client that you are genuinely interested in understanding and addressing
their reservations. (Rosenberg 2015)


**Key Takeaway:**


Never Take It Personal: Understand that objections are not personal
attacks. They are reflections of concerns or unmet needs related to the
product, not you as an individual.

Listen Even If You Want to Fight Back: Resist the urge to immediately
defensively counter objections. Instead, listen attentively to understand
the underlying issues that the prospect is facing.

Park Questions for Later Consideration: If you bump into a question or
objection that requires more thought or information, acknowledge it and
note it down for a break or follow-up after the demonstration.

Stay Calm and Collected: Do not get defensive. Instead, objections are seen
as opportunities to clarify or delve deeper.

Acknowledge and Pivot: Acknowledge the concern and pivot to how your
solution can address it or how it compares to alternatives.

Seek Further Clarification: If an objection is unclear, ask probing questions.
This can help in getting to the root of the concern.

Anticipate: Prepare answers to potential questions or concerns based on
prior interactions.

Listen Actively: Ensure that you fully grasp the objection before
responding.

Admit Uncertainty: If you do not have an answer on hand, commit to
getting back to them post-demo.


> **Story**

Early in my career, I found myself leading a critical meeting with an
aerospace client from the Middle East. This was a significant opportunity,
as I was stepping in for my manager, who could not attend. Armed with
confidence and enthusiasm for our new product, I was ready to impress.

Among the client representatives was a French lady who persistently
questioned our product, challenging its capabilities and my understanding.
In my eagerness to showcase my knowledge, I began responding
prematurely, often cutting her off mid-sentences. This approach, far from
convincing her, escalated her frustration and intensified her probing.

Upon reflecting on this encounter back at my hotel, I was still under the
impression that my rapid-fire responses had been effective. Eager for
validation, I called my supervisor, anticipating praise. Surprisingly, his
feedback was starkly different. He pointed out that my interruptions were
not only disrespectful to her but to all participants. He stressed that my
presumptive answers, assuming that I understood all the questions and
issues, were more harmful than admitting uncertainty.

This was a turning point for me. The key lesson was clear: listening,
listening, and listening. Effective listening involves letting others complete
their thoughts and asking for clarification. It is about understanding needs
and concerns and not just about providing immediate answers. This
experience taught me the value of patience, respect, and the power of truly
listening to understand, not just to respond.


### 10.6 After the Demo

Completing a successful product demo is a significant milestone in the
PreSales journey. Nevertheless, it is a common misconception to assume
that hard work is performed once the demo ends. The post-demo phase is
equally crucial. This is where the initial excitement must be channelled into
concrete actions, and potential concerns must be addressed. This chapter
sheds light on the strategies and steps that should be taken after the demo
to ensure that momentum is not lost. (Hess 2020)

Immediate Follow-up: Mastering the Momentum
The period immediately after a PreSales demo, typically the first 24-48
hours, is what can be likened to the 'golden hours' in emergency medicine.
This is the window of opportunity in which actions can have the most
significant impact. Much like the critical hours following trauma can
determine a patient’s recovery trajectory, the steps you take post-demo
can profoundly influence the decision-making process. By understanding
and utilising this window, it is possible to ensure that the momentum
generated during the demo carries forward, creating a trajectory that
maximises the chances of success. (Hess 2020)

Let us delve into the specifics of what this crucial period entails and how
best to navigate it.

The Power of Gratitude
Begin with gratitude. The simple act of sending a personalised thank you
note immediately post-demo does more than just extend courtesy. It
acknowledges the time and attention of the attendees, demonstrating that
you value their engagement. Such a note should not merely be a generic
acknowledgement but should also serve as a gentle reminder, reiterating
the key points discussed during the demo. Additionally, if any resources or
additional information were requested during the session, this is the
perfect time to provide them. By doing so, you subtly reinforce the
message, "We listen, and we deliver." (Brian 2006)

Tuning in to Attendee Perceptions
However confident you may be about the success of demos, there is
