nothing more revealing than direct feedback from those who attend.
Sending a short survey or feedback form could be enlightening. It offers
insights into what resonates, what may have been overlooked, and where
there may be room for improvement. This feedback is not only about the
demo you have just conducted; it is a treasure trove of information that can
enhance future presentations. One of my favourite Sales Directors is
picking up the phone after each demo to call every participant one by one
to obtain their thoughts, objections, and feelings about what they have
seen. A fantastic way to receive feedback.

In-house Reflections
While external feedback is invaluable, do not forget to turn the lens inward.
Convene with the team to assess the performance of demos, which is
crucial to pinpoint what went exceptionally well and which areas might
need tweaking. Sharing perspectives among different team members can
offer diverse insights. This collective introspection ensures that each
subsequent demo is a more refined version of the last one, with continuous
improvement at its core. If more people attend, you can launch a survey to
obtain anonymous feedback.

Keeping Everyone on the Same Page
Memory can be a fickle thing. As days pass, the clarity of what was
discussed during the demo might start to fade. Counter this by sending a

succinct meeting recap to all the attendees. This summary serves as a
touchstone, reminding them of the content, discussions, and commitments
made during the sessions.

Reinforcing the Pitch
While the demo is the main event, the post-demo period is a chance to
reinforce and solidify impressions. Take the time to underscore the key
points and value propositions that were showcased. This reinforcement is
less about introducing new information and more about emphasising what
has already been presented, ensuring it is front and centre in the potential
clients’ minds.

Clearing the Air
It is natural for attendees to have questions or doubts after the demo.
Sometimes, these concerns arise after some reflection, or they may be
points attendees feel hesitant to bring up during the session. Ensure that
you provide a platform for these to be voiced and promptly address them.
This proactive approach clears misconceptions and demonstrates a
commitment to transparency and client satisfaction. (Rosenberg 2015)

Nudging Towards the Next Step
Finally, while the immediate follow-up is about consolidation, it is also
about progression. You have showcased the product, you have reinforced
its merits, and you have addressed concerns. Now, it is time to gently guide
the prospect towards the next logical step, be it a deeper engagement, a
trial, or even a purchase decision. This guidance is not about being pushy
but about providing clear pathways for the potential client to move
forward. (Raz 2017)


**Key Takeaway:**

The first 24-48 hours post-demo is crucial.

Thank You Note: This is a personalised email or message thanking
attendees for their time, reiterating key points, and providing any
requested resources.

Feedback Collection: Sending a short survey to gather attendees' thoughts
about the demo. This can provide insights into future improvements.

Internal Feeback round: Gathering information from the team about what
went well. Use the lessons learned for the next demo.

Meeting Recap: A brief summary of the demo content, including any
commitments made during the session.

Solidifying Impressions: Reinforcing the key points and value propositions
showcased during the demo.

Addressing Concerns: Ensuring that potential clients lingering doubts or
questions are promptly addressed.

Guiding Towards a Decision: Nudging the prospect closer to making a
positive purchase or partnership decision.

Addressing Questions and Concerns
The true measure of a successful demonstration often lies in the dialogue
it sparks. The questions, concerns, and feedback raised during or after a
demo are, in essence, engagement indicators. This demonstrates that your
audience was invested enough to seek clarity, express reservations, or even
challenge what was presented. In this context, managing these questions
and concerns is just as crucial, if not more so, as the demo itself.

Understanding this dynamic is essential, as addressing questions and
concerns is not just a process; it is an art. It is about striking a balance
between acknowledging the prospect's perspective while still steering the
narrative towards product value. It is about fostering an environment
where open communication is encouraged and no question is deemed too
trivial or too challenging. (Hess 2020)

Compiling Queries for Clarity
During a demo, especially one that is comprehensive and engaging, it is
natural for the audience to ask numerous questions. Some of these may be
addressed on the spot, while others may require further research or more
in-depth responses. However, amidst the flow of the presentation and the
ensuing discussion, it is easy for some questions to get lost in the shuffle.

To counter this, it is prudent to have a systematic approach to capture
every query. This could be as straightforward as jotting down questions as
they arise or designating a team member to play the role of a scribe,
ensuring that every concern is noted. Once the demo concludes, these
questions can be compiled and categorised. Grouping them based on
themes or topics makes the response process more organised and provides
insights into areas where the audience’s interest or uncertainty is
concentrated. Maybe you even add the names of the person who asked the
question.

Once you have categorised the questions, we dedicate time to research and
craft comprehensive answers. Recall that the depth of your response often
indicates your commitment to transparency and client satisfaction. Avoid
generic replies. Instead, the aim for answers was tailored to the specific
concerns raised, reflecting empathy and expertise. You can add a small
recording, video, or other marketing material to support your answers.
(Care 2020)

In some cases, this research may also reveal gaps in the product or
presentation. Instead of shying away, they are used as opportunities for
improvement. By addressing these gaps head-on in your responses, you
position yourself as a partner who is both self-aware and committed to
continuous betterment.

Encouraging Continuous Dialogue
Always include clear pathways for prospects to reach out with further
questions in your follow-up communication. Whether it is a dedicated
email address, phone line, or even a feedback form, it ensures that these
channels are accessible and monitored. By doing so, you are sending a clear
message: "We are here, we are listening, and we are eager to assist."

Nevertheless, it is not just about providing these channels but also about
promoting their use. Encourage prospects to use these platforms for any
further queries or clarifications they might have. By fostering this open
dialogue, you address their immediate concerns and build a foundation for
a more long-term, trust-based relationship.


**Key Takeaway:**

Compile and Categorise: List all questions and concerns raised during the
demo.

Research and Respond: For queries not addressed during the demo,
provide comprehensive answers in the follow-up communications.

Open Channels: Ensure prospects know they can reach out with further
questions.

Sharing Supplementary Materials
The saying "seeing is believing" holds an undeniable truth. However, in a
complex ecosystem where products and services can span a vast spectrum
of features, capabilities, and potential applications, "seeing" is not always
confined to the demo itself. Often, product or solution richness is best
appreciated when the audience can delve deeper, explore at their own
pace, and reflect on the information shared. This is where supplementary
materials have come into play, serving as invaluable resources that bolster
the demo experience and provide avenues for further exploration. (Care
2020)

The Power of Documentation
Any seasoned professional knows that a product or solution, whether
intuitive or groundbreaking, often comes with layers of complexity. While
the demo aims to provide an overview and highlight key benefits,
supplementary documentation, such as product manuals, technical
specifications, and whitepapers, offers a more granular understanding.

Consider these documents as the backbone of your product. You delve into
the 'hows' and 'whys' behind each feature, providing insights into the
underlying technology, design principles, or even research findings that
power the product. Sharing such documentation post-demo allows the
audience to engage in these nuances, fostering a deeper appreciation of
the product’s capabilities.

Moreover, this kind of documentation often aids decision-makers who
might need to justify the purchase or adoption of the solution to other

stakeholders. Equipped with detailed specifications and whitepapers, you
can make a compelling case grounded in facts, figures, and technical merits.

The Persuasion of Case Studies
Narratives have the intrinsic power to resonate, and in the PreSales
universe, case studies are narratives that often hit closest to home. These
are the real-world stories of clients who have successfully integrated and
benefited from their products. You paint a picture not of possibilities but of
realised potential. (Hess 2020)

By sharing case studies, you are achieving two primary objectives. First, you
showcase the tangible benefits of your product in diverse settings, which
can reassure potential clients of its versatility and efficacy. Second, these
stories can act as blueprints, helping prospects envision how they might
deploy and gain value from your solution in their unique contexts. Please
keep in mind less is more. Do not send ten materials at one time or share a
content portal with 100 documents. Create a touchpoint every week or
send something insightful to keep oneself in mind.

Engaging Through Videos
In an era dominated by digital content, videos are among the most
engaging media. Whether it is a short explainer video, a deep-dive feature,
or even a testimonial, videos can encapsulate complex information in easily
digestible formats.

For prospects who might not have the inclination or time to sift through
detailed documentation, videos offer a quick yet comprehensive overview.
They can serve as excellent reference materials, especially for those who
want a refresher on specific product aspects discussed during the demo.
Moreover, the dynamic nature of videos – with visuals, animations, and
sounds – can make the content more memorable, aiding recall and
understanding.

Interactive Exploration with Click-Through Demos
One of the most powerful tools in the PreSales professional’s arsenal is the
'Click Through Demo'. Unlike passive materials, this is an interactive
platform in which prospects can explore the product first hand. It offers a

controlled environment where users can click around, test features, and
get a feel for the product without any risks or commitments.

Such demos empower prospects and give them a sense of agency. One can
probe areas of interest, experiment with functionalities, and grasp the
product user experience. For many, this hands-on exploration can be the
deciding factor, as one can personally attest to the products’ ease of use,
capabilities, and fit for their needs.


**Key Takeaway:**

Documentation: Product manuals, technical specifications,                and
whitepapers offer deeper insights into product features.

Case Studies: Sharing the success stories of existing clients can build
confidence.

Video: Short explainer videos or feature deep dives can serve as useful
reference materials.

Click Through Demo: Prospect to explore the solution in a secure
environment with canned guides.

Scheduling a Follow-Up Meeting
The more touchpoints you have, the less they speak with other vendors.
Use the time to build additional relationships and rapport. (Hess 2020)

In the grand tapestry of the PreSales process, scheduling a follow-up
meeting may seem like a mere administrative task, another checkbox to
tick in the workflow. Nevertheless, for discerning PreSales professionals, it
represents much more. At its heart, the follow-up meeting is a potent tool
that cements relationships, fosters trust, and nudges potential clients
closer to a positive purchase decision.

The Importance of Touch Points
Imagine you are in a bustling market. A vendor hands you a product sample,
explains its virtues, and then disappears into the crowd, leaving you alone
in the loudness of the competing voices and offers. Now, imagine another
scenario in which the vendor remains nearby after the initial introduction,

checking in on you, offering additional insights, answering your questions,
and guiding you through your purchasing journey. (Brian 2006)

In PreSales, these scenarios are not significantly different from a one-off
demo or a sustained engagement process. The latter, marked by multiple
touch points, such as follow-up meetings, ensure that the potential client
remains connected, engaged, and feels valued. Moreover, continued
interaction keeps your solution at the forefront of their mind. It subtly
suggests that while they might be considering other vendors, your
dedication and consistent presence make your offer uniquely compelling.

Delving Deeper and Nurturing Curiosity
The initial demo, by its very nature, offers a panoramic view of the product
or solution. It is designed to spark interest, showcase values, and provide
an overview of capabilities. However, every client’s needs and interests are
unique, and the initial demo might only scratch the surface of what they
want to explore. (Cohan 2023)

A follow-up meeting is a perfect opportunity to delve deeper into the
specific aspects of the product. Perhaps the client showed an interest in a
particular feature or had questions about integration capabilities. Follow-
up can be tailored to focus on these areas, offering a more in-depth and
personalised exploration that aligns closely with the client’s priorities.

Addressing the Elephant in the Room: Concerns and Reservations
No product or solution is without its challenges or limitations. Furthermore,
no client engagement is without its reservations, doubts, or concerns. This
might stem from aspects of the demo, competitor offerings, or internal
client deliberations. Ignoring these concerns is a missed opportunity.
Addressing them head-on can turn potential roadblocks into avenues of
trust-building. (Cohan 2023)

A follow-up meeting provides a more relaxed and open environment to
discuss these reservations. It signals to the client that you value their
feedback, are confident in your product, and are committed to ensuring
their success. By creating a space where concerns can be openly discussed,
you can alleviate doubts and demonstrate the depth of your product
knowledge and the strength of your support ecosystem. (Rosenberg 2015)

Paving the Path to Implementation
As the client’s journey progresses from curiosity to serious consideration,
the narrative shifts from 'what' and 'why' to 'how'. If they are leaning
towards a purchase decision, they will naturally have questions about
implementation, integration, training, and support. The follow-up meeting
can transition into a strategic discussion about the next steps. (Riefstahl
2011; Cohan 2023)

By proactively addressing the path to implementation, you show your
preparedness and give the client a clear vision of the journey ahead. This
clarity can significantly reduce the perceived risks associated with adopting
a new solution, thereby making the decision-making process smoother for
the client.


**Key Takeaway:**

Delve Deeper: Explore specific aspects of the product in more detail.

Address Concerns: Discuss any reservations that the potential client may
have.

Plan Implementation: This meeting discusses the next steps if the client is
nearing a purchase decision.

Collaborating with Sales
The life of a PreSales professional is similar to that of an elite orchestra
musician. While you might be skilled at playing your instrument (in this
case, delivering impactful demos), the real magic happens when you
synchronise with other musicians. In the vast orchestra of a business, your
closest partner is often the sales team. When PreSales and sales collaborate
harmoniously, the music – or, in our analogy, the business results – reach
new heights.

The Confluence of Two Worlds
The PreSales world is deeply rooted in product knowledge, technical
expertise, and client-centric presentations. Meanwhile, the sales realm is
characterised by relationship building, negotiation, and the pursuit of

closure. These domains, though distinct, are deeply interconnected. The
bridge between them? Effective collaboration.

The Power of Shared Insights
Every product demo and every interaction with a potential client is rich with
insights. As a PreSales professional, you gather invaluable feedback during
your presentations. This could range from the clients’ initial reactions and
their questions to their reservations and areas of keen interest.

Sharing these insights post-demo with the sales team is not just a
procedural step; it is a goldmine. Why? These insights ensure that the sales
team does not walk into follow-up conversations blindly. You will know
exactly which features intrigued the client, which concerns need
addressing, and which areas require further emphasis. In doing so, every
sales pitch, email, and call becomes a tailored response, resonating deeply
with the clients’ unique needs and interests.

Imagine the difference between a salesperson starting a conversation with,
"I hope you found our demo insightful," versus, "I understand you showed
keen interest in our AI-driven analytics feature during the demo." The latter
demonstrates thoroughness and builds immediate rapport with clients.

Crafting a Joint Strategy
Sales and PreSales bring two unique perspectives to the table. PreSales,
with its technical and product-centric viewpoints, understands the
intricacies of the solution and its potential fit for the client. Sales, with its
pulse on client relationships and market dynamics, have a keen sense of
clients’ decision-making trajectories. (Brian 2006)

When these two worlds collaborate to design strategies, especially for
hesitant or on-the-fence prospects, the result is a powerful blend of
technical persuasion and relational negotiation.

Consider a scenario in which a prospect hesitates because one is unsure of
the products’ integration capabilities. While the PreSales team can provide
technical reassurances, the sales team can emphasise testimonials from
similar clients, offer special training, or even provide unique pricing
structures or trial periods to mitigate the perceived risks.

Navigating the Decision Landscape with Stakeholder Mapping
In any organisation, purchase decisions, especially significant ones, are
rarely unilateral. There is often a web of decision-makers, influencers, and
stakeholders, each with their motivations, concerns, and aspirations.
Navigating this web is like playing a strategic board game, and stakeholder
mapping is like your playbook. (Raz 2017)

By collaborating with sales, you can identify the key decision-makers and
understand their unique perspectives. Perhaps the CEO is looking for
strategic alignment, the IT head is concerned about integration, and the
operations lead is keen on ease of use.

This approach allows follow-up messages to be more specific. Instead of a
standard product pitch, the team can talk to the CEO about how the
product fits their big plans, reassure the IT leader about easy integration,
and show the operations manager that it is user-friendly. Tailoring
messages such as this increases the likelihood of a positive response.

The Symphony of Success
The true strength of PreSales does not lie in isolated brilliance but in its
ability to amplify the collective efforts of the organisation, especially sales.
By sharing insights, strategizing together, and navigating the decision-
making landscape with precision, PreSales and Sales become more than
just two functions. You become partners in the symphony of success, where
every demo, every pitch, and every interaction hits the right note. (Hess
2020)


**Key Takeaway:**

Shared Insights: Post-demo, share feedback. This ensures that everyone is
aligned.

Joint Strategy: Collaborating to design strategies for nudging hesitant
prospects.

Stakeholder Mapping: Identify key decision-makers and their concerns,
tailoring follow-up communications accordingly.

Analysing Demo Feedback for Continuous Improvement
With rapidly changing technologies, dynamic market demands, and the
continuous influx of innovative competitors, remaining static is not an
option. Every demo you deliver offers a learning opportunity, and these
insights can fuel your journey towards perfection. The key lies in
meticulously analysing the feedback from these demos and leveraging it for
continuous improvement.

The Feedback Goldmine
Feedback is a goldmine of insights. Every question asked, every puzzled
expression noted, and every suggestion made by your potential client
carries with it the potential to elevate your demos to new heights. The key,
however, is not just collecting this feedback but also delving deep into it,
deciphering its underlying messages. (Rosenberg 2015)

Spotting the Patterns
In a world full of different opinions, it is easy to get lost in all the comments
and reactions. However, the key to understanding feedback is to identify
trends. For example, you might notice that there is one part of your product
that always seems to confuse people. Or maybe there is a point in your
demo where you see people start to lose interest. It could even be a certain
slide that always brings up a lot of questions. Spotting these trends helps
determine what parts of your product or presentation require more work.

For instance, if a certain product feature is consistently confusing for your
audience, it might indicate a need for a clearer explanation or a more
relatable use-case scenario. If there is a section in your demo where
engagement consistently drops, it may be too technical or not resonate
with your audience’s primary concerns. (Riefstahl 2011)

Refining with Precision
Once these patterns are identified, the next step is refinement. Based on
this feedback, modifications of the demo structure or content may be
required. For sections that are consistently confusing, consider breaking
down the information further, using analogies, or incorporating a short
story or anecdote that can make the concept more relatable.

If a particular slide generates numerous questions, it may be beneficial to
anticipate these queries and address them proactively during the demo.
Alternatively, redesigning the slide for clarity or incorporating more visual
aids could be a solution.

However, it is also essential to ensure that any modifications maintain the
flow and coherence of the demo. Every change should enhance the
audience’s understanding and engagement and not detract from it.

Reinforcing through Training
It is not just the demo content that can benefit from feedback analysis; the
presenter can also benefit. If certain questions or objections regularly
stump the demo presenter, this indicates that there might be gaps in
knowledge or understanding.

A refresher course or training session may be conducted in such scenarios.
Training should be tailored to address specific areas of concern. For
instance, if the presenter struggles to explain the technical background of
a feature, a session with the product development team can prove
invaluable. Role-playing exercises with a sales team can be beneficial if the
challenge lies in handling objections. (Clear 2018)

It is also worth noting that training is not only about addressing
weaknesses. This is equally valuable for reinforcing strengths. If sections of
the demo consistently garner praise or if aspects of the presentation style
resonate well, they should be identified and emphasised in training
sessions. This ensures that the presenter overcomes challenges and
capitalises on their strengths.


**Key Takeaway:**

Identify Patterns: Are certain features consistently confusing? Is there a
particular slide or section in which engagement drops?

Refine the Demo: Based on feedback, modify the Demo structure or
content.

Training: It may be time for a refresher course if certain questions regularly
stump the demo presenter.

Staying on the Prospects Radar
In PreSales, it is essential to maintain a strong presence in the clients’
minds. This is not just about being persistent; it is about staying relevant,
constantly demonstrating your value, and nurturing a long-term
relationship. Making a great first impression is one thing, but the real
challenge is making it stick in a market full of distractions and competitors.
(Brian 2006)

The Art of Regular Check-ins
After all the demos, talks, and negotiations, it might seem that it is time to
take a break and remain silent. However, be careful – silence in sales can
backfire. When you are out of sight, you are often out of mind. In big
companies, decisions take time with many steps and people involved.
Therefore, staying connected and visible is key even when things seem
slow.

However, this waiting period should not translate into a communication
blackout. Regular check-ins play pivotal roles in this process. These are not
intrusive daily calls but thoughtfully spaced-out interactions that serve dual
purposes: you show the prospect that you are genuinely interested in their
business, and you gently remind them of the value your product or solution
brings.

Such communications should never come off as impatient nudges for
decisions. Instead, they should be framed as a continuation of the
relationship you have begun building. It is about showing concern,
understanding their decision-making process, and reassuring them that you
are there to provide any further information or assistance they might need.

Keeping the Conversation Fresh with Updates
Changes are the only constant in the fast-paced world of technology and
innovation. It is quite possible that between your initial demo and the
prospective decision-making period, your product or service might see
enhancements, new feature roll-outs, or even updates based on market
feedback.

Sharing these updates is not just about showcasing the dynamism of your
product; it is also about demonstrating commitment. It shows the prospect
that you are continuously striving for betterment and, by extension,
ensuring that their investment – should they choose to make it – is future-
proof.

In addition, such updates can sometimes address reservations or concerns
that the potential client might have had during the demo. Perhaps they
were hoping for a specific feature that was not available during the initial
presentation but is now part of the offering. Sharing such updates can tip
the scale in your favour in many instances.

Engaging Beyond the Product
Engagement content, be it blog posts, webinars, industry news, or thought
leadership articles, is a subtle yet potent tool in your arsenal. When you
share such content, the message goes beyond just “buy our product.” It
becomes “Let us grow together.” (Carnegie 2010)

Sharing a blog post or webinar on industry best practices, for example,
demonstrates thought leadership. It shows the prospect that you are not
just a vendor but also a potential partner who understands the challenges
of their industry and is invested in finding solutions.

Furthermore, this content provides value. A well-researched article might
offer potential client insights that they have not considered. A webinar may
introduce them to industry leaders or introduce novel strategies. By
providing this value, you reinforce the idea that your relationship with them
is transformational rather than transactional. (Hess 2020)


**Key Takeaway:**

Regular Check-ins: Even if a decision is pending, periodic communications
keep your product in the potential client’s mind.

Sharing Updates: If new features or updates roll out post-demo, share
these with the prospect.

Engagement Content: Share blog posts, webinars, or industry news to
provide value and stay relevant.

Closing the Loop: When to Move On
Engaging a potential client requires significant investments in time, effort,
and resources. Moreover, knowing when to move on can be as crucial as
knowing when to go all in. However, how does one determine that it is time
to close the loop? How do we ensure that this closure is not an end but
perhaps a pause, leaving room for future possibilities? This section delves
into these crucial decisions.

Recognising the Signs
Like any relationship, the interactions between a PreSales professional and
a prospect have telltale signs. These signs provide signals regarding the
trajectory of engagement. One must become skilled in reading them.

One of the most apparent indicators is repeated delays from the
prospective end. While it is standard practice to provide ample time for
decisions – especially significant ones – there is a fine line between a
company taking its time and one that drags its feet. Repeated
postponements can signal a lack of interest or commitment, particularly
without concrete reasons. (Raz 2017)

Another sign is the nature of communication. A potential client genuinely
interested in your product or solution will have questions or clarifications
or will seek deeper dives into certain aspects. Their communication reflects
a desire to understand better and evaluate thoroughly. On the other hand,
generic responses, lack of engagement, or reduced responsiveness can be
indicative of diminished interest. (Hess 2020)

The key here is not to make hasty judgments but to consider these signs in
conjunction with the overall interaction narrative. A seasoned PreSales
professional develops intuition, the ability to differentiate between
genuine delays and disinterest, between a busy phase for the client and a
lack of enthusiasm.

Seeking Feedback on Hesitation
Before deciding to move on, it is imperative to understand the 'why' behind
a prospect’s hesitation or decision against a purchase. This feedback is gold.
(Keenan 2018)

Firstly, it provides clarity. Perhaps hesitation is not about the product at all.
Perhaps it is about internal changes in the prospects company, budgetary
constraints, or a shift in priorities. Understanding the context can help to
strategize the next steps in such scenarios more effectively.

Second, feedback offers learning opportunities. Perhaps there is a feature
the prospect hoped for which your product currently does not offer.
Perhaps it is about pricing or the support ecosystem. Understanding these
hesitations provides valuable insights that can guide product
enhancements, pricing strategies, and the way demos are conducted in the
future. (Clear 2018)

However, soliciting this feedback requires finesse. It is not an inquisition
but a request for understanding. The tone should convey genuine interest
from the perspective of prospects, ensuring that they do not feel cornered
but valued.

Leaving the Door Open
Just because the current narrative with a prospect does not culminate in a
sale does not mean that the story is over. Business landscapes change
needs, evolve, and today's 'no' might very well be tomorrow's 'yes'.
Therefore, ensuring that the loop is not tightly locked is essential when
closing the loop. (Hess 2020)

Ensure that the prospect knows that while respecting their current
decisions, the door remains open for future interactions. Maybe six months
down the line, the feature they wanted is now a part of your product.
Perhaps their internal landscape has changed, making the solution more
relevant.

Leaving the door open is also about maintaining goodwill. It is about
respecting the time and interactions shared and valuing the relationship
over the transaction. It is a gesture that says, “We are here, should you wish
to explore again.” (Care 2020)


**Key Takeaway:**

Recognising the Signs: If a prospect continually delays or seems
disinterested, it might be time to move on.

Feedback on Hesitation: Before ending the engagement, seek feedback.
Why did the prospect decide against the purchase?

Leave the Door Open: Ensure that the prospect knows that they can return
if they reconsider it in the future.


> **Story**

Some engagements are smooth and straightforward, while others are
fraught with challenges and unexpected turns. One particular engagement
taught us invaluable lessons about persistence, communication, and the
importance of proving our value to all stakeholders.

We engaged with a client for almost three months, during which time we
built a strong relationship with their operations team. They loved our
solution and consistently told us that we were by far the best option
available. Encouraged by their enthusiasm, we moved forward and
executed a detailed scoping workshop. This led to the development of a
comprehensive Statement of Work (SOW), and even the challenging IT
team praised our integration approach as the best they had seen.

However, despite all the positive feedback and endorsements from the
operations and IT teams, the decision-making power ultimately rested with
the purchasing department. Unfortunately, we made a critical mistake by
not engaging with the purchasing department early in the process to
demonstrate our value proposition more clearly. As a result, they decided
to go with a much cheaper vendor, driven by their mandate to minimize
costs.

The loss was a tough blow. We conducted a thorough lessons-learned
session to understand where we went wrong and to identify ways to
improve our approach. Importantly, we kept the lines of communication
open with the client, letting them know that we were available if they
needed further assistance.

Four months later, we received an unexpected call from the client. The
cheap vendor they had chosen turned out to be a poor fit, resulting in
numerous issues that disrupted their operations. Dissatisfied and
frustrated, the client decided to cancel the contract with the competitor
and reached out to us for help.

Seizing the opportunity, we quickly re-engaged, providing the support and
solutions they needed. This time, having learned from our previous
experience, we ensured that we demonstrated our value to all
stakeholders, including the purchasing department. The implementation
was successful, and we ultimately had a very happy client who appreciated
the quality and reliability of our solution.

This experience reinforced an important lesson: sometimes, patience and
persistence pay off. A lost deal is not truly lost if you maintain a positive
relationship and remain available to assist. Similarly, a won deal is not truly
won until you consistently deliver and prove your value over time.

In the end, this engagement underscored the importance of
comprehensive stakeholder engagement and the need to demonstrate
value across all decision-making levels. By staying persistent and open to
re-engagement, we turned a temporary setback into a lasting success.


### 10.7 Why Demos fail and how to avoid it

Demos are often seen as the zenith of the PreSales process, the moment at
which the solution comes alive in front of a prospective client. However, as
in any complex process, countless variables are at play, and sometimes,
despite the best efforts, demos do not resonate. This chapter aims to
explore the common reasons behind unsuccessful demos and provide a
roadmap for navigating around these pitfalls.

Understanding the Gravity of Demo Failures
An unsuccessful demo is not just a missed opportunity but can have wider
implications.

In PreSales, a product demo is a crucial moment. It is when a solution
shines, moving beyond just the features and specifications to demonstrate
how it can solve actual problems. A great demo can be a game changer that
converts potential customers into actual clients. On the other hand, a demo
that does not hit the right notes can have negative effects that go beyond
just one missed chance.

The Hidden Costs: Lost Time and Resources
Every product demo, irrespective of its outcome, represents an investment.
Days, sometimes weeks, of preparation go into tailoring a demo to a
prospect’s unique requirements. Technical experts, product managers, and
PreSales professionals burn the midnight oil, ensuring every feature is
showcased, every potential question anticipated, and every snag ironed
out. On D-Day, a team often travels, sometimes crossing cities or countries,
to present the solution.

From the prospect's side, key stakeholders and decision-makers carve out
time from their schedules, ensuring that they are present to evaluate the
solution. Meeting rooms are booked, equipment is sometimes arranged,
and an entire ecosystem is geared up in anticipation.

When a demo fails to resonate, this investment – in terms of time, effort,
and actual financial costs – goes down the drain. It is not just about the
hours put into that particular presentation; it is also about the opportunity
cost. The time spent on this demo could have been used to engage another
potential client or to further refine the product. (Hess 2020)

The Echoing Impacts: A Damaged Reputation
In business ecosystems, especially in niche industries, words travel quickly.
A successful demo might lead to a sale and, possibly, a few
recommendations. However, an unsuccessful one, especially if perceived
as unprofessional or ill-prepared, can have wider ripples.

In today’s digital world, where everyone is connected, a product demo is
more than just a presentation. If it is not done well, it can create a bad
image for the company. People talk, whether it is clients, competitors, or
on social media. A bad demo can make it seem like your company does not
pay attention to detail, is not fully committed, or is just not good enough.
Once this idea starts spreading, it can really be used in future deals.
Remember that in business, your reputation is everything. (Greene 2018)

Counting the Real Losses: Lost Revenue
In this case, an unsuccessful demo represents missed sales. However, the
financial implications run deeper when you scratch beneath the surface.

Every potential client represents not just a singular contract but also a
doorway to long-term engagements, repeat business, upselling
opportunities, and referrals. A demo that fails to convert essentially shuts
this door. While it is easy to quantify the immediate loss in terms of the
unsigned contract, the long-term revenue implications, although intangible
at times, are substantial.

The Intangible Blow: Loss of Motivation
Beyond tangible losses, an unsuccessful demo has an intangible yet
significant fallout — the impact on team morale. PreSales professionals are
passionate individuals. They believe in the product, its potential, and its
ability to make a difference. When a demo fails to resonate, it is not just a
professional setback; it is personal.

The lack of motivation can be harmful. A discouraged team may not have
the same energy, determination, or excitement in the next presentation.
The impact of one unsuccessful demo can affect future interactions,
creating a difficult cycle to break. (Greene 2018)

A Broader Perspective
Understanding the gravity of demo failures is not about cultivating a fear of
failure. It is about recognising the broader implications of our actions or
sometimes the lack thereof. It is about understanding that stakes are high
in the PreSales world. Every engagement, presentation, and interaction is
laced with potential – potential for success, growth, and forging lasting
business relationships. (Hess 2020)

However, it is equally essential to remember that failures, while daunting,
are also stepping stones to growth. They offer lessons, insights, and
sometimes the most valuable feedback. Recognising the gravity of a demo
failure is the first step. The next, and possibly more crucial, step is to
harness this understanding, learn from the missteps, and ensure that every
subsequent demo is a step closer to perfection.

In the chapters to come, we delve into strategies to turn these setbacks
into comebacks, ensuring that every demo, irrespective of its immediate
outcome, adds value to the journey of PreSales excellence.

Common Causes of Demo Failures
Lack of Preparation
Implications: Coming across as unprepared can erode trust and make even
a fantastic product seem unreliable.

Solution: Always undertake meticulous planning. Ensure that all equipment
is in working order and that the product being demonstrated is glitch-free.
(Riefstahl 2011)

Not Tailored to the Audience
Implications: A generic demo can make prospective clients feel
undervalued.

Solution: Customise the demo to address audience-specific pain points.

Overcomplicating Things
Implications: Drowning prospects in technical jargon can lead to
disengagement.

Solution: Keep it simple. Explain features that resonate with clients’ needs,
avoiding unnecessary complexity.

Ignoring the Business Perspective
Implications: While technical features are essential, not highlighting the
business impact can leave prospects unconvinced.

Solution: Always tie features back to the business benefits that you bring.

Not Addressing Questions or Concerns
Implications: Leaving unanswered questions can sow the seeds of doubt.

Solution: Create a conducive environment in which prospects feel
comfortable raising questions and ensuring that they are adequately
addressed.

Navigating Technical Glitches
Reality: Technical issues can arise, even with the best preparation. Wi-Fi
may drop, or the software may act up.

Perception: How one handles these glitches can shape the clients’
perception more than the glitch itself.

Solution: Always have backups. If it is a software demo, have offline
versions ready. If it is a web-based tool, have redundant internet
connections.

Transparency: If things go wrong, be open about it. A straightforward
approach can turn a potential negative into a demonstration of a
company’s transparency and problem-solving approach. (Cohan 2023)

The Trap of Overselling
Temptation: In a bid to impress, people are often tempted to oversell or
promise features that are not yet available.

Risks: This can lead to trust issues when clients come on board and realise
a reality.

Solution: Always be honest about what your product can and cannot do. It
is better to have a client who is on board with your product’s current
product capabilities than one who feels deceived.

Failing to Engage All Stakeholders
Scenario: Demos are often presented to a subset of decision-makers.

Challenge: If not all stakeholders feel involved or see product value, it can
lead to decision-making bottlenecks.

Solution: Ensure demos cater to all relevant stakeholders either by having
them in the same session or by holding multiple tailored sessions.

Ignoring Competitor Landscape
Reality: Prospects often demo multiple solutions before making decisions.

Need: Understanding how competitors present and what they offer can
give you an edge.

Solution: Regularly undertake competitor analyses. Be prepared to
highlight what sets your product apart.

Not Setting Clear Next Steps
Mistake: Ending the demo without a clear path forward.

Consequence: A lack of direction can lead to prospects feeling lost or
considering other options.

Solution: Always conclude with clear next steps, be it another deep-dive
session, proposal discussion, or contract negotiation.

Overlooking the Power of Storytelling
Oversight: Relying solely on technical demonstrations without weaving a
narrative.

Drawbacks: This can lead to a lack of emotional connections or resonance.

Solution: Craft a compelling story around the demo. Make the prospect the
protagonist, with your product being the tool that they need to overcome
challenges and add value.

Failing to Follow Up Promptly
Implication: Not following up quickly after a demo can diminish the impact
and momentum of the presentation.

Solution: Establish a follow-up plan before the demo. Schedule it within an
appropriate timeframe to keep the conversation going and address any
additional questions or needs that may arise.

Inadequate Demo Customization Tools
Implication: Relying on rigid demo scripts or tools that can't be easily
adapted on the fly may prevent addressing specific audience interests or
questions during the presentation.

Solution: Utilize flexible demo tools that allow you to tailor the
presentation as it happens, adapting to the flow of conversation and
audience feedback.

Ignoring the Importance of Aesthetics
Implication: An unattractive or outdated user interface or presentations in
the demo can leave a poor impression, even if the functionality is top-
notch.

Solution: Ensure the demo is visually appealing and reflects current design
trends to help bolster the perceived value of the product.

Poor Handling of Objections
Implication: Inadequately addressed objections can lead to unresolved
concerns that stall decision-making.

Solution: Train and prepare to handle common objections effectively,
showing how your product can overcome potential hurdles.

Lack of Interactivity
Implication: A one-way presentation without interactive elements can lead
to lower engagement from the audience.

Solution: Incorporate interactive elements such as Q&A, live polls, or
hands-on segments to increase engagement and gather real-time
feedback.

Insufficient Rehearsal / Dry Runs
Implication: Underestimating the need for practice can lead to poor
execution, even if the demo is well-planned.

Solution: Schedule rehearsal sessions to ensure smooth delivery and to
iron out any kinks in the presentation or in handling the product live.


### 10.8 No Demo is the best demo?

You might have heard the phrase "No demo is the best demo" many times
from PreSales experts on LinkedIn. The idea is that you should concentrate
solely on discovery. But is this really true? Let's consider this through an
analogy.

Imagine you're going to a car dealership to buy a new sports car, say a
Porsche. You've already done your research online and have fallen in love

with a particular model. Standing in front of the car, the dealer starts
narrating the story of the car's features, horsepower, and capabilities. You
are eager to sit in it, feel the interior, and maybe even take it for a test
drive. However, the dealer continues talking without offering you a chance
to experience the car. Eventually, you grow bored, stop listening, and might
even leave disappointed, feeling unheard.

Don't get me wrong — discovery is crucial. Understanding a client's needs
and context is the foundation of any successful sale. However, equally
important is getting the client excited about what's coming next. Providing
a taste of the product through an initial demo can transform the entire
conversation.

For instance, offering an automated demo, a Look & Feel demo at the start,
or sharing a click-through demo upfront can significantly impact
subsequent discussions. When clients see that you can deliver a great
solution, their confidence in you increases.

Now, let's consider an alternative scenario. You enter the dealership with
all your Porsche insights, and the dealer asks what you're interested in. You
mention you've researched online and are interested in the "911" model.
Instead of launching into more questions, the dealer invites you to sit in the
car and feel it for a moment. You envision yourself driving it, imagine what
you could do with it, and the dealer stands by, offering brief explanations
and asking questions like, "Have you considered a specific engine?" or
"What features are important to you?"

After a few moments, the dealer suggests, "Before we take a test drive, let's
quickly sit down and discuss what you'd like to have so I can adjust the offer
accordingly." How would you feel? This approach creates a perfect
customer experience by making the client feel heard and excited. Even if
you eventually decide to consider a different model, the initial engagement
pumps you up for more.

While discovery is a critical element of the sales process, integrating an
early demo can be equally powerful. It shows that you can deliver and helps
in building a strong foundation for a positive client relationship.


### 10.9 Key Takeaways for Demo

Before Demo
What are the Top 3-5 things the Client is interested in (Prepare and Show
Top-Down). (Cohan 2023)

Proper Discovery helps to understand the pain points. The first discovery
should not exceed 30 min. After 25 min offer a second discovery (prove if
they are serious). (Cohan 2022)

Understanding the audience: Before conducting a software demo, it is
essential to understand the audience and tailor the demo to their specific
needs, goals, and pain points. This will help to ensure that the
demonstration is relevant and engaging.

Plan and rehearse the demo: Planning and rehearsing the software demo
is crucial to ensure that the demonstration is smooth and professional. This
includes preparing visual aids such as screen sharing or slides and
rehearsing the flow of the demo.

Test the software: Before conducting the demonstration, ensure that the
software is running smoothly and troubleshoot any issues that may arise.
This will help avoid any technical difficulties during the demonstration and
ensure that the software is functioning as it should.

Gather resources: Having readily available resources such as client
testimonials, case studies, and information about the competition can help
to support the demonstration and answer any questions that may arise.

Prepare for questions: Be prepared to answer any technical or functional
questions regarding the software. It is important to have a clear
understanding of software capabilities and limitations to be able to
effectively demonstrate it and address any concerns or objections that may
arise.

Send a mail out with the meeting details, agenda, thoughts around
recording, usage of webcams, etc.

Set Clear Objectives: Determine what you aim to achieve from the demo –
is it purely informational, are you looking to close a sale, or get feedback?

Tech Check: Make sure your Internet connection is stable, your microphone
and webcam function correctly, and any external platforms/tools you are
using (for example, Zoom and Microsoft Teams) are updated to their latest
versions.

Backup Plans: Prepare for any technological failures. Have a backup plan,
such as a downloadable video of the demo or offline slides, only in the case
of connectivity issues.

Research Your Audience: Beyond their needs and pain points, it is
beneficial to know about a company’s size, industry, and history. This helps
in personalising the demo further.

Competitor analysis: Understanding how your software stands out from
the competition. This helps in positioning your product distinctively during
the demo.

Audience Persona: Beyond knowing the company, understand who in the
company you are speaking to. The demo for a CTO may differ from that of
a Marketing Manager. Tailor your content accordingly.

Agenda Clarity: While you have mentioned sending out an agenda, it is also
vital to stick to it during the demo unless the audience specifically guides
you otherwise.

While Demo
Tell-Show-Tell. Start with the most important. Keep the demo focused and
avoid unnecessary information. Stick to the key features and benefits of the
software and avoid going into too much detail or showing features that are
not relevant to the audience. (Roam 2016)

Use real-world examples and scenarios to demonstrate the software’s
capabilities. Show the audience how the software can be used in their
industry or business and the results they can expect to achieve.

Storytelling: Weave a story throughout your demo. This helps make your
presentation memorable and easier for the audience to relate to. (Riefstahl
2011)

Comparison Points: Briefly touch upon how your solution differs or is
superior to typical solutions they might have encountered or are using.

Visual Aids: Use diagrams, infographics, or other visual aids to break the
monotony and make certain complex points easier to grasp.

Demonstrate ROI: Whenever possible, use numbers to show potential ROI.
This can be powerful, especially if the audience needs to justify their
investment in other stakeholders.

Avoid Overloading Information: The point about avoiding unnecessary
information is essential. Recall that it is better to leave them curious and
wanting more than to overwhelm them.

Encourage audience participation and interaction during the demo: Ask
questions, solicit feedback and involve the audience in the demonstration
to keep them engaged and interested.

Stay Flexible: While you have a plan, be ready to pivot if the audience is
more interested in a different feature or if your needs become clearer
during the demo.

Interactive Elements: Use polls, quizzes, or other interactive tools to keep
your audience engaged.

Emphasis on Benefits Over Features: While good to showcase the
capabilities of the software, focusing on how it solves specific problems or
delivers value can be more compelling.

Maintaining Positivity: If confronted with objections or scepticism, remain
optimistic and emphasise the value offered by the software.

Be prepared to answer technical questions. Please have a good
understanding of the technical details of the software and be able to
answer any questions the audience may have about its capabilities and
functionality.

If you explain the use case of a persona you should jump into their shoes;
(always say I am Kevin, I do, I execute, Avoid saying you can or you should)

Use the ASD to make notes, organise timing and questions in the chat.

Pause and ask questions to get people’s attention

Do breaks at least every 45-60 min. Use the break timer.

Use the webcam and ask people to join you!

Give at least 10 min at the end for questions, a mutual action plan & next
steps.

After the Demo
Feedback Mechanisms: Offer an avenue for the audience to provide
feedback, either through a structured survey or an open forum.

Documentation: Provide a comprehensive documentation or user guide for
the audience to delve deeper into the software at their own time.

Engage with Non-Participants: Reach out to key stakeholders or decision-
makers who cannot attend the demo. Separate sessions or specific
information might be needed.

Record Key Insights: Document any crucial insights or feedback obtained
during the demonstration that can be vital for product development or
refining the sales pitch.

Referral Request: If the demo went well and the participants seemed
engaged or positive, consider tactfully asking if they know others who
might benefit from a similar demo.

Document Next Steps: Ensure both parties are clear about the next steps.
Send an email summarising agreed-upon actions to avoid confusion.

Nurture Relationship: Even if the demo does not lead to an immediate sale,
maintain the relationship. They may become clients in the future or provide
referrals.

Thank-You Note: A simple thank-you note expressing gratitude for their
time can leave a positive impression and set the stage for further
interaction.

It is good to call all participants and ask about their opinions, what was
good, and what is missing. This should be done by the Sales Responsible
person and one by one.

Share the recording and material via sharing tools, such as paperflite.

Answer the questions raised and not answered in the demo.

Organise a follow-up call or meeting: Schedule a follow-up call or meeting
with the audience to discuss the next steps and move forward with the
sales process if they want to purchase software.

10.10.Presenting in person vs. remote
The PreSales landscape has undergone a tectonic shift, particularly with the
increasing feasibility and acceptability of remote work and digital
communication tools. The choice between in-person and remote
presentations is no longer just a matter of convenience but a strategic
decision that can influence the outcome of a demo. This chapter delves into
the nuances, advantages, challenges, and best practices of both modes.


#### 10.10.1 In-Person Presentations: The Traditional Approach

The digital age may have ushered in a plethora of virtual tools and
platforms for presentations; however, the allure and effectiveness of in-
person presentations remain timeless. When one is standing in front of an
audience, sharing the same physical space, and breathing the same air,
there is an intangible connection that is difficult to replicate in a virtual
setting. It is the traditional approach and the tried and tested method
which brings with it a unique set of advantages and challenges.

In-person presentations are not just about showcasing a product or an idea.
They are about creating an experience, an event. It is about establishing
rapport, gauging real-time reactions, and adjusting on the fly. It is dynamic,
spontaneous, and involves human touch, which can sometimes make all
the difference. (Cohan 2023; Riefstahl 2011)

Reaping the Advantages
One of the most significant aspects of in-person presentations is immediate
feedback. There is no delay, no need for follow-up emails or polls. As a
presenter, you can read a room literally. Body language, facial expressions,
subtle nods, or the furrowed brow provide instant insights into the
audience’s reactions, allowing for immediate adjustments. If a particular

point is unclear or there is a notable interest in a specific feature, the
presenter can delve deeper or steer the conversation accordingly.

Then, there is the aspect of the engagement. Virtual presentations, while
convenient, often struggle with the challenge of a wandering attention
span. There are plenty of distractions, from a new email notification to a
ringing doorbell. By contrast, an in-person setting often secures better
attention and courtesy of physical presence. There is a certain decorum, an
unspoken contract of sorts, that binds the audience to the presentation.
(Cohan 2023)

Finally, in-person presentations often transform into networking
goldmines. The moment before the presentation starts, the coffee breaks,
and post-demo mingling – these are opportunities to foster deeper
relationships. During these informal interactions, barriers drop,
conversations become candid, and relationships truly blossom.

Navigating the Challenges
However, as with all things, the traditional approach comes with its set of
hurdles. Logistical concerns often top the list. Organising an in-person
presentation is not just about preparing content. It is a holistic endeavour
encompassing travel, accommodation, and venue arrangements and
ensuring that all equipment, from the projector to the product prototype,
is on-site and functioning becomes paramount.

The geographical constraints inherent to in-person presentations also pose
challenges. While a company would love to have every stakeholder in one
room, practical limitations often reduce the attendee list. Distance, travel
constraints, and time commitments can make it hard for everyone to
attend, limiting the presentation's reach.

Moreover, in-person presentations are undeniably time-intensive. The
preparation is just one part of the equation. Factor in travel, setup, and the
presentation itself, and it is clear that this approach requires a significant
time investment from both the presenting team and the attendees.

Mastering the Craft with Best Practices
Success with in-person presentations often boils down to meticulous
planning and a dash of interpersonal skills. Arriving early, not just on time,
is non-negotiable. It provides a buffer for any unforeseen issues, such as a
malfunctioning microphone or missing connector cable. An early arrival
also allows for a calm setup, ensuring that when the audience walks in, they
are met with an aura of readiness and confidence.

The presentation's formal segments are crucial, but the magic often lies in
the moments in between. Using breaks, Q&A sessions, and post-demo
time to informally engage attendees can cement the connection. This is an
opportunity to delve deeper into specific points, address concerns, or
simply get to know the client better.

Finally, the power of tangible takeaways cannot be underestimated. In the
digital age, there is something profoundly impactful about physical
material. Handouts, brochures, or even product samples serve as tangible
reminders of the presentation, ensuring that the message lingers long
after the event concludes.


#### 10.10.2 Remote Presentations: The Digital Frontier

In an era dominated by technology and connectivity, the rise of remote
presentations emerged as a revolutionary force. The convenience,
efficiency, and adaptability of virtual platforms provide opportunities to
engage audiences across geographical boundaries. While lacking the
physical proximity inherent in traditional presentations, remote
presentations have carved out their own set of unique advantages and
challenges.

Remote presentations go beyond merely conveying the information. They
offer a blend of technology and content, aiming to create an immersive
experience across distance. The challenge is to bridge the digital divide,
ensuring that participants feel connected, engaged, and valued throughout
the session.

Reaping the Advantages
The most apparent advantage of remote presentation is its global reach. A
presentation can be delivered to participants from different continents,
allowing for a diverse and wide-reaching engagement. This omnipresence
eliminates geographical constraints and maximises potential audiences.

Flexibility is another hallmark of the remote approach. Participants can join
from the comfort of their homes or offices, catering to varied schedules and
commitments. This flexibility also extends to replayability; sessions can be
recorded and revisited, offering attendees the luxury of engaging with the
content at their own pace.

Cost-effectiveness has emerged as a key benefit. With no need for travel,
accommodation, or venue arrangements, both presenters and attendees
could achieve significant savings.

Navigating the Challenges
Remote presentations come with their own challenges. Technical glitches,
from poor Internet connectivity to software incompatibilities, can hamper
the smooth flow of a session. A proactive approach to troubleshooting and
backup planning is essential.

Engaging a distracted audience can become more difficult online. Without
direct eye contact or physical presence, presenters must find innovative
ways to keep the attendees hooked.

There is also a challenge in establishing rapport. Without in-person
nuances, building connections requires more conscious effort and fine-
tuned digital communication skills. (Hansen 2021)

Mastering the Craft with Best Practices
Effective remote presentations necessitate an understanding of digital
media. Testing all technology tools ahead of time and ensuring optimal
audio and visual quality is imperative.

Using interactive elements, such as polls, Q&A sessions, and real-time
feedback tools, can increase engagement. When used strategically, these
digital tools can foster a sense of participation in the community.

Finally, follow-up is crucial. Just because the session ends does not mean
engagement should. Email summaries, digital handouts, and feedback
surveys can help sustain momentum and solidify messages.


#### 10.10.3 Key Takeaway In-Person & Remote Presentation
