#### 10.10.4 Hybrid Presentations: Best of Both Worlds

In today’s business presentations, a fresh and effective method is taking
centre stage: the hybrid demo. This approach smartly blends the best of
both worlds: the engaging nature of in-person demos and the wide
accessibility of remote presentations. Hybrid demos offer a lot of
advantages, but they come with their own set of challenges. To excel in
hybrid demos, it is crucial to recognise these challenges and develop
strategies specifically designed for this combined format.

The Allure of the Hybrid Model
Before delving into the details, let us take a moment to appreciate the
genius behind the hybrid demos. By allowing in-person and remote
attendance, organisations can provide a personal touch crucial for building
trust and rapport while ensuring that geographical constraints do not
inhibit participation. This ensures that no stakeholder, whether in the same
city or halfway across the globe, misses out on the opportunity to engage
with the presentation.

The Dual Challenge of Consistency
The primary challenge that presents itself in the realm of hybrid demos is
ensuring a consistent experience for both in-person and remote attendees.
The goal is to ensure that the remote audience does not feel like mere
spectators, but they are as much a part of the presentation as those
physically present.

The issue of consistency manifests itself in multiple ways. For instance, in-
person attendees benefit from the nuances of body language, the energy
of the room, and the opportunity for spontaneous interactions. In contrast,
remote attendees might only receive voice feed, missing out on the visuals
or the atmosphere. Similarly, asking questions, interacting with the
presenter, or engaging in discussions can be seamless for those in the room
but might require additional effort for those attending remotely.

Crafting a Unified Experience
Several solutions have been proven to be effective in addressing these
challenges. The first and perhaps most crucial aspect is the use of cameras
to stream in-person events. While this might seem like a given, the specifics

are essential. A static camera focused solely on the presenter may not
capture the entirety of the experience. Multiple strategically placed
cameras can offer varied angles, capturing the presenter, slides, and even
the reactions of the in-person audience. This visual feed ensures that
remote attendees obtain a holistic view, mirroring the in-person
experience as closely as possible.

Clear audio is another pillar of a successful hybrid demo. While in-room
acoustics might be perfect for the physical audience, remote attendees rely
entirely on transmitted audio feeds. Utilising high-quality microphones,
preferably lapel mics for presenters and ambient mics to capture audience
questions or comments, ensures clarity. It is also prudent to have a
dedicated audio feed for online attendees, ensuring that they receive
undistorted, clear sounds throughout.

However, while technology is a significant part of the solution, it is not the
only aspect. The human touch in the form of a moderator can be invaluable.
This individual’s role is to bridge the gap between physical and virtual
realms. You should ensure that questions from the remote audience are
fielded in real time, fostering a sense of inclusion. The moderator can also
relay the mood and reactions of the remote audience to the presenter, thus
ensuring a two-way flow of information.

The Future of Hybrid Demos
As businesses globalise and remote work becomes more prevalent, the
relevance and importance of hybrid demos are set to grow. Organisations
that recognise the challenges and proactively work on solutions will be
better positioned to leverage the benefits of this format.

How to present remote
If you want to learn how to present remotely, there is no better book than
“Look Me in the Eye: Using Video to Build Relationships with Clients,
Partners and Team” by Julie Hansen. I highly recommend reading this book
if you present remotely.

We have all experienced it. You start a video call, get ready, and try to bring
the same energy as if you were meeting face-to-face. But something just
does not click. The way we do business is evolving, as are the skills and tools

we need as PreSales professionals. Remote presentation has broken down
geographical barriers, which is great. However, it also presents a set of
challenges. To really succeed in this virtual world, it is crucial to understand
how remote interactions work and how to engage people effectively, even
through a screen. (Hansen 2021)

The Perception of Attention in Virtual Interactions
There is a distinct nuance in understanding how attention is perceived in a
virtual environment. Let us draw a picture of a typical scenario: Mark, an
experienced PreSales professional, is on a call with a potential client,
Jessica. Mark believes he is giving his undivided attention. He focuses on
the Jessicas image on his screen, trying to gain insights from her reactions
and body language. On the other hand, Jessica sees Mark's gaze as directed
slightly downwards, not meeting her eyes. She feels like she is being
observed rather than being engaged with. The connection starts to fray. By
the time you end the call, Jessica feels that Mark is not genuinely interested
in her needs, and Mark is bewildered by the lost opportunity.

What went wrong? A simple misinterpretation of attention. Mark intended
to show engagement by looking at Jessica's image. However, in the realm
of video calls, the camera serves as the "eyes". This slight shift in gaze
direction, which is often unnoticed by the presenter, can significantly
impact how the audience perceives attentiveness.

This seemingly small nuance highlights a broader challenge in remote
presentation: the dissonance between intent and perception. When a
person does not share a physical space with someone, subtle cues that
communicate understanding, empathy, and interest can be lost or
misinterpreted.

Bridging the Gap: Effective Engagement in Remote Presentations
How do we ensure that our messages are transmitted, understood, and
felt?

Eye Contact: Because the webcam acts as our virtual eye, it is crucial to
maintain "eye contact" by looking at it, especially when making pivotal
points. This simple action establishes a connection and assures the client
that they are being listened to. (Hansen 2021)

Active Listening: As in physical meetings, one must demonstrate that one
is actively processing what is being said. Nodding at appropriate times,
paraphrasing clients’ concerns, and asking relevant questions indicated
active participation. (Rosenberg 2015)

Verbal Affirmations: In the absence of many non-verbal cues, verbal
affirmations become more critical. Simple acknowledgements such as "I
understand”, "That is a valid point”, or "Please continue" reassure the client
of your engagement.

Body Language: Despite its limited frame, body language remains
significant. A straight posture exudes confidence, whereas leaning slightly
forward can show eagerness and interest. (Hansen 2021)

Visual Aids: Remote presentations provide a unique advantage: the ability
to seamlessly integrate digital tools. The use of slides, graphics, or short
videos can break monotony and emphasise crucial points.

Anticipating and Overcoming Technical Hurdles
While mastering the art of virtual engagement is vital, ensuring that the
medium itself does not become a barrier is equally essential. A glitchy
video, delayed audio, or frozen screen can quickly dismantle the rapport
that one is trying to build.

Always test your equipment and Internet connections before the
presentation. Familiarise yourself with the platform features to
troubleshoot common issues or effectively utilise tools such as screen
sharing. If possible, have a backup device or alternative communication
system ready. These safeguards might seem mundane, but one can
differentiate between a successful presentation and a missed opportunity.

Understanding the Virtual Dynamics
The evolution of the digital age has reshaped several traditional business
practices. One such transformation has been the steady migration from in-
person meetings and presentations to virtual ones. This shift, while offering
numerous advantages, has also brought with it an array of challenges that
PreSales professionals need to navigate. Understanding the underlying

dynamics of these virtual environments is paramount for ensuring effective
communication and creating lasting impressions.

The Ephemeral Nature of Virtual Connection
The core difference between in-person and virtual presentations lies in the
nature of human interaction. When we are physically present in a room, we
communicate not only with our words but with our entire being. Our body
language, the energy we project, and even spontaneous interactions that
can occur during breaks or side conversations all contribute to the overall
narrative. Many of these nuanced interactions are invisible in the virtual
world. We are primarily left with our voices and, if video is enabled, our
facial expressions and upper body gestures. (Hansen 2021)

While virtual platforms offer the gift of connecting individuals across
continents without the need to travel, they can sometimes lack the
immediacy and tangible energy that in-person interactions bring. The
richness of standing in front of an audience, sensing mood changes, and
adapting in real-time based on reactions is a intense force that is difficult
to replicate online. In the virtual realm, a presenter is often met with a wall
of silence, or at best, a grid of faces on a screen, each ensconced in their
own unique environment, reacting silently or in ways not immediately
perceivable.

Battling Time, Space, and Distractions
Another facet of remote presentation is the dissolution of the shared
physical space and time. In traditional settings, everyone is bound by the
same environmental conditions: room temperature, chair comfort, and
sound system quality. In the virtual sphere, each participant exists in a
bubble that is complete with its set of conditions and distractions.
Participants could be tuned from their home office, a bustling café, or even
while in transit. These varied environments can lead to disparities in
attention span and engagement levels.

Time zones further complicate this dynamic. What might be a morning
presentation for someone could be a late-night meeting for another
participant. This disparity can affect the audience’s energy levels and
concentration. A PreSales professional must be acutely aware of these

factors and attempt to schedule sessions at times most conducive to the
majority or segment presentations to cater to different zones.

The Double-Edged Sword of Technology
Technology, the very enabler of virtual presentations, can sometimes be its
Achilles heel. Relying on the internet, software platforms, microphones,
and cameras introduce several points of potential failure. Nothing is as
disconcerting as presenting critical information when a technical glitch
occurs. This disrupts the flow, breaks immersion, and can sometimes
undermine the presenter’s credibility.

However, not all challenges and pitfalls exist. Virtual presentations also
include a suite of tools and features that can enhance interaction and
engagement. Polls, chat boxes, breakout rooms, screen sharing, and
interactive whiteboards can offer unique ways to engage the audience,
gather feedback, and foster collaboration. Leveraging these tools
effectively can sometimes turn the tide, making virtual presentations
impactful, if not more so than their in-person counterparts.

Setting Up for Success
In the digital age, where remote work and virtual presentations are
increasingly becoming the norm, the stage for your performance has
moved from grand auditoriums and plush meeting rooms to the corner of
your home or a designated workspace. The shift might seem trivial;
however, in a virtual space, even the smallest details can make a profound
difference in how you are perceived and how effectively you communicate.
Setting up for success, therefore, is not just about content preparation; it
is about optimising every element of the virtual presentation space.

Crafting the Optimal Tech Infrastructure
Let us begin with the foundation of your technological infrastructure. This
is the backbone of your virtual presentation, the unsung hero who can
make or break a session. Imagine a scenario where you are about to unveil
a pivotal point in your presentation and suddenly freeze due to a spotty
Internet connection. Not only does it break the flow, but it also creates
room for distractions, interruptions, and doubts about professionalism.

Investing in stable and fast Internet connections is paramount. This ensures
that your presentations stream seamlessly, that your voice is clear and
uninterrupted, and that any videos or interactive elements you introduce
do not lag or stutter.

However, this is not just about the Internet. The visual and auditory
components are just as vital. A high-definition webcam ensures that the
audience sees crisply. It can capture the nuances of facial expressions and
gestures, aiding nonverbal communication. Similarly, a noise-cancelling
microphone ensures that your voice rises above any ambient noise,
focusing the audience on your message rather than on the distant sounds
of traffic, household activities, or other environmental distractions.

Creating a Professional Virtual Environment
Once the technology infrastructure is in place, the environment is the next
layer to consider. This is the stage at which you perform and needs to be
carefully crafted.

When presenting, you are not just sharing information; you are sharing part
of your world. The background that the audience sees, the lighting that
illuminates your face, and even your room acoustics all play a part in
creating ambience. (Hansen 2021)

Your chosen location should ideally be quiet and free from the hustle and
bustle of daily life. The last thing you want is to compete for attention with
the background noise. The visual backdrop reflects professionalism. A
cluttered or overly personal background can distract an audience. This can
lead to unintended judgments or, at the very least, curious diversions. A
neutral, tidy, and organised space suggests preparation, seriousness, and
professionalism.

Therefore, lighting deserves special attention. Although natural light is
ideal, offering warm and even illumination, it is not always feasible.
External lighting solutions can be used in the absence of natural light. The
primary objective is to have your face well-lit, avoiding stark shadows or
overly bright spots. Proper lighting ensures that you are visible and adds
depth and clarity to the video, making the session visually pleasant.
(Hansen 2021)

Mastering Your Tools: Platform Familiarity
Every artist needs to know the tools; your tool is the chosen presentation
platform for the virtual presenter. Mastering the functionalities of Zoom,
Microsoft Teams, WebEx, or any of the myriad other platforms available is
essential.

Being fluent with platform features is not just about avoiding embarrassing
mishaps; it is also about creating a fluid and interactive presentation
experience. Knowing how to transition between slides seamlessly,
incorporate multimedia, manage participants, mute or unmute with ease,
and utilise chat or poll features can enhance the engagement and
dynamism of a session. This ensures that technological capabilities are
harnessed to enhance, rather than hinder, presentation.


**Key Takeaway:**

Tech Infrastructure: Invest in a stable Internet connection, a high-quality
webcam, and a noise-cancelling microphone. These are the basics that
ensure communication clarity.

Environment: Choose a quiet location. The background should be tidy and
professional, reflecting the workspace rather than a casual setting. Proper
lighting is crucial; natural light is the best, but if not possible, it ensures that
the face is well-lit using external lights.

Platform Familiarity: Zoom, Microsoft Teams, WebEx, or any other
platform know the functionalities inside out. This includes screen sharing,
muting/unmuting participants, recording, using chats, and other
interactive features.

Preparing the Content
In the realm of PreSales, the content of your presentation is the heart and
soul of communication. It is the message you want to convey, the value
proposition you wish to highlight, and the insights you aim to share.
However, the dynamics of presentation in a virtual setting are distinct from
those of traditional face-to-face interactions. The screens separating
presenters from the audience introduce an element of detachment, making
it more challenging to maintain attention and engagement. Therefore,

while the message remains paramount, the delivery method requires some
rethinking.

Embracing Brevity in a Digital World
In today’s fast-paced world, where people are bombarded with
information, the principle "less is more" is crucial, especially for virtual
presentations. When you are not face-to-face, your audience’s attention
can drift away more easily. Therefore, every slide, sentence, and word in
your presentation must be counted.

Making your presentation concise is not just about cutting content for the
sake of it. Focus on the core points that the audience needs to hear. Get rid
of any unnecessary details or "fluff", and present your key messages clearly
and directly.

Being brief does not mean that you must rush through your presentation.
Instead, it is about being efficient in your words and time. Show respect to
your audience by not taking up more time than necessary while ensuring
that they understand the essential points you are trying to convey.

The Power of Visual Storytelling
Humans are inherently visual creatures. Our brains process images faster
than text, and we are more likely to remember and be influenced by
something we have seen rather than just heard. This visual element
becomes even more pivotal in a virtual presentation. With a plethora of
distractions just a click away from your audience, your slides cannot afford
to be dull.

Incorporating high-quality visuals, infographics, and animations does not
involve adding decorative flair but enhances comprehension and retention.
A well-designed infographic can convey complex data in an easily digestible
manner. Engaging animation can illustrate a process or product
functionality more effectively than mere words. A compelling visual
narrative can make a presentation more memorable and simplify complex
topics, making them more accessible.

However, the quality trumps the quantity. Overloading slides with visuals
can be counterproductive, as having none at all. The goal is balance. Each

visual element should serve a purpose, either by elucidating a point,
emphasising a message, or guiding narrative flow.

Engaging Through Interactivity
One of the challenges of virtual presentations is their potential for a passive
audience. In the absence of a physical presence, the energy exchange
between the presenter and the audience can be muted. In other words,
interactive elements can come into play.

Incorporating polls, quizzes, or other interactive segments transforms the
audience from passive to active participants. First, interactive elements
break the monotony of one-way monologues.They inject energy and
prompt engagement, which can often lead to insightful audience
contributions. Second, they serve as a checkpoint, ensuring that your
audience follows along, understands the content, and remains engaged.

Imagine introducing a complex product feature and then conducting a
quick poll to gauge the audience’s initial reactions. Alternatively, a quiz is
initiated to reinforce the key points after explaining the benefits of a
product. These interactions enrich the presentation and provide real-time
feedback, allowing you to adjust your approach if needed.


**Key Takeaway:**

Brevity is Key: Attention spans can be shorter in a virtual setting. Keep your
presentation concise, focused, and engaged.

Visual Appeal: Use high-quality visuals, infographics, and animations. A
compelling visual narrative can make a significant contribution.

Interactive Elements: Incorporate polls, quizzes, or interactive segments
that require audience participation. This breaks the monotony and keeps
the audience engaged.

Engaging the Audience
The art of engagement in the virtual realm is an intricate dance. Picture
this: Your audience is nestled comfortably in their homes or offices, free
from societal norms that often mandate undivided attention in a physical
setting. A world of distractions surrounds them - an unread email, a buzzing

smartphone, or perhaps a pet vying for their attention. In this environment,
engaging your audience is not just a matter of holding their attention; it is
about making them active participants in your narrative.

The Power of a Captivating Beginning
Every story, be it in literature, film, or sales pitch, hinges on its opening. A
strong start not only sets the tone but also captures the imagination. This
becomes even more crucial in the virtual world, where one often competes
with multiple distractions from the get-go. A captivating introduction acts
as a beacon, drawing the audience and making them eager for what is to
come. (Hansen 2021)

Consider starting with a surprising fact — one that challenges common
perceptions or introduces a new perspective. Surprises can push audiences’
attention, making them curious and investing.

Did you know that according to a study, 60% of SaaS
customers decide whether they will consider a product
within the first five minutes of a demo?

Alternatively, a well-chosen anecdote, preferably a personal one or a client
success story, can humanise your presentation, making it relatable. This
reminds your audience that behind the screen is a person with experiences,
challenges, and insights.

Let me tell you about one of our clients, a leading
healthcare provider. They were overwhelmed with
managing patient data across multiple systems,
leading to errors and delays in patient care. After
adopting our solution, they unified their data
management processes, reducing errors by 40% and
cutting patient wait times by 30%. This not only
improved patient satisfaction but also enhanced their

operational efficiency, demonstrating the real impact
of our technology.

Finally, rhetorical questions are powerful tools for this purpose. By posing
a question and letting it hang in the digital space, you invite your audience
to ponder, reflect, and mentally engage with the subject. (Riefstahl 2011)

The Art of Continuous Engagement
While a strong start is vital, maintaining momentum is the real challenge.
No matter how compelling the content is, a monologue runs the risk of
turning into background noise. This is when frequent check-ins come into
play. Periodically pausing to ask questions or seek opinions serves multiple
purposes.

First, it breaks rhythm, ensuring that the presentation does not become
monotonous. Second, it acts as a pulse check, providing real-time feedback
on how well the content is received and where the audience’s interest
levels lie. It can be as simple as asking, "Does that make sense?" or "How
does this align with your experiences?" These check-ins made the
participants feel seen and valued. You transform the presentation from a
one-sided dissemination of information into a collaborative conversation.
(Cohan 2023)

Personalisation: The Key to Deeper Connection
In a physical room, there is a certain energy, an unspoken connection that
forms when someone addresses you directly. In the virtual space, this
energy needs to be consciously cultivated. One of the most effective ways
to accomplish this is to use names. This might seem like a small gesture, but
it can have a profound impact.

Addressing participants by their names, especially when responding to
questions or comments, adds a layer of personalisation. It signals to the
individual that they are not just anonymous entities in a sea of digital faces
but a valued participant in the dialogue. This sense of being acknowledged
can foster greater investment in the discussion and prompt more active
participation from others who see the interaction as genuinely two-way.

However, this is not only about using names. It is about being genuinely
attentive to the comments, questions, and insights shared by the audience.
It is about responding with thoughtfulness, building on their contributions,
and weaving them into the fabric of your presentation.


**Key Takeaway:**

Starting Strong: Begin with a captivating introduction, be it a surprising
fact, relevant anecdote, or rhetorical question.

Frequent Check-ins: Periodically ask questions or seek opinions. This
ensures participants are attentive and feel involved.

Use Names: Personalise the experience by addressing participants with
their names, especially when responding to questions or comments.

Managing Q&A
In the realm of virtual presentations, the Question and Answer (Q&A)
stands out as a unique opportunity. It is a moment when the presentations’
one-way flow shifts, and the audience, having absorbed your content, gets
a chance to seek clarity, challenge assumptions, or simply delve deeper. For
PreSales professionals, a well-handled Q&A can be a crucible where trust is
forged, credibility is established, and deals are catalysed. Nevertheless, it is
also an arena fraught with potential pitfalls, especially in the digital realm,
where visual cues might be absent and technological glitches can arise.

Crafting Pockets of Interaction: The Power of Dedicated Time
Imagine a river flowing smoothly and then suddenly interrupted by a
barrage of stones, causing turbulence. This is akin to what happens when a
presentation is continually halted for questions. While it is essential to
address doubts, doing so without a structure can disrupt the narrative and
make the content feel disjointed.

Hence, the idea is to craft pockets of interaction. You achieve two goals by
allocating specific times for Q&A, such as after covering distinct sections or
crucial points. First, you allow your narrative to flow uninterrupted,
ensuring that the audience can absorb content in a structured manner.
Second, you set clear expectations by signalling to your audience that there

will be dedicated moments for interaction. You can mentally catalogue
your questions and read them for upcoming Q&A slots.

Smooth Sailing with Moderation
Anyone who has been part of a large virtual meeting knows that it can
sometimes resemble the Wild West. Multiple people might simultaneously
attempt to voice their questions, leading to overlaps and confusion. In
other cases, some participants, especially those with less tech-savvy, might
struggle to find the appropriate buttons or features to voice their doubts.

Here, the role of a moderator or colleague is invaluable. In larger groups,
having someone specifically tasked with managing questions ensures that
the process is smooth and orderly. This person can keep track of hands
raised, voices seeking attention, or questions popping up in the chat. You
can then relay these queries to the presenter in a structured manner,
ensuring that no question is overlooked and that the presenter can focus
on delivering clear and concise answers. One person who would fit the role
perfectly is the Sales Representative.

The Grace in Not Knowing
There is an inherent expectation that the person presenting, especially in a
PreSales scenario, will have all the answers. Moreover, while expertise is
crucial, it is also human to occasionally be stumped up, especially when
faced with complex, multifaceted questions.

In such moments, the key is not to bluff or provide half-baked answers but
to acknowledge the complexity of the query. A simple acknowledgement,
such as "That is a fantastic question, and I want to ensure I give you the
most accurate answer. Let me research this further and revert post-
presentation," can go a long way. It signals honesty, commitment to
accuracy, and respect for the questioners’ intelligence.


**Key Takeaway:**

Dedicated Time: Allocate specific times for Q&A, perhaps after covering
distinct sections, such that the flow is not interrupted.

Moderation: In larger groups, have a colleague or a dedicated moderator
to manage questions, either voicing them out or typing them in chat.

Acknowledgement: If a question is complex and requires further research,
acknowledge it and promise to revert post-presentation.

Cultivating a Virtual Presence
As the world of PreSales adapts and evolves in the digital age, so too does
the arena of presentations. Once confined to physical conference rooms,
pitches, demos, and discussions have found a new home in the vast
expanse of the virtual world. With this shift comes a unique challenge and
opportunity: How does one cultivate a commanding and impactful virtual
presence? The crux of the matter lies in three primary areas – the silent
language of the body, the resonance of voice, and the elimination of
distractions.

The Silent Conversations of Body Language

While the canvas of a virtual presentation may limit the scope of body
language, its importance remains undiminished. Often, before a single
word is spoken, our posture, gaze, and movements communicate their
narratives. Consider how a mere frame, typically a headshot or upper-body
view, becomes your stage when engaged in a virtual dialogue or
presentation. Every gesture, every tilt of the head, and every shift in
posture are magnified. (Hansen 2021)

One of the most effective ways to engage the audience is through the
illusion of eye contact. In face-to-face interactions, eyes are a powerful tool
for connection, conveying authenticity, interest, and confidence. In a
virtual setting, this translates into looking at the camera. It may feel
counterintuitive, especially when you are inclined to look at the screen to
gauge reactions or view the shared content. However, consistently looking
into the camera creates the impression of direct eye contact with the
audience, fostering a sense of intimacy and understanding. (Hansen 2021)

In addition, your posture can set the tone for your presentation. Sitting up
straight, shoulders relaxed but not slouched, communicates
professionalism and attentiveness. It silently tells your audience, "I'm
present, focused, and value this interaction." Even better, if you can stand
instead of sitting. This maximises energy and provides more power.
(Hansen 2021)

Hand gestures, although potentially limited by the frame, can still be
judiciously employed to reinforce points or convey enthusiasm. However,


*Figure 25: Eye Position for Webcam*


the key lies in its moderation and relevance. Overly animated gestures can
distract, whereas a lack of movement can become rigid or disinterested.

The Melody of Voice Modulation

Our voice, with its myriad tones, pitches, and cadences, is akin to musical
instruments. In the virtual world, where visual cues are often limited, voice
is pivotal in maintaining engagement and driving home points.

The danger of monotonous delivery is that it can swiftly lead to audience
disengagement. By varying your tone, you retain attention, underscore key
points, raise questions, and convey emotions. The rise and fall of your voice
can guide the listener through the narrative of your presentation, signalling
when to perk up and take notes or when a reflective or lighter moment is
at hand. (Greene 2018)

Crafting a Distraction-Free Zone

Even the most compelling presentations can be undermined by
distractions. The digital realm is rife with potential interruptions, such as a
sudden notification, an unexpected call, or background noise. As
professionals, our task is to minimise these disturbances, ensuring that our
message remains at the front and centre.

First, quickly auditing your devices to turn off non-essential notifications
can work wonders. This reduces disturbances and prevents any unwelcome
pings or pop-ups from distracting the audience during screen-sharing
segments.

Furthermore, the space in which you operate communicates a narrative of
its own. A clutter-free, organised workspace not only aids in mental clarity
but also projects professionalism. It subtly communicates that you have
made an effort that this presentation matters, and so does the audience.

The last is the human element – family, roommates, or pets. A simple
heads-up about your presentation can usually ensure a quiet environment.
This proactive approach can circumvent potential disturbances such as a
knock on the door or background chatter.


**Key Takeaway:**

Body Language: Even in a virtual setting, nonverbal signals are important.
Sit up straight, maintain "eye contact" by looking into the camera and use
hand gestures judiciously. If you can stand, stand.

Voice Modulation: Use varied tones to emphasise points. A monotonous
voice can quickly disengage the audience.

Avoid Distractions: Turn off notifications on your devices, keep your
workspace clutter-free, and inform cohabitants about your presentation to
prevent disturbances.

10.11.The Home Office Setup
A good home office setup is crucial for sales and presales professionals who
now work and present primarily remotely.

Why is it important?
Here are several reasons why investing in a quality home office setup is
important:

Professionalism and Trust
First impressions matter, especially in remote settings. A well-organized
and professional-looking home office conveys credibility and reliability to
prospects and clients.

A clutter-free background and proper lighting can enhance the overall
quality of video presentations, making them more engaging and effective.

Productivity and Efficiency
A comfortable and ergonomic workspace helps maintain focus and
reduces physical strain, leading to higher productivity and longer periods of
effective work.

High-quality equipment, such as a good webcam, microphone, and reliable
internet connection, ensures smooth and uninterrupted communication,
preventing technical issues that could derail presentations.

Enhanced Communication
Clear audio and video are essential for effective communication. A good
microphone and camera ensure that your voice and expressions are
conveyed accurately, making it easier to build rapport and convey your
message.

Tools like dual monitors can facilitate better multitasking during calls,
allowing you to manage presentations, notes, and client interactions more
seamlessly.

Improved Demonstrations and Demos
Delivering compelling product demonstrations is a key part of the role of
presales professionals. A good home office setup can include tools like a
drawing tablet, high-resolution monitors, and specialized software to
create more interactive and engaging demos.

Being able to switch between different types of content smoothly (e.g.,
slides, live demos, videos) enhances the overall impact of your
presentations.

Key Components
Setting up a home office is crucial for optimizing productivity, comfort, and
work-life balance, especially in a remote working environment.

Here are key components and considerations for an effective home office
setup.

Location and Space
Choose a Dedicated Space: To minimize distractions, select a quiet, low-
traffic area. Ideally, this should be a separate room, but a well-defined
corner can also work.

Adequate Lighting: Ensure the space is well-lit. Natural light is ideal,
supplemented by task lighting to reduce eye strain.

Ergonomic Furniture: Invest in a comfortable chair and a desk at the correct
height. An ergonomic chair supports good posture, which can prevent back
and neck pain.

Technology and Equipment
Reliable Internet Connection: High-speed internet is essential for video
conferencing, large file transfers, and other work-related activities.

Computer and Accessories: A reliable computer (desktop or laptop) with
necessary accessories like a keyboard, mouse, and monitor. Consider dual
monitors for increased productivity.

Video Conferencing Tools: A high-quality webcam and microphone for
clear communication during virtual meetings.

Backup Solutions: External hard drives or cloud storage solutions to
regularly back up important data.

Organization and Storage
Desk Organization: Keep your desk clutter-free. Use organizers, trays, and
holders for stationary and documents.

Filing System: Set up a filing system (physical and digital) to keep important
documents organized and easily accessible.

Shelving and Cabinets: Use shelves and cabinets to store books, files, and
office supplies.

Comfort and Personalization
Temperature Control: Ensure the room is well-ventilated and has a
comfortable temperature.

Personal Touches: Decorate the space with items that inspire you, such as
artwork, plants, or family photos. This makes the space more inviting and
can boost morale.

Health and Wellness
Break Areas: Designate a separate area for breaks to separate work from
relaxation mentally.

Exercise Equipment: To incorporate movement into your day, consider
small exercise equipment like a yoga mat or resistance bands.

Hydration and Snacks: Keep water and healthy snacks within reach to
maintain energy levels throughout the day.

Productivity Tools
Task Management Software: Use tools like Trello, Asana, or Microsoft To-
Do to keep track of tasks and deadlines.

Communication Platforms: Stay connected with your team using platforms
like Slack, Microsoft Teams, or Zoom.

Focus Tools: Utilize apps and techniques that help maintain focus, such as
Pomodoro timers or noise-cancelling headphones.

Security and Privacy
Data Security: Use strong passwords, encryption, and secure networks to
protect sensitive information.

Privacy Measures: Ensure your work area is private, especially if handling
confidential information. Use screen protectors if necessary.

Lighting Setup for Video Recording
For recording videos, the right lighting setup is crucial to achieving a
professional look. Here is a simple guide on how to set up your lighting from
a lumen and light perspective:

Key Light: This is the main light source and should be the brightest. Use a
softbox or LED panel with adjustable brightness. Aim for 1,000 to 2,000
lumens for indoor recordings. Position it at a 45-degree angle from your
face to create a flattering light with soft shadows.

Fill Light: This light reduces shadows created by the key light. It should be
less bright, around 500 to 1,000 lumens. Place it on the opposite side of the
key light at a similar angle but at a lower intensity.

Back Light (or Hair Light): This light separates you from the background,
adding depth. A small LED light with 500 to 1,000 lumens works well.
Position it behind you, aimed at your head and shoulders.

Background Light: To illuminate the background, use another light source
with 500 to 1,000 lumens. This prevents the background from looking too
dark or flat.

Adjusting for Natural Light: If you have natural light, use it to your
advantage. Position yourself facing the window to use the daylight as your
key light. Supplement with artificial lights as needed.

Diffusers: Use diffusers or softboxes on all your lights to soften the light
and reduce harsh shadows.

Colour Temperature: Ensure all lights are at the same colour temperature,
ideally around 5,500K, for a natural daylight look.

Professional Video Background
A professional background enhances the credibility of your video
recordings. Here are some tips on setting up an ideal background:

Avoid Virtual Backgrounds: While virtual backgrounds can be tempting,
they can reduce trust as people might think you are hiding something. They
also often create visual artefacts that can be distracting.

Neutral and Clean Background: Use a clean, uncluttered background.
Neutral colours like white, grey, or soft pastels work best and do not
distract from the speaker.

Personal Touches: Add a few personal items, such as books, plants, or
artwork, to make the space feel warm and personal, but avoid clutter.

Consistent Branding: If applicable, include subtle branding elements like a
company logo or colours that match your brand.

Depth and Dimension: To avoid a flat look, ensure there is some depth
between you and the background. You can achieve this by positioning your
desk a few feet away from the wall.

Avoid Reflective Surfaces: Reflective surfaces can cause unwanted glare
and distractions. Ensure the background materials are matte or non-
reflective.

Tidy and Organized: Keep your background tidy. An organized space
conveys professionalism and attention to detail.

By focusing on these aspects, you can create a home office that is not only
functional but also a pleasant place to work, enhancing your productivity
and overall job satisfaction.

Step-by-Step Guide to Setting Up Your Home Office
Setting up a home office involves careful planning to create a space that
enhances productivity and comfort. Follow these steps to set up an efficient
and professional home office.

1. Choose the Location

and away from high-traffic areas to minimize distractions.

### 1.2 Natural Light: Preferably select a space with natural light to

reduce eye strain and improve mood.

### 1.3 Space Requirements: Ensure the space is large enough to

accommodate your desk, chair, and other necessary equipment.

### 2.1 Desk: Choose a desk that fits your space and meets your work

glare.

Setup Schema with different lights
The following are some schemas for how to set up your background in a
professional way.

Schema with one Light
Chair to Camera

Distance: 3 to 6 feet (0.9 to 1.8 meters)

Reason: This distance allows for a clear head-and-shoulders shot, creating
a professional and focused frame.

Chair to Key Light

Distance: 3 to 5 feet (0.9 to 1.5 meters)

Reason: The key light should be positioned at about a 45-degree angle to
one side of your face to create depth and dimension.

Chair to Background (Without Greenscreen)

Distance: 5 to 10 feet (1.5 to 3 meters)

Reason: This helps create a nice separation between you and the
background, reducing distractions and focusing attention on you.


*Figure 26: Setup with one light*


Schema with two lights
Chair to Fill Light

Distance: 3 to 5 feet (0.9 to 1.5 meters)

Reason: The fill light should be placed on the opposite side of the key light,
also at a 45-degree angle, to soften shadows and provide a balanced look.


*Figure 27: Setup with two light*


Schema with three lights
Chair to Hair Light

Distance: 3 to 4 feet (0.9 to 1.2 meters) above and slightly behind you

Reason: This light separates you from the background and adds depth to
your image.


*Figure 28: Setup with three light*


Setup with full lights
Purpose of Background Light

Separation: It helps create a clear separation between you and the
background, preventing you from blending into the backdrop.

Depth: Adds depth to the scene, making it look more three-dimensional
and less flat.

Mood Setting: Can be used to set the mood or tone of the video by adding
colours or highlighting certain elements of the background.

Placement of Background Light

Distance: Position the background light 3 to 7 feet (0.9 to 2.1 meters) from
the background. The exact distance depends on the size of the room and
the effect you want to achieve.

Angle: Place the light at a low angle, pointing upwards or downwards to
create interesting shadows and highlights on the background.

Height: Depending on the effect, the light can be placed on the floor
pointing up or mounted high pointing down. Experiment with different
heights to see what works best for your setup.

Types of Background Lighting

Spotlights: These can highlight specific areas or objects in the background,
adding focus and interest.

Wash Lights: These provide even illumination across the background, ideal
for a clean and professional look.

Color Gels: Adding colored gels to your lights can change the color of your
background, helping to set a specific mood or theme.

Tips for Optimal Use

Avoid Overlighting: Too much light can cause the background to
overshadow the subject. Balance is key.

Consistency: Ensure the background lighting is consistent with the overall
lighting scheme of the video. Sudden changes in light can be distracting.

Shadows and Highlights: Use shadows and highlights creatively to add
texture and interest to the background.

Background Content: Be mindful of what’s in the background. Avoid clutter
and ensure it complements the subject without being distracting.

Common Mistakes to Avoid

Direct Light on Subject: Ensure the background light doesn’t spill onto the
subject, as this can cause unwanted shadows and glare.

Inconsistent Color Temperature: Match the color temperature of the
background light with your key and fill lights for a cohesive look.

Ignoring Background: A poorly lit or cluttered background can detract from
the overall professionalism of the video. Always take time to set up your
background properly.


*Figure 29: Setup with full light*


Setup with Greenscreen
Chair to Greenscreen

Distance: 5 to 7 feet (1.5 to 2.1 meters)

Reason: This distance helps prevent shadows and ensures even lighting,
making it easier to remove the background during editing.


*Figure 30: Setup with greenscreen*


10.12.The Top 20 tips for presenting remote
In today’s digitally driven world, remote presentations have become a
linchpin in PreSales. As the digital frontier expands, mastering the art of
online presentations is no longer a luxury but a necessity. Here, we delve
into the top 20 insights that can make your remote presentation stand out.
(Hansen 2021)

Creating an Authentic Connection: The beauty of technology is that it can
bridge vast geographical divides. However, ensuring that this technological
interface does not dilute human connections is a challenge. When you are
presenting remotely, your camera is the metaphorical window to your
audience’s soul. It represents their eyes. Anything outside its view is non-
existent in the realm of the presentation. Therefore, maintaining eye
contact with the camera is paramount for establishing trust and fostering a
genuine connection.

Mastering the Visual Canvas: To make your presence feel, position yourself
centrally within the video frame, ensuring equal space on both sides. This
balanced positioning provides a sense of stability and authority. Gestures

also play a pivotal role in communication. Open-palm gestures are
universally perceived as signs of honesty and transparency. However, on
the camera, movements should be slower than usual. Quick gestures can
appear blurry and can be misinterpreted. Dressing also plays an influential
role. It is essential to dress professionally, ensuring there is a stark contrast
between your attire, hair, and background, bolstering visibility and focus.
Avoid wearing black, as it can impact depth perception, making you seem
flat or two-dimensional. Stripes and checks are a no-go; you can cause a
moiré effect, seeming to shimmer or move on the video.

Delivering Content Seamlessly: A significant aspect of remote presentation
is content delivery. While it might be tempting to rely on a script, it is vital
to personalise the discourse. Speak as though you are addressing an
individual rather than a collective, crafting a personal and concentrated
connection. Although you should not read exactly, having your notes or key
pointers near the camera can serve as quick references. Memorise
essential segments, such as value propositions, metrics, and benefits. This
ensures that your delivery is smooth, and you come across well.

Technological Mastery and Collaboration: Before diving into the core of
your presentation, do a tech rehearsal. This will familiarise you with the
tools you use and pre-emptively address any glitches. Collaborate
effectively with your Sales Partner or technology assistant. Clearly define
roles, establish a discreet communication channel, and ensure that the
responsibilities are well understood. Joining the meeting slightly ahead of
schedule gives you ample time to set up your content and verify that
everything is in working order. During the presentation, keep an eye on the
chat, admit participants, and ensure that any technological issues are
promptly addressed.

Post-Presentation Analysis and Growth: After the presentation, take some
time to assess how it went. Record the session or take diligent notes.
Discuss with your team offline, focusing on observations and constructive
feedback. Reviewing the recording will help identify areas of excellence and
those needing improvement. However, instead of fixing everything at once,
pick one specific area to refine. As you consistently work in these areas over
time, you will find a marked enhancement in your overall presentation
style.

Further Tips for Excellence: Your presentation environment is as crucial as
your content. Use a solid-coloured backdrop to minimise distractions. Light
from behind can throw shadows on the face, ensuring that the primary light
source is in front. Having a reliable Internet connection and updated
software for your webcam platform is also beneficial. Using an external
microphone positioned appropriately can drastically improve the sound
quality. Keep in mind the ideal distance from the camera, which is about 1-

### 2.5 feet (40 -90 cm), is perfect for relationship building. Finally, always

approach your presentation as a dialogue, not a monologue. Use your script
as a guiding star, not a crutch. The more you can improvise and adapt, the
more genuine and impactful your presentation will be.

Remote presentations offer myriad opportunities for PreSales
professionals. By incorporating these tips and continually refining your
skills, you can ensure that your presentations are not just good but
exemplary.

Further Tips for Excellence:

•   On video, the camera is the eyes of your client! If the camera does not
see it, it does not exist.

•   Maintain eye contact with the camera to establish trust and connection
with your audience.

•   Use open-palm gestures to appear trustworthy and non-threatening.

•   Position yourself in the centre of the frame with equal space on both
sides.

•   Slow down movements, as fast movements can be difficult to read on
camera.

•   Use a solid-coloured background to avoid distractions.

•   Dress professionally and consistently to establish credibility.

•   Ensure there is enough contrast between your clothing, hair, and
background.

•   Speak to an individual, not a group, to create a more intimate
connection.

•   Avoid black clothing as it can negatively impact depth perception on
video.

•   Avoid stripes and checks as you may appear to move on camera.

•   Position your script or notes close to the camera for easy reference.

•   Clearly define responsibilities and establish a back channel with your
producer.

•   Speak to one person rather than a group to create a more intimate and
focused connection.

•   Do a tech rehearsal to eliminate errors and lags.

•   Do not read from a script or slides. Put it in your own words and
rehearse well.

•   Memorise key lines such as value propositions, benefits, and metrics to
avoid mistakes.

•   Join the meeting early to upload content and test your platform and
tools.

•   Allow participants into the meeting, monitor chat, and manage
technical support.

•   Record or take notes during the meeting.

•   Do a debrief offline to discuss observations and provide constructive
feedback.

•   Make a list of specific improvements for the next session.

•   Review your video for what you did well and areas for improvement.

•   Pick one specific area to work on for improvement.

•   Practice and rehearse your script, and memorise key lines.

•   Use notes and scripts as a guide, not a crutch, so you can improvise.

•   Have a good internet connection and test your equipment before the
meeting.

•   Use a microphone positioned four finger-widths away from your mouth
for optimal sound.

•   Eliminate or limit any light coming from behind your background.

•   Update your webcam platform or drivers for the best results.

•   Be mindful of your personal distance from the camera, as the ideal
distance for relationship building is 1-2.5 feet (40 -90 cm)

10.13.The Top 20 for presenting in person
In today’s age, while digital platforms dominate, the classic art of in-person
presentations remains a pivotal skill. The ability to connect, persuade, and
deliver your message in a room full of people can be both exhilarating and
daunting. Let us unravel the top 20 techniques that can elevate in-person
presentation. (Brian 2006; Cohan 2023; Keenan 2018; Raz 2017; Riefstahl
2011; Roam 2016; Care 2020)

Creating Genuine Rapport: Unlike a remote setup, where the screen
separates you from your audience, in-person allows for a palpable energy
exchange. Make the most of this by frequently making eye contact and
scanning the room to ensure that you connect with everyone. Your
audience can feel your energy and authenticity. A genuine smile can go a
long way in establishing warmth and openness.

Listen Actively: Beyond visual cues, pay attention to the audience's
reactions to your content. Are they nodding in agreement, or do their faces
show confusion? This will allow you to adjust your explanations or dive
deeper into certain points.

Mastering Body Language: Standing with a straight posture exudes
confidence. Use hand gestures to emphasise key points, but avoid
fidgeting, which can be distracting. Movement is powerful – use the stage
or presentation area purposefully, walking to change topics or engage a
new part of the audience.

Crafting Content with Precision: In remote as in person presentations, your
content must be perfect. Use visuals that complement and enhance your

message, not overshadow it. Avoid overcrowding slides and let them
breathe. The simpler, the better.

Engaging Audience Participation: Pose questions, encourage feedback, or
use interactive tools, such as live polls. This allows the audience to be active
and involved.

Breakout Discussions: For longer sessions, consider having breakout
discussions where smaller groups can discuss specific points or questions.
This can give you additional insights into the group's dynamics and
concerns.

Sending a Preparation Email: Prior to your session, send out a detailed
preparation email to all participants. Include the session agenda, any
ground rules, and what is needed from them. For instance, ensure that you
request access to the room at least 30 minutes before the presentation to
set up. Outline any technical requirements, such as a projector or access to
specific software, and confirm that these will be available. This proactive
approach helps set clear expectations, minimizes last-minute issues, and
ensures that everything runs smoothly.

Avoid Phone Distractions: Put your phone away during your presentation.
Do not hold it in your hands or leave it on the table. This simple action
conveys that your audience is your top priority and nothing is more
important than their time and attention. Keeping your phone out of sight
minimizes distractions and demonstrates your commitment to a focused
and professional interaction.

Timed Breaks: Plan short breaks strategically within your presentation to
give your audience time to digest the content. This can be after a complex
section or before a significant topic change.

Networking Breaks: Include longer breaks if the setting is appropriate, such
as seminars or workshops, to encourage networking. These breaks can be
informal, allowing you and your audience to discuss topics in a more
relaxed setting.

Managing Distractions: While presenting, disturbances may arise – a
latecomer, or ringing phone. Gracefully navigate these by acknowledging
them without getting flustered.

Harnessing Vocal Dynamics: Your voice tone, pitch, and volume can add
layers to your presentation. Use pauses for emphasis. Modulate your voice
to keep the audience attentive.

Personalising the Discourse: Tailor your presentation to the specific
audience. Addressing your unique needs or concerns can make your
message resonate more powerfully.

Practising Relentlessly: The key to seamless delivery is practising. Know
your content thoroughly, which can aid in recovering from any slip-ups
during the presentation.

Receiving Feedback Gracefully: Be open to feedback. This not only displays
humility but also provides insights for improvement.

Maintaining Flexibility: Be prepared to adapt on-the-fly, whether it is
skipping certain parts due to time constraints or deep-diving into topics
that garner particular interest.

Further Tips for Excellence:

•   Ensure your attire is professional aligning with the occasion and
audience.

•   Use storytelling to make your presentation memorable.

•   Use props or aids judiciously to drive home certain points.

•   Ensure that the visual aids (such as a projector or screen) are set up
correctly and are easily visible to all.

•   Bring backup equipment – an extra laptop, clicker, or even hard copies
of your presentation.

•   Engage with the audience to gauge their mood and expectations before
the presentation begins.

•   Address questions confidently. If you do not know an answer, admit it
and offer to find it.

•   Stay hydrated; a glass of water nearby can be a lifesaver.

•   Use humour wisely; it can break the ice but should not be forced.

•   At the end of your presentation, provide a concise summary and
encourage further interactions.


## 11. Demo Automation

In the ever-evolving world of technology sales, demos have become a
cornerstone for engaging potential clients. They are not just a tool for
showcasing product features but also a platform for telling a story, a
narrative that convinces the potential client of the solutions’ ability to
resolve their unique challenges. With the growth of the digital realm and
the increasing demand for immediacy, the automation of these demos has
become a crucial strategy. Let us delve into the dynamics of demo
automation and understand its facets.


### 11.1 Why is it important

Imagine this: You are in a meeting, and the salesperson has already
promised a demo, or the client, just minutes into the conversation, is eager
to skip the slides and dive straight into the demo. For us, in PreSales,
showing a demo on the fly is part of the job. However, what if the demo
system is shaky, you are new to the role, or you are worried about
presenting the wrong features? The pressure can be overwhelming.

Here is a solution that works wonders: automate your demos. By using
demo automation tools, you can share a guided tour of your product even
before your first discovery meeting and sometimes even before the sales
team steps in. This approach does wonders in easing the pressure. The
client receives an early peak at the system and its interface, grasping the
basics of how it works. This means that when you meet, there is no rush to
jump into a live demo. You have earned your credit to ask in-depth
questions and conduct a thorough discovery. Let us explore how we can
make the most of the demo automation to simplify our lives and enhance
client understanding.

The latest tools you will find on www.PreSales -handbook.com


### 11.2 Client Facing Click-Through Demos

The traditional approach to delivering demos usually involves live
presentations, which require the Sales and PreSales Personnel and the
prospective client to align schedules. These presentations are often long

and may not touch upon stakeholders’ specific concerns. This is where
client-facing click-through demos come into play.

A client-facing click-through demo is essentially an interactive experience
in which potential clients navigate a simulated version of a software or
product at their own pace. It is akin to the test drive of a new car, where
instead of just hearing about the features, you get to experience them. By
providing a hands-on environment, these demos empower clients to
engage directly with the features that interest them the most, allowing
them to visualise how the solution might fit into their existing workflows.

These self-driven demos are remarkably effective. Potential clients can
access them anytime and anywhere without waiting for a scheduled demo.
This immediacy and accessibility mean that prospects are more likely to be
engaged, even during the initial exploratory phases. Moreover, as these are
interactive, you offer a deeper engagement level, allowing potential clients
to understand the product at a granular level, which a conventional demo
might not achieve.


### 11.3 Personalised Videos

While generic demos provide a broad overview of capabilities, personalised
demos elevate the user experience by catering specifically to viewers’
unique interests and challenges. Imagine a demo that starts by addressing
the pain points of a specific industry or even a particular company, fine-
tuning its focus to offer solutions that align perfectly with these unique
needs. This level of personalisation sets the stage for much deeper
engagement.

In the realm of demo automation, personalised demos are the pièce de
resistance, combining the power of technology with a deep understanding
of potential clients’ needs. These are not typical one-size-fits-all
demonstrations; they are curated experiences crafted using insights and
data specific to the viewer. These demos resonate profoundly by zoning on
particular challenges and opportunities relevant to the viewer, ensuring
that the content genuinely connects.

The strength of personalised demos lies in their dual impact. First, they
foster feelings of connection and value. When a potential client perceives

that a demo is curated specifically for them, it transcends the boundaries
of a standard sales approach, evolving into personalised consultation.

Second, personalised demos are key to grabbing attention in today’s world,
where everyone is overloaded with information. By tailoring the demo to
address the specific needs and concerns of your audience, you show that
you are not just presenting a generic solution but one that is relevant and
valuable to them. This approach not only keeps their attention but also
demonstrates your commitment and understanding of their unique
challenges.

Integrating personalised demos into the sales journey could revolutionise
the efficacy of the engagement process. When a live interaction occurs
between the sales representative and a potential client, there is already a
mutual understanding and rapport. This foundation paves the way for more
insightful and targeted discussions, thus amplifying the chances of a fruitful
partnership.


### 11.4 Benefits of Demo Automation

As we navigate this chapter, we delve deep into the multifaceted benefits
of demo automation, painting a comprehensive picture of its
transformative potential for PreSales professionals.

The Digital Shift and Demo Automation
To understand the advantages of demo automation, we first need to
contextualise it within the larger digital shift that the world is undergoing.
Digital transformation, as we know it, has brought forth an array of tools
that simplify, enhance, and redefine traditional processes. Product
demonstrations are no exception.

Historically, product demos were in-person, often lengthy, and required
extensive setup. There was little room for flexibility, and it hinged heavily
on the demonstrator’s skills. Enter demo automation. Suddenly, the
process became streamlined, personalised, and far-reaching. However,
what exactly makes automated demos a treasure in the modern PreSales
Toolkit?

Enabling a 24/7 Sales Cycle
One of the standout benefits of demo automation is its ability to cater to
potential clients around the clock. The world no longer operates strictly
within nine-to-five boundaries. Globalisation means that businesses deal
with clients across time zones, cultures, and varying schedules. With
automated demos, a salesperson does not need to be actively involved in
every stage. A potential client can view a demo during a suitable time such
as midnight or noon. This flexibility enhances the user experience and
broadens the window of opportunity for businesses.

Personalisation at its Best
Each potential client or business has a unique set of requirements. The one-
size-fits-all model of product demonstrations is no longer viable in a world
that increasingly values personalisation. Automated demos can be tailored
to resonate with the viewers’ specific needs, pain points, and aspirations.
Such targeted demonstrations have a higher potential to strike a chord,
ensuring that the message is not only visible but also felt.

Scalability and Consistency
As businesses expand, their ability to scale their operations becomes
paramount. Automated demos offer scalability as never before. Whether it
is ten prospects or ten thousand, automated demos can reach them
without additional resource allocation. This scalability goes hand-in-hand
with consistency. Every viewer receives the same high-quality
demonstration, ensuring that the brand message remains unaltered and
undiluted.

Data-Driven Insights for Enhanced Strategy
Modern automated demos are equipped with analytics tools. These are not
just numbers but also insights, narratives, and feedback loops. PreSales
professionals can see how a prospect engaged with the demo, which
sections they focused on, and where they might have dropped off. Such
granular insights are invaluable for refining future pitches, understanding
market needs, and tailoring products to resonate better.

Cost-effective and resource-efficient
Traditionally, demos require not only human resources but also logistics,
especially if they are in-person. Automated demos eliminate several of
these costs. You reduce the need for physical presence, logistical setups,
and repeated human intervention. In the long run, this results in substantial
cost savings while ensuring that resources are optimally utilised.

Building a Foundation for Deeper Engagement
Automated demos give prospects a head start. Before a salesperson dives
into deeper talk, the prospect already knows quite a bit about the product
from the demo. This means that later conversations can skip the basics and
focus more on specific solutions and advanced topics.

Enhancing the Role of PreSales Professionals
Contrary to some beliefs, automation does not diminish the role of PreSales
professionals; it elevates it. With automated routine demo processes, these
professionals can focus on strategic tasks, build relationships, and delve
deeper into understanding clients’ needs. It is a shift from the operational
to the strategic and from the mundane to the impactful.

Incorporating Feedback Seamlessly
In the age of agile methodologies, feedback is gold. Automated demos can
easily be tweaked, adjusted, and refined based on client feedback. If a
certain feature does not resonate or a new functionality needs spotlighting,
these changes can be swiftly made in the demo, ensuring that it remains
up-to-date and relevant.


### 11.5 Content Sharing

Efficient and effective content sharing is paramount for PreSales success.
Ensuring that your team and clients have seamless access to the latest
materials can greatly enhance the sales process. Paperflite, combined with
tools like GoConsensus and Walnut, can centralize and streamline your
content sharing, creating a single point of truth that simplifies the entire
experience for both sales teams and clients. Sales teams often need help
managing and sharing content, leading to inefficiencies and potential loss
of sales opportunities. By centralizing content, you can enhance

accessibility by ensuring that all team members and clients have easy
access to the most up-to-date materials. This also improves efficiency,
reducing the time spent searching for or duplicating content, and maintains
consistency, ensuring that all shared content is accurate and consistent
across all channels.

Tools like Paperflite serve as a powerful hub where all your marketing and
sales materials can be stored and organized. Store all your videos,
testimonials, click-through demos, automated demos, whitepapers, and
case studies in one place, ensuring that everyone in your team can access
the same up-to-date materials. With Paperflite, sharing content with clients
is as simple as sending a single link, which can lead to a customized content
hub tailored to the client’s needs, ensuring they have everything they need
at their fingertips.


## 12. The Return on Investment (ROI) and Value Calculation

Modern clients demand a comprehensive understanding of the value they
derive from investments. Demonstrating a clear Return on Investment
(ROI) and the tangible and intangible values associated with a solution can
be the linchpin for turning a prospect into a client. This chapter delves into
the importance, methodologies, and nuances of ROI and value calculations
in the Sales process. (Care 2020)

The Significance of ROI in PreSales
Simply claiming its advantages is insufficient when introducing a product or
service. Think of ROI as concrete evidence to support your assertion.
Demonstrating the potential ROI fortifies your claims, suggesting, "If you
invest X amount, you can anticipate returns of Y, based on these specific
data and scenarios." This evidence-based approach instils trust in the pitch.

ROI (Return on Investment) plays a crucial role beyond just its basic
significance. Imagine a client evaluating different options. The main
question they have is: Which option gives the most value to the
investment? In this scenario, the ROI became a vital yardstick. This helps
clients determine the solution that offers the greatest value for their
spending. If your proposal shows a high ROI, it is likely to be seen as a better
choice, assuming that other factors are similar.

In large companies, making decisions is a team effort, not just one person's
job. This team is composed of different people, each with their own specific
interests and goals. However, one thing they all understand and care about
is ROI. It does not matter if it is the finance person looking at the costs, the
tech expert interested in how well something works, or the marketing
leader looking for features to promote; they all see the value in getting a
good return on the money spent. By focusing on the ROI, you can give
everyone a common goal to support and agree on. (Raz 2017)

The ROI metric is more than just a number. It:

Build Credibility: Showcasing a potential ROI based on tangible data and
relevant scenarios lends credibility to your pitch.

Offers a Comparative Tool: Clients often evaluate multiple solutions. A
clear ROI provides a tangible metric for comparison.

Aligns Stakeholders: In B2B sales, multiple stakeholders are involved. ROI
offers a universally understood metric that aligns diverse stakeholder
interests.

Breaking Down ROI
What is ROI?

ROI stands for "Return on Investment." This is a way to measure the gain
or loss made from an investment compared to its cost.

How Do We Calculate It?

Here is the formula: ROI = [(Net Profit - Cost of Investment) / Cost of
Investment] x 100%

Let us break that down:

Net Profit: This is the money you earn from your investment after all
expenses have been taken out. Think of it as your "take-home" profit.

Cost of Investment: This is how much you originally spent or invested.

Now, you subtract your Cost of Investment from your Net Profit. Then,
divide what you get by the Cost of Investment. Multiply the result by 100,
and you get your ROI as a percentage!

Why Is It Tricky?

The formula seems easy, right? Nevertheless, the hard part is getting
accurate numbers for 'Net Profit' and 'Cost of Investment'. Estimating these
can be complex because of hidden costs or unexpected gains. Therefore,
always ensure that you have the correct data before doing math.

Determining Costs (Investment)
It is important to look at the different types when considering the costs
associated with an investment. The first is Direct Cost. These are the
obvious, immediate expenses and usually the first ones to come to mind.

They include things like purchasing software or hardware or paying for
specific services.

Next, there are Implementation Costs. Once you decide on a solution, you
need to set it up. This setup can come with its own set of expenses, such as
training employees to use it, customising it to fit one’s needs, or integrating
it with existing systems.

As time goes on, there are Maintenance Costs to consider. These are
continuous costs to ensure that the solution runs smoothly and effectively.

Finally, we have Opportunity Costs. This one is different. It is not about
money spent but rather about the benefits or opportunities you might miss
out on by choosing one solution over another or by changing your current
way of doing things.

Determining costs in the ROI calculation for a Software as a Service (SaaS)
business has its advantages, which make the process relatively
straightforward compared to traditional business models. Here is why:

Predictable Subscription Pricing: Most SaaS businesses operate using a
subscription model. This means that clients pay a set fee regularly
(monthly, quarterly, or annually) for access to software. This makes the
revenue stream for SaaS businesses predictable, simplifying the cost
calculation for users, as you know, the expenditure over a specified
period.

Reduced Infrastructure and Hardware Costs: Traditional software often
requires significant investment in infrastructure and hardware. With SaaS,
these costs are minimal because the software is cloud-based. This clear
delineation of infrastructure responsibility makes it easier for clients to
calculate direct costs.

Centralised Upgrades and Maintenance: SaaS solutions are maintained
and upgraded centrally by the provider. This means that clients do not need
to worry about the additional costs for upgrades or patches. This clarity
simplifies the ROI calculation because maintenance costs are generally
bundled into the subscription price.

Transparent Pricing Models: Many SaaS companies use tiered pricing
models based on features, users, or usage limits. This transparency allows
businesses to easily predict costs based on their needs, thus facilitating a
more straightforward ROI calculation.

No Hidden Implementation Costs: The implementation of SaaS solutions is
typically quicker and less resource-intensive than on-premises software
installations. There are no additional hardware installations or complex
configurations. While there may be some initial training or integration
costs, they are often lower and more predictable than traditional software.

Scalability: SaaS solutions are designed to scale. If a business grows and
needs more resources or additional features, its subscription can often be
upgraded. This flexibility means that costs can be predicted and adjusted
more fluidly, making the ROI calculations more adaptable.

It is much more difficult to calculate Net Profit.

Calculating Net Profit from Investment
First, there are Direct Benefits. These are the clear, measurable profits that
you can see immediately. For instance, if after using a solution, your sales
go up or you spend less on operations, these are your direct benefits.

Next, we consider the Operational Efficiency. This is all about making the
process smoother. By streamlining how you work, you can save time and
money. This is similar to oiling the gears of a machine to make it run faster
and better.

Thus, there is Long-Term Strategic Value. Think of this as a big-picture
benefit. It is not just about immediate profits but also about positioning
oneself better in the market for the future. This can mean earning the
loyalty of clients, making smarter decisions based on data, or even
outperforming competitors.

Lastly, we have Intangible Benefits. These might be somewhat harder to
measure, but they are just as important. It is the positive change in how
people see your brand, the happiness and satisfaction of your employees,
or the superior experience your clients experience when interacting with
you.

Calculating the costs & benefits
Variability in Outcomes: Costs are generally fixed or predictable, such as
the price of a software subscription or a new hire's salary. Benefits, on the
other hand, can vary widely. For instance, predicting an exact increase in
sales from a new marketing campaign can be difficult.

Long-Term vs. Short-Term: Some benefits, especially strategic ones, are
realised over the long term. For instance, an investment in brand
awareness might not yield immediate sales but could solidify a company’s
position in the market over time. Same if you go with a cheap vendor can
cost you more in the long run.

Intangible Benefits: While costs are typically tangible and quantifiable,
benefits, such as improved brand perception or enhanced client loyalty, can
often be intangible. Measuring these intangibles in concrete terms is a
challenging task.

External Factors: External factors can influence benefits beyond a
company’s control. For example, a new product might be innovative, but
its success could be affected by economic downturns, regulatory changes,
or shifts in consumer behaviour.

Dependency on Implementation: The benefits derived from an initiative
often depend on how well it is executed. Two companies might adopt the
same technology, but if one fails to adequately train its staff, the benefits
realised could differ significantly.

Subjectivity: What one stakeholder views as a benefit, another might see
as neutral or even negative. For instance, new software might speed up
processes (a clear benefit) but might be perceived as reducing the quality
of human touch in client service.

Measurement Complexity: It often requires sophisticated tools and
methodologies to measure benefits accurately. For instance, calculating
the ROI from an enhanced brand reputation might require surveys, focus
groups, and data analytics.

Delayed Realisation: Benefits, especially from significant investments or
strategic shifts, might take time to materialise. For example, a company

might not see the advantages of a new training program until employees
have had time to apply their new skills.

Crafting a Value Proposition
The ROI (Return on Investment) is a straightforward numerical calculation
that offers insight into the tangible returns an investment might yield.
However, when dealing with complex business solutions, especially in
PreSales, the value of an offering is not solely based on its potential
monetary returns. This is where a well-crafted value proposition comes in.

The value proposition contextualises and deepens the meaning of the ROI.
It explains why the ROI is what it is and how the solution can bring about
the projected returns. By breaking down the benefits (both tangible and
intangible), addressing client-specific concerns, and showcasing
differentiators, the value proposition provides a more holistic view of the
solutions worth.

Consider the difference between these two pitches:

"Our solution offers a 20% ROI over three years."

"Our solution offers a 20% ROI over three years by addressing your specific
pain points, streamlining operations, offering post-sale support, and
integrating seamlessly with your existing systems."

The first is a simple ROI statement, whereas the second is an ROI backed by
a value proposition. The latter provides context for the ROI and paints a
vivid picture of how and why the solution is beneficial.

This involves:
